Good Breeding

Or Sex and Status in the 23rd Century

An Adaptation of Pride and Prejudice Set on a Colony on Titan in 2270



By Jane Austen and Kelly Inglis



On the arid and sandy moon of a distant planet, a family of Bennet daughters find themselves reenacting an ancient story of misunderstandings, precipitous judgments and mate selection.


Chapter One

It is a truth universally stipulated by the law of every colony on Titan, that a childless man endowed with the management of a good mineshare must be in search of a breeding partner.

However the feelings or views of such a man, he is under the obligation to choose a lass to become the mother of his children within two years of taking up the post.

So familiar is this point of law that, on taking up a mineshare, a childless man is immediately considered the rightful protector of some one or other of the daughters of the surrounding families.

“My dear Bennet Pater,’’ said his co-parent to him one day, “have you heard that Netherfield mine is to be open again at last?”
Bennet Pater replied that he had not.
“But it is,” returned she; “for Llewellyn Mater has just been here, and she told me all about it.” Bennet Pater made no answer. He was engaged in applying sealant to the edges of the windows to sand-proof them in preparation for the upcoming sandstorm. It was a task that had to be repeated almost every week, as the sands were unrelenting in their assaults.
“Do not you want to know who has taken it?” cried his co-parent impatiently.
“You want to tell me, and I have no objection to hearing it.” This was invitation enough.
“Why, my dear, you must know, Llewellyn Mater says that the management of Netherfield has been granted to a young man of large fortune from the East, from New Arabia; that he came down on Moonday in a silver V28 Maxius ferflivver to see the place, and was so much delighted with it that he agreed with Morris Pater immediately; that he is to take possession before the end of the dusty season, and some of his employees are to start preliminary work in the mine by the end of next week.”
“What is his name?”
“Bingley.”
“Is he a father or still without issue?”
“Oh! Without issue, my dear, to be sure! A childless man of large fortune; four or five thousand a year. What a fine thing for our girls!”
“How so? How can it affect them?”
“My dear Bennet Pater,’’ replied his co-parent, “how can you be so tiresome! You must know that I am thinking of his choosing one of them as a breeding partner.”
“Is that his design in settling here?”
“Design! Nonsense, how can you talk so! His designs are quite beside the point. You know that he must choose a breeding partner if he is to settle here. You must visit him as soon as he comes.”
“I see no occasion for that. You and the girls go, or you may send them by themselves, which perhaps will be still better; for, as you appear as fertile as any of them, he might like you the best of the party.”
“My dear, you flatter me. I certainly may still retain the appearance of fertility, but I am sure he would be able to perceive that I have already bred. When a woman has five grown up daughters, she can no longer indulge in the idle fantasy of further procreation.”
“Indeed, we have made our contribution to the population of this god-forsaken colony. Surely we can rest on our laurels for a little while before scheming on behalf of the next generation.”
“But, my dear, you must indeed go and see Squire Bingley when he comes into the neighbourhood.”
“It is more than I engage for, I assure you.”
“But consider your daughters. Only think what an establishment it would be for one of them. Senator and Lady Lucas are determined to go, merely on that account, for you know we have so few breeding prospects in the area. Indeed you must go, for it will be impossible for us to visit him, if you do not.”
“You are over-scrupulous, surely. I dare say Squire Bingley will be very glad to see you; and I will send a few lines by you to assure him of my hearty consent to his breeding with which ever he chooses of the girls; though I must add in a line to dissuade him from fixing on Lizzy.”
“I desire you will do no such thing. Lizzy is not a bit better than the others; and I am sure she is not half so handsome as Jane nor half so good humoured as Lydia. But you are always giving her the preference.”
“They have none of them much to recommend them,” replied he; “they are all silly and ignorant like other girls; but Lizzy has something more of quickness than her sisters, and it would be a shame to have that light dimmed too soon by the strictures of motherhood.”
“Bennet Pater, how can you abuse your own children in such way? You take delight in vexing me. You have no compassion on my poor nerves.”
“You mistake me, my dear. I have a high respect for your nerves. They are my old friends. I have heard you mention them with consideration these twenty years at least.’’
“Ah! You do not now what I suffer. You know that if Lizzy, or indeed any of the girls, fails to find herself a breeding partner before she is thirty, she will be forced to leave Titan. Then what will happen to your darling Lizzy?”
“But she is only twenty-two, my dear, and will live to see many young men of four thousand a year come into the neighbourhood.”
“It will be no use to us if twenty such should come, since you will not visit them.” 
“Depend upon it, my dear, that when there are twenty I will visit them all.’’ Bennet Pater was so odd a mixture of quick parts, sarcastic humour, reserve, and caprice, that the experience of five and twenty years had been insufficient to make his co-parent understand his character. Her mind was less difficult to develop. She was a woman of mean understanding, little information, and uncertain temper, but she was also a women with a great affection for her daughters and a lively horror of losing them to exile. When she was nervous, her co-parent castigated her for being silly, but no castigation could sooth her nerves. The business of her life was to get her daughters contracted to a suitable breeding partner, the alternative unthinkable, and the eldest already twenty-four.


Chapter Two

Bennet Pater was among the earliest of those who waited on Squire Bingley, making the twenty mile journey on camelback. He had always intended to visit him, though to the last always assuring Bennet Mater that he should not go; and till the evening after the visit was paid, she had no knowledge of it. It was then disclosed in the following manner. Observing his second daughter employed in embroidering a head scarf, he suddenly addressed her with,
“I hope Squire Bingley will like it, Lizzy, and will take your skill as a testament to your fine genetic heritage.”
“We are not in a way to know what Squire Bingley likes or what standards he uses to judge prospective candidates for motherhood,” said her mother resentfully, “since we are not to visit.”
“But you forget, Mama,” said Elizabeth, “that we shall meet him at the assemblies, and that Llewellyn Mater has promised to introduce him.”
“I do not believe Llewellyn Mater will do any such thing. She has two nieces of her own. She is a selfish, hypocritical woman, and I have no opinion of her.”
“No more have I,” said Bennet Pater; “and I am glad to find that you do not depend on her serving you.”
Bennet Mater deigned not to make any reply; but unable to contain herself, began scolding one of her daughters.
“Don’t keep coughing so, Kitty for heaven’s sake! Have a little compassion on my nerves. You tear them to pieces.”
“Kitty has no discretion in her coughs,” said her father; “she times them ill.” 
“I do not cough for my own amusement,” replied Kitty fretfully.
“It is the dust and the dry air,” said Jane sympathetically. “She was made for a gentler climate.”
“So were we all, but she’s lived on Titan her whole life. She should be used to it by now,” remarked Bennet Pater impatiently.
“Yes,” added Mary, thoughtfully, “And she would be wise not to cough in front of any eligible gentlemen, lest they think her incapable of breeding healthy young.”
Kitty scowled. “I’m sure I shall be a very competent breeder,” she responded.
“When is your next ball to be, Lizzy?”
“Tomorrow fortnight.”
“Aye, so it is,” cried her mother, “and Llewellyn Mater does not come back from visiting her paramour till the day before; it will be impossible for her to introduce him, for she will not know him herself.”
“Then, my dear, you may have the advantage of your friend, and introduce Squire Bingley to her.”
“Impossible, Bennet Pater, impossible, when I am not acquainted with him myself; how can you be so teasing?”
“I honour your circumspection. A fortnight’s acquaintance is certainly very little. One cannot know what a man really is by the end of a fortnight. But if we do not venture, somebody else will; and after all, Llewellyn Mater and her nieces must stand their chance; and therefore, as she will think it an act of kindness, if you decline the office, I will take it on myself.”
The girls stared at their father. Bennet Mater said only, “Nonsense, nonsense!” 
“What can be the meaning of that emphatic exclamation?’’ cried he. “Do you consider the forms of introduction, and the stress that is laid on them, as nonsense? I cannot quite agree with you there. What say you, Mary? For you are a young lady of deep reflection I know, and read great books, and make extracts.” 
Mary strived to say something sensible. “The convention of introductions,” she opined, “allows people to be quickly ascertained of the social status of new acquaintances, so that they need not waste their time making small talk with people of lowly ranks.” 
“Quite so,” her father continued, “Now let us return to Squire Bingley.” 
“I am sick of Squire Bingley,” cried his co-parent.
“I am sorry to hear that; but why did not you tell me so before? If I had known as much this morning, I certainly would not have called on him. It is very unlucky; but as I have actually paid the visit, we cannot escape the acquaintance now.”
The astonishment of the ladies was just what he wished; that of Bennet Mater perhaps surpassing the rest; though when the first tumult of joy was over, she began to declare that it was what she had expected all the while.
“How good it was in you, my dear Bennet Pater! But I knew I should persuade you at last. I was sure you loved our girls too well to neglect such an acquaintance. Well, how pleased I am! And it is such a good joke, too, that you should have gone this morning, and never said a word about it till now.”
“Now, Kitty, you may cough as much as you choose,” said Bennet Pater, “as long as there are no young men about to hear you”; and, as he spoke, he left the room, fatigued with the raptures of his co-parent.
“What an excellent father you have, girls,” said she, when the door was shut. “I do not know how you will ever make him amends for his kindness; or me either, for that matter. At our time of life, it is not so  pleasant I can tell you, to be making new acquaintance every day; but to save you girls from exile, we would do any thing. Lydia, my love, though you are the youngest, I dare say Squire Bingley will dance with you at the next ball.”
“Oh! I am not afraid; for though I am the youngest, I am the most robust,” said Lydia stoutly, with a proud swivel of her sturdy child-bearing hips.
The rest of the evening was spent in conjecturing how soon he would return Bennet Pater’s visit, and determining when they should ask him to dinner.


Chapter Three

Not all that Bennet Mater, however, with the assistance of her five daughters, could ask on the subject was sufficient to draw from her co-parent any satisfactory description of Squire Bingley. They attacked him in various ways; with barefaced questions, ingenious suppositions, and distant surmises; but he eluded the skill of them all; and they were at last obliged to accept the second-hand intelligence of their neighbour Lady Lucas. Her report was highly favourable.  Senator Weiming had been delighted with him. He was quite young, had a handsome physique that spoke of a good genetic heritage, was extremely agreeable, and, to crown the whole, he meant to be at the next assembly with a large party. Nothing could be more delightful! To engage in dancing is certainly a time-honoured means of evaluating reproductive fitness; and very lively hopes of Squire Bingley’s choice lighting on a local lass were entertained.
“If I can but see one of my daughters happily settled at Netherfield,” said Bennet Mater to her co-parent, “and all the others with equally good contracts, I shall have nothing to wish for.”
In a few days Squire Bingley returned Bennet Pater’s visit, and sat about ten minutes with him in his den. He had entertained hopes of being admitted to a sight of the young lasses, of whose fecundity he had heard much; but he saw only the father. The lasses were somewhat more fortunate, for they had the advantage of ascertaining from an upper window, that he wore an azure thobe with gold trim and rode in a handsome two-camel carriage. The white camels against the black sands looked magnificent, posing with the haughty dignity of exquisitely well-bred beasts.
An invitation to dinner was soon afterwards dispatched; and already had Bennet Mater planned the courses that were to do credit to her housekeeping, when an email arrived which deferred it all. Squire Bingley was obliged to be in the capital the following day, and consequently unable to accept the honour of their invitation, &c. Bennet Mater was quite disconcerted. She could not imagine what business he could have in the city so soon after his arrival in Mexistan; and she began to fear that he might be flying about from one place to another, evaluating potential breeding partners throughout the colonies. Lady Lucas quieted her fears a little by starting the idea of his being gone to NeoLondon only to get a large party for the ball; and a report soon followed that Squire Bingley was to bring twelve lasses and seven gentlemen with him to the assembly. The girls grieved over such a large number of lasses; but were comforted the day before the ball by hearing that, instead of twelve, he had brought only six with him from NeoLondon, his four sisters, an aunt and a cousin. And when the party entered the assembly room, it consisted of only five altogether; Squire Bingley, his sister, an aunt and her paramour, and another young man.
Squire Bingley was good looking and gentlemanlike; he had a pleasant countenance, and easy, unaffected manners. His sister and aunt were fine women, with an air of decided fashion. His older sister’s paramour, Hernandez Pater, merely looked the gentleman; but his friend Squire Darcy soon drew the attention of the room by his fine, tall person, handsome features, noble mien; and the report which was in general circulation within five minutes after his entrance, of his having ten thousand a year. The gentlemen pronounced him to be a fine figure of a man and the lasses declared his genetic endowment to be superior even to that of Squire Bingley. He was looked at with great admiration for about half the evening, till his manners gave a disgust which turned the tide of his popularity; for he was discovered to be proud, to be above his company, and above any consideration of local breeding prospects; and not even his management of a large diamond mine could save him from having a most forbidding, disagreeable countenance, and being unworthy to be compared with his friend.
Squire Bingley had soon made himself acquainted with all the principal people in the room; he was lively and unreserved, danced every dance, was angry that the ball closed so early, and talked of giving one himself at Netherfield. Such amiable qualities must speak for themselves. What a contrast between him and his friend! Squire Darcy danced only once with Squire Bingley’s aunt, Sondakh Mater, and once with Miss Bingley, declined being introduced to any other lass, and spent the rest of the evening in walking about the room, speaking occasionally to one of his own party. His character was decided. He was the proudest, most disagreeable man in the world, and everybody hoped that he would never come there again. Amongst the most violent against him was Bennet Mater, whose dislike of his general behaviour was sharpened into particular resentment by his having slighted one of her daughters.
Elizabeth Bennet had been obliged, by the scarcity of gentlemen, to sit down for two dances; and during part of that time, Squire Darcy had been standing near enough for her to overhear a conversation between him and Squire Bingley, who came from the dance for a few minutes to press his friend to join it.
“Come, Darcy,” said he, “I must have you dance. I hate to see you standing about by yourself in this stupid manner. People will think you’re unfit. You had much better dance.”
“I certainly shall not. I don’t care a pebble what people think and you know how I detest it. It is unseemly for a man to take pains to display his genetic quality, and I have no wish to assess the qualifications of any lass here. Your sister is engaged, and there is not another woman in the room whom it would not be a punishment to me to stand up with.”
“I would not be so fastidious as you are,” cried Bingley, “for a Martian kingdom! Upon my honour I never met with so many pleasant lasses in my life, as I have this evening; and there are several of them, you see, uncommonly nubile, and quite brimming with motherly qualities.”
“You are dancing with the only shapely lass in the room,” said Squire Darcy, looking at the eldest Miss Bennet.
“Oh! She is the most fecund and maternal creature I ever beheld! But there is one of her sisters sitting down just behind you, who is very nubile, and I dare say very agreeable. Do let me ask Miss Bingley to introduce you.”
“Which do you mean?” and turning round, he looked for a moment at Elizabeth, till catching her eye, he withdrew his own and coldly said, “She is tolerable; but not shapely enough to tempt me; and I am in no humour at present to give consequence to young lasses who are slighted by other men. You had better return to your nubile partner  and enjoy her smiles, for you are wasting your time with me.”
Squire Bingley followed his advice. Squire Darcy walked off; and Elizabeth remained with no very cordial feelings towards him. She told the story however with great spirit among her friends; for she had a lively, playful disposition, which delighted in any thing ridiculous.
The evening altogether passed off pleasantly to the whole family. Bennet Mater had seen her eldest daughter much admired by the Netherfield party. Squire Bingley had danced with her twice, and she had been distinguished by his sisters. Jane was as much gratified by this as her mother could be, though in a quieter way. Elizabeth felt Jane’s pleasure. Mary had heard herself mentioned to Miss Bingley as the most accomplished lass in the neighbourhood; and Catherine and Lydia had been fortunate enough to be never without partners, which was all that they had yet learnt to care for at a ball. And never had a ball  been so beautiful! The brightly colored saris of the ladies, the elegant linen robes of the gentlemen, the lanterns, the music, the wine...
They returned, therefore, in good spirits to Liqua, the village where they lived, and of which they were the only inhabitants of consequence. They found Bennet Pater still up. With a book, he was regardless of time; and on the present occasion he had a good deal of curiosity as to the event of an evening which had raised such splendid expectations. He had rather hoped that all his coparent’s views on the stranger would be disappointed; but he soon found that he had a very different story to hear.
“Oh! My dear Bennet Pater,’” as she entered the room, “we have had a most delightful evening, a most excellent ball. I wish you had been there. Jane was so admired, nothing could be like it. Every body said how healthy she looked; and Squire Bingley thought her fecund and well-grown, and danced with her twice. Only think of that my dear; he actually danced with her twice; and she was the only creature in the room that he asked a second time. First of all, he asked Miss Lucas. I was so vexed to see him stand up with her; but, however, he did not admire her at all: indeed, nobody can, you know, with those skinny hips and lacklustre hair – the perfect embodiment of feeble genes. He seemed quite struck with Jane as she was going down the dance. So, he enquired who she was, and got introduced, and asked her for the two next. Then the jig he danced with Miss Kwame, and the fandango with Maria Lucas, and the jitterbug with Jane again, and the Charleston he danced with Miss Lizzy, and the Quadrille --”
“If he had had any compassion for me,” cried her coparent impatiently, “he would not have danced half so much! For God’s sake, say no more of his partners. Oh! That he had sprained his ankle in the first dance!”
“Oh! My dear,” continued Bennet Mater, “I am quite delighted with him. He is such a paragon of genetic fitness! And his sister and aunt are charming, well-endowed ladies. I never in my life saw any thing more elegant than than the dresses. I dare say the silver embroidery upon Sondakh Mater’s sari --”
Here she was interrupted again. Bennet Pater protested against any description of finery. She was therefore obliged to seek another branch of the subject, and related, with much bitterness of spirit and some exaggeration, the shocking rudeness of Squire Darcy.
“But I can assure you,” she added, “that Lizzy does not lose much by not suiting his fancy; for he is a most disagreeable, horrid man, not at all worth pleasing. So high and conceited that there was no enduring him! He walked here, and he walked there, fancying himself so very great! Not nubile enough to dance with! I wish you had been there, my dear, to have given him one of your set downs. I quite detest the man. I hope that the entirety of his fortune is inadequate to persuade a single lass to bear his horrid offspring!”


Chapter 4


When Jane and Elizabeth were alone, the former, who had been cautious in her praise of Squire Bingley before, expressed to her sister just how very much she admired him.
“He is just what a young man ought to be,” said she, “sensible, good-humoured, lively; and I never saw such happy manners!--so much ease, with such perfect good breeding!”
“His breeding appears to be impeccable,” Elizabeth agreed. “It certainly has endowed him with a robust physique and a handsome countenance.  He is, moreover, very well-established, which a young man ought likewise to be, if he possibly can. His qualifications are thereby complete.”
“I was very much flattered by his asking me to dance a second time. I did not expect such a compliment.”
“Did not you? I did for you. But that is one great difference between us. Compliments always take you by surprise, and me never. What could be more natural than his asking you again? He could not help seeing that you were about five times as fecund as any other woman in the room. No thanks to his gallantry for that. Well, he certainly is very agreeable, and I give you leave to like him. You have liked many a stupider person.”
“Dear Lizzy!”
“Oh! You are a great deal too apt, you know, to like people in general. You never see a fault in anybody. All the world are good and agreeable in your eyes. I never heard you speak ill of a human being in your life.”
“I would not wish to be hasty in censuring anyone; but I always speak what I think.”
“I know you do; and it is that which makes the wonder. With your good sense, to be so honestly blind to the follies and nonsense of others! Does it never bother you, Jane, that these men you find so charming only want us for our wombs?”
“Don’t be absurd, Elizabeth!” retorted Jane. “We are not mere wombs! Yes, they want us for our child-bearing capacities, but that alone is not all we have to offer. They also prize us for our superior genetics. That is why they care that we are pretty and lively and accomplished. We display our good genes and they know that we could bear them healthy, intelligent children.” Jane reflected with quiet composure on her own excellent breeding potential.
“But what about love?” persisted Elizabeth.
Jane looked puzzled. “But that is love, Lizzy.”
“Is it?” asked Elizabeth. “Is that all there is to love? That’s not how things use to be, in the old days... What if a gentleman evaluated you highly as a mate, decided to breed with you and then discovered that you were barren” (Jane flinched at the vulgarity) “and he wanted to share his life with you anyway. Now that would be love.”
Jane considered the absurd scenario solemnly. “Perhaps,” she said doubtfully, “but that would be romantic love, like paramour love, not co-parenting love,” she said judiciously. “And you know we will have a ample opportunity to experience paramour love some day, when we’re older, after we have bred.”
“But what if you could have paramour love with your breeding partner,” Elizabeth persisted. “Wouldn’t that be sublime?” But even as she spoke these words, Elizabeth understood the impossibility of such a fantasy. Maybe it had been possible once on Earth, but it was not suitable for modern life.
“You have been reading too many ancient Earthan books, Lizzy,” Jane responded. “You know that’s not reality. Reality is co-parenting love, children, and then maybe paramour love, if we live long enough. But that’s far in the future. For now, why not allow us to delight in the admiration of handsome men that might want us to have their babies?” she coaxed. “Like the very charming Squire Bingley...”
“Well he does have very nice manners,” Elizabeth conceded, “and his pedigree is immaculate. Moreover, he seems like a man that would treat you well…
“But tell me,” she continued. “Do you like this man’s sister and aunt, too? Their manners are not equal to his.”
“Certainly not--at first. But they are very pleasing women when you converse with them. Miss Bingley is to live with her brother, and keep his house; and I am much mistaken if we shall not find a very charming neighbour in her.”
Elizabeth listened in silence, but was not convinced; their behaviour at the assembly had not been calculated to please in general; and with more quickness of observation and less pliancy of temper than her sister, and with a judgement too unassailed by any attention to herself, she was very little disposed to approve them. They were in fact very fine ladies; not deficient in good humour when they were pleased, nor in the power of making themselves agreeable when they chose it, but proud and conceited.  They had the good fortune of being so well-connected that they would certainly be able to receive a dispensation to stay on Titan even if they did not find breeding partners, and coupled with that security was the unlikelihood of such an eventuality ever arising. Nature had been kind, amplying endowing them with secondary sexual characteristics that advertised their superior genetics, and they had the additional advantage of being able to supplement nature with artifice, having access to all the hair lotions, skin creams, spa treatments and cosmetics that Titan had to offer. The happy result was a superabundance of fertility signals. To complete their conspicuous superiority, they enjoyed a stunning collection of sarongs and saris to set off their shapely figures. They were therefore in every respect entitled to think well of themselves, and meanly of others. They owed their many advantages to their membership in a respectable family in New Arabia; a circumstance more deeply impressed on their memories than that their brother’s fortune (and thus the source of their own protection) had been acquired by a swashbuckling adventurer and bioengineer.
Their great-grandfather had been exiled from Earth in circumstances that the family had chosen not to remember, but by good fortune his skills as a bioengineer had enabled him to join the pioneering project of terraforming Titan. He had been instrumental in the decision to terraform Titan just sufficiently as to allow human habitation in the equatorial zone, a 3000 mile strip girdling the moon that resembled an earthan desert, although the sand was composed of black hydrocarbon instead of silicon. After terraforming, this habitable zone was as hot as the Sahara, though surrounded by the frozen bulk of Titan on all sides, and the string of settlements within it was sufficient to provide a base for the exploitation of Titan’s precious metal and gem mines.  As a consequence of great-grandfather Bingley’s parsimonious calculations, life was rather primitive on Titan’s desert surface, but the mines made a fortune for some and a living for the rest. As one of the founding fathers, the Bingley ancestor had been granted the first copper mine on Titan and had managed it with scientific efficiency. His sons supplemented his legacy with diamond and ruby mines.
Squire Bingley had inherited property in the amount of nearly a hundred thousand platinums from his father, who had intended to purchase an estate on earth’s moon, but did not live to do so. Squire Bingley intended it likewise, and sometimes even considered the possibility of buying a residency on Earth itself; but as he was now provided with a good mineshare and the liberty of the attached manor, it was doubtful to many of those who best knew the easiness of his temper, whether he might not spend the remainder of his days on Titan, and leave the next generation the option of returning to the old world.
His sisters relied on his staying on Titan. It is doubtful that funds could have been found to purchase residencies for all of them on Earth, and if their brother left Titan, they could no longer rely on his protection. They would face the threat that gave all unpartnered women on Titan uneasy nights – the possibility of being exiled from Titan and forced to look for a position, as breeding partner, laborer or whore, on one of the newer, more remote and distinctly more primitive moon colonies of Jupiter, such as Callisto or Ganymede.
Although Bingley’s sisters would never admit to any anxiety regarding their ability to find suitable breeding partners, and hence secure their positions on Titan, they were nevertheless eager to make colonial life comfortable and attractive to their brother.
Now that Squire Bingley had secured a mineshare at Netherfield, Miss Bingley was pleased to preside at his table—and his aunt, Sondakh Mater, whose breeding years were behind her and whose children had reached an degree of independence that enabled her to pursue romantic liasions, was no less disposed to consider his house as her home when it suited her, often bringing a paramour with her. Squire Bingley had not been of age two years, when he was tempted by an accidental recommendation to look at Netherfield mine. He did look at it, and into it for half-an-hour--was pleased with its prospects, satisfied with what the owner said in its praise, and accepted the position immediately.
Between him and Darcy there was a very steady friendship, in spite of great opposition of character. Bingley was endeared to Darcy by the easiness, openness, and ductility of his temper, though no disposition could offer a greater contrast to his own, and though with his own he never appeared dissatisfied. On the strength of Darcy’s regard, Bingley had the firmest reliance, and of his judgement the highest opinion. In understanding, Darcy was the superior. Bingley was by no means deficient, but Darcy was clever. He was at the same time haughty, reserved, and fastidious, and his manners, though polished, were not inviting. In that respect his friend had greatly the advantage. Bingley was sure of being liked wherever he appeared, Darcy was continually giving offense.
The manner in which they spoke of the Mandalay assembly was sufficiently characteristic. Bingley had never met with more pleasant people or more promising breeding prospects in his life; everybody had been most kind and attentive to him; there had been no formality, no stiffness; he had soon felt acquainted with all the room; and, as to Miss Bennet, he had never met a woman more suitable to bear his children. Darcy, on the contrary, had seen a collection of people in whom there was little beauty and no fashion, for none of whom he had felt the smallest interest in forming a breeding arrangement or even in engaging in conversation, and from none received either attention or pleasure. Miss Bennet he acknowledged to be nubile, and quite likely a suitable co-parent for Bingley, but she smiled too much.
Sondakh Mater and her niece allowed it to be so--but still they admired her and liked her, and pronounced her to be a sweet lass, and one whom they would not object to know more of. Miss Bennet was therefore established as a sweet lass, and Squire Bingley felt authorized by such commendation to consider her qualifications for motherhood.


Chapter 5

Within a short ride of Liqua lived a family with whom the Bennets were particularly intimate. Senator Weiming Lucas had been formerly the manager of a small tungsten mine in Mandalay, where he had made a tolerable fortune, and risen to the honour of an appointment to the Colony senate. The distinction had perhaps been felt too strongly. It had given him a disgust to his business, and to his residence in a small mining town; and, in quitting them both, he had removed with his family to a house about ten miles from Mandalay, denominated from that period Lucas Lodge, where he could think with pleasure of his own importance; and since the duties of the Senate were light, consisting primarily of efficiently sanctioning the decisions of the heriditary governors, he had ample leisure to devote himself in being civil to all the world. For, though elated by his position, it did not render him supercilious; on the contrary, he was all attention to everybody. By nature inoffensive, friendly, and obliging, his appointment to the Senate had made him courteous.
Lady Lucas was a very good kind of woman, not too clever to be a valuable neighbour to Bennet Mater. They had several children. The eldest of them had been unable to find a breeding partner and was exiled to Ganymede, though her family did not dare to ask how she made a living there. The second eldest, a sensible, intelligent young woman of about twenty-seven, was Elizabeth’s intimate friend.
That the Miss Lucases and the Miss Bennets should meet to talk over a ball was absolutely necessary; and the morning after the assembly brought the former to Liqua to hear and to communicate.
“You  began the evening well, Charlotte,” said Bennet Mater with civil self-command to Miss Lucas. You were Squire Bingley’s first choice.”
“Yes; but he seemed to like his second better.”
“Oh! You mean Jane, I suppose, because he danced with her twice. To be sure that did seem as if he admired her qualifications—indeed I rather believe he did—I heard something about it—but I hardly know what—something about Squire Rubenstein.”
 “Perhaps you mean what I overheard between him and Squire Rubenstein; did not I mention it to you? Squire Rubenstein’s asking him how he liked our Mandalay assemblies, and whether he did not think there were a great many nubile women in the room, and which he thought the shapeliest and his answering immediately to the last question: ‘Oh! The eldest Miss Bennet, beyond a doubt; there cannot be two opinions on that point.’”

“Upon my word! Well, that is very decided indeed—that does seem as if—but, however, it may all come to nothing, you know.”
“My overhearings were more to the purpose than yours, Eliza,” said Charlotte. “Squire Darcy is not so well worth listening to as his friend, is he?—poor Eliza!—to be only just tolerable.”
“I beg you would not put it into Lizzy’s head to be vexed by his ill-treatment, for he is such a disagreeable man, that it would be quite a misfortune to be liked by him. Llewellyn Mater told me last night that he sat close to her for half-an-hour without once opening his lips.”
“Miss Bingley told me,” said Jane, “that he never speaks much, unless among his intimate acquaintances. With them he is remarkably agreeable.”
“I do not believe a word of it, my dear. If he had been so very agreeable, he would have talked to Llewellyn Mater. But I can guess how it was; everybody says that he is eaten up with pride, and I dare say he had heard somehow that Llewellyn Mater does not keep a ferflivver, or even a camel, and had to travel the twenty miles to the ball in a llama cart.”
“I do not mind his not talking to Llewellyn Mater,” said Miss Lucas, “but I wish he had danced with Eliza.”
“Another time, Lizzy,” said her mother, “I would not dance with him, if I were you.”
“I believe, ma’am, I may safely promise you never to dance with him.”
“His pride,” said Miss Lucas, “does not offend me so much as pride often does, because there is an excuse for it. One cannot wonder that so very fine a young man, with family, fortune and impeccable genetics, should think highly of himself. If I may so express it, he has a right to be proud.”
“That is very true,” replied Elizabeth, “and I could easily forgive his pride, if he had not mortified mine.”
“Pride,” observed Mary, who preened herself upon the solidity of her reflections, “is a very common failing, I believe. By all that I have ever read, I am convinced that the urge to obtain status among one’s conspecifics, and to defend such status once achieved, is very common indeed throughout the animal kingdom and that humans are particularly prone to it.  Vanity and pride are different things, though the words are often used synonymously. A person may be proud without being vain. Pride relates more to our intellectual or social status within a community, vanity to the need to attract suitable mates.”
“If I were as rich as Squire Darcy,” cried a young Lucas, who came with his sisters, “I should not care how proud I was. I would keep a houseful of domestic robots, have a fleet of ferflivvers and gyrocopters, and play war games in my holodeck all day long.”
“Oh what I would give for even one domestic robot,” sighed Bennet Mater wistfully.
“But as to spending all day in a holodeck,” she cried, turning to the boy, “I’m sure you would be immersed in fantasy a great deal more than you ought to be,” said Bennet Mater; “and if I were to see you at it, I should lock up your holodeck directly.”
The boy protested that she should not; she continued to declare that she would, and the argument ended only with the visit.


Chapter 6

The ladies of Liqua soon called on those of Netherfield. The visit was soon returned in due form. Miss Bennet’s pleasing manners grew on the goodwill of Sondakh Mater and Miss Bingley; and though the mother was found to be intolerable, and the younger sisters not worth speaking to, a wish of being better acquainted with them was expressed towards the two eldest. By Jane, this attention was received with the greatest pleasure, but Elizabeth still saw superciliousness in their treatment of everybody, hardly excepting even her sister, and could not like them; even their kindness to Jane, such as it was, arose in all probability from the influence of their brother’s admiration. On the one hand, they could scarcely afford to offend their fraternal protector, and on the other hand, a breeding partner would solidify Squire Bingley’s ties to Titan and reduce the likelihood of his abandoning his sisters to the vagaries of this desperate desert society. It was generally evident whenever they met, that Squire Bingley did admire her and to Elizabeth it was equally evident that Jane was yielding to the preference which she had begun to entertain for him from the first, and was in a way to be very much in love; but she considered with pleasure that it was not likely to be discovered by the world in general, since Jane united, with great strength of feeling, a composure of temper and a uniform cheerfulness of manner which would guard her from the suspicions of the impertinent. Until everything was settled, it behooved Jane to be discreet in her feelings, in case Squire Bingley should unaccountably decide that another woman would make a better mother to his children. As open and artless as Jane was, Elizabeth knew that it would be severely damaging to her future prospects of partnering well if it came to be known that she had loved and been rejected by another man. She mentioned this to her friend Miss Lucas.
“It may perhaps be prudent,” replied Charlotte, “to be able to impose on the public in such a case; but it is sometimes a disadvantage to be so very guarded. If a woman conceals her affection with the same skill from the object of it, she may lose the opportunity of fixing him; and it will then be but poor consolation to believe the world equally in the dark.  There is so much vanity in men, that even when choosing a breeding partner and not a paramour, a man cannot help but to be influenced by a lass’s admiration for himself. And then, of course,” she reflected further, “there is a superstition among men that a breeding partner who is initially in love with them is less likely to stray before the end of her breeding years, possibly incurring an illegitimate conception...”
“Don’t be absurd,” said Elizabeth sharply. “No woman on Titan would dare to stray before the end of her breeding years! If an illegitimate baby were conceived, her infidelity would be detected immediately upon its first prenatal DNA analysis, whereupon she’d be thrown off the Colony and left to her own devices.” Elizabeth shivered. “No one would dare.”
“Yes, of course,” said Charlotte in a conciliatory tone. “We know that and so do they, but it is nevertheless a kind of ancient superstition among them.  There are very few of men who have heart enough to enter into a breeding arrangement without harboring the belief that a woman is truly in love with them. In nine cases out of ten a women had better show more affection than she feels. Bingley likes your sister undoubtedly; but he may never commit to a breeding partnership, if she does not help him on.”
“But she does help him on, as much as her nature will allow. If I can perceive her regard for him, he must be a simpleton, indeed, not to discover it too.”
“Remember, Eliza, that he does not know Jane’s disposition as you do.”
“But if a woman is partial to a man, and does not endeavour to conceal it, he must find it out.”
“Perhaps he must, if he sees enough of her. But, though Bingley and Jane meet tolerably often, it is never for many hours together; and, as they always see each other in large mixed parties, it is impossible that every moment should be employed in conversing together. Jane should therefore make the most of every half-hour in which she can command his attention. When she is secure of him, there will be more leisure for falling in love as much as she chooses.”
“Your plan is a good one,” replied Elizabeth, “where nothing is in question but the desire of being well partnered, and if I were determined to get a breeding partner with a good establishment, or in a desperate plight to attract any breeding partner at all, I dare say I should adopt it. But these are not Jane’s feelings; she is not acting by design, and she has many years before desperation sets in. As yet, she cannot even be certain of the degree of her own regard nor of its reasonableness. She has known him only a fortnight. She danced four dances with him at Mandalay; she saw him one morning at his own house, and has since dined with him in company four times. This is not quite enough to make her understand his character.”
“Not as you represent it. Had she merely dined with him, she might only have discovered whether he had a good appetite; but you must remember that four evenings have also been spent together--and four evenings may do a great deal.”
“Yes; these four evenings have enabled them to ascertain that they both like the blues better than Mozart and dungeons and dragons better than go; that they are both reasonably intelligent but not intellectual, healthy and in possession of good genes, and rather more good-natured than may be wise, but with respect to any other leading characteristic, I do not imagine that much has been unfolded.”
“Well,” said Charlotte, “I wish Jane success with all my heart; and if she were to enter into a breeding contract with him tomorrow, I should think she had as good a chance of happiness as if she were to be studying his character for a tenmonth. Happiness in co-parenting arrangements is entirely a matter of chance. If the dispositions of the parties are ever so well known to each other or ever so similar beforehand, it does not advance their felicity in the least. After a short period of blissful cohabitation, she will become entirely immersed in the business of motherhood and he will begin to take paramours. They will continue to grow sufficiently unlike afterwards that by the time she is eligible to take a paramour of her own, the relationship will have become so detached as to be able to continue in civil cooperation unfettered by passion. Outside the realm of genetics, it is better to know as little as possible of the defects of the person with whom you are to breed.”
“You make me laugh, Charlotte; but it is not sound. You know it is not sound, and that you would never act in this way yourself.”
“Well, you are twenty-two and Jane is twenty-four, and you both exude fecundity. You have the leisure to be romantic and finicky in your choice. I am twenty-seven and rapidly losing what nubility I have ever possessed. I have but three years to find myself a partner and I have already lost one sister to exile. I consider, therefore, that pragmatism is an appropriate principle for me to adopt.”
Occupied in observing Bingley’s attentions to her sister, Elizabeth was far from suspecting that she was herself becoming an object of some interest in the eyes of his friend. Darcy had at first scarcely allowed her to be nubile; he had looked at her without admiration at the ball; and when they next met, he looked at her only to criticise. But no sooner had he made it clear to himself and his friends that she hardly had a good feature in her face, than he began to find it was rendered uncommonly intelligent by the beautiful expression of her dark eyes. To this discovery succeeded some others equally mortifying. Though he had detected with a critical eye more than one failure of perfect symmetry in her form, he was forced to acknowledge her figure to be light and pleasing; and in spite of his asserting that her manners were not those of the fashionable world, he was caught by their easy playfulness. Of this she was perfectly unaware; to her he was only the man who made himself agreeable nowhere, and who had not thought her nubile enough to dance with.
He was aware that it would be perverse for him to develop a romantic interest in a lass who had not yet bred, and with whom, moreover, he could never consider entering into a breeding partnership. In spite of the faint suspicion that he may be entertaining inappropriate feelings, however, he began to wish to know more of her, and as a step towards conversing with her himself, attended to her conversation with others. His doing so drew her notice. It was at Senator Weiming Lucas’s, where a large party were assembled.
“What does Squire Darcy mean,” said she to Charlotte, “by listening to my conversation with Colonel Forster?”
“That is a question which Squire Darcy only can answer.”
“But if he does it any more I shall certainly let him know that I see what he is about. He has a very satirical eye, and if I do not begin being impertinent myself, I shall soon grow afraid of him.”
On his approaching them soon afterwards, though without seeming to have any intention of speaking, Miss Lucas defied her friend to mention such a subject to him; which immediately provoking Elizabeth to do it, she turned to him and said:
“Did you not think, Squire Darcy, that I expressed myself uncommonly well just now, when I was importuning Colonel Forster to give us a ball at Mandalay?”
“With great energy; but it is always a subject which makes a lass energetic.”
“You are severe on us.”
“It will be her turn soon to be importuned,” said Miss Lucas. “I am going to open the instrument, Eliza, and you know what follows.”
“You are a very strange creature by way of a friend!--always wanting me to play and sing before anybody and everybody! If I had chosen to develop a musical talent as a way advertising the fitness of my genes, you would have been invaluable; but as it is, I would really rather not sit down before those who must be in the habit of hearing the very best performers.” On Miss Lucas’s persevering, however, she added, “Very well, if it must be so, it must.”
Her performance was pleasing, though by no means capital. After a song or two, and before she could reply to the entreaties of several that she would sing again, she was eagerly succeeded at the instrument by her sister Mary, who having, in consequence of being the least nubile lass in the family, worked hard for knowledge and accomplishments, and was always impatient for display. Not being endowed with the shapeliness of her sisters, Mary’s only hope was to attract a man who valued the possibility of intelligent children over the probability of robust ones. After all, although life on Titan was physically demanding, and the prospect of frail children understandably abhored, intelligence was always an advantage. With full understanding of the precariousness of her position even at the tender age of eighteen, she was motivated to work hard at the acquisition of accomplishments that could advertise her less apparent genetic gifts and eagerly seized occasions for demonstrating them.
Squire Darcy stood near them in silent indignation at such a mode of passing the evening, to the exclusion of all conversation, and was too much engrossed by his thoughts to perceive that Senator Weiming Lucas was his neighbour, till Senator Lucas thus began:
“What a charming amusement for young people this is, Squire Darcy! There is nothing like dancing after all. I consider it as one of the first refinements of polished society.”
“Certainly, sir; and it has the advantage also of being in vogue amongst the less polished societies of the world. Every savage can dance.”
Senator Lucas smiled. “Yes, but what better and more pleasing way of discerning the relative genetic endowments of young people! Whether savage or sophisticate, the best dancers must draw on considerable internal resources of intelligence, agility and dexterity. Truly, as they say, good dancers make good parents.”
“Your friend performs delightfully,” he continued after a pause, on seeing Bingley join the group; “and I doubt not that you are an adept in the science yourself, Squire Darcy.”
“You saw me dance at Mandalay, I believe, sir.”
“Yes, indeed, and received no inconsiderable pleasure from the sight. Do you often dance at St. James’s?”
“Never, sir.”
“Do you not think it would be a proper compliment to the place?”
“It is a compliment which I never pay to any place if I can avoid it.”
“You have a house in the city, I conclude?”
Squire Darcy bowed.
“I had once had some thought of fixing in town myself--for I am fond of superior society; but I did not feel quite certain that the air of NeoLondon would agree with Lady Lucas. She is sensitive to the high levels of cadmium there.”
He paused in hopes of an answer; but his companion was not disposed to make any; and Elizabeth at that instant moving towards them, he was struck with the action of doing a very gallant thing, and called out to her:
“My dear Miss Eliza, why are you not dancing? Squire Darcy, you must allow me to present this young lass to you as a very desirable partner. You cannot refuse to give this young lass a chance to display her nubility.”
And, taking her hand, he would have given it to Squire Darcy who, though extremely surprised, was not unwilling to receive it, when she instantly drew back, and said with some discomposure to Senator Lucas:
“Indeed, sir, I have not the least intention of dancing. I entreat you not to suppose that I moved this way in order to beg for a partner.”
Squire Darcy, with grave propriety, requested to be allowed the honour of her hand, but in vain. Elizabeth was determined; nor did Senator Lucas at all shake her purpose by his attempt at persuasion.
“You excel so much in the dance, Miss Eliza, that it would be a shame to miss this opportunity to display your shapeliness and grace to this room of potential fathers; and though this gentleman dislikes the amusement in general, he can have no objection, I am sure, to oblige us for one half-hour.”
“Squire Darcy is all politeness,” said Elizabeth, smiling.
“He is, indeed; but, considering the inducement, my dear Miss Eliza, we cannot wonder at his complaisance--for who would object to such a partner?”
Elizabeth looked archly, and turned away. Her resistance had not injured her with the gentleman, and he was thinking of her with some complacency, when thus accosted by Miss Bingley:
“I can guess the subject of your reverie.”
“I should imagine not.”
“You are considering how insupportable it would be to pass many evenings in this manner--in such society; and indeed I am quite of your opinion. I was never more annoyed! The insipidity, and yet the noise—the nothingness, and yet the self-importance of all those people! What would I give to hear your strictures on them!”
“Your conjecture is totally wrong, I assure you. My mind was more agreeably engaged. I have been meditating on the important role a pair of fine eyes in the face of a nubile lass has in a thorough assessment of genetic quality.”
Miss Bingley immediately fixed her eyes on his face, and desired he would tell her what lass had the credit of inspiring such reflections. Squire Darcy replied with great intrepidity:
“Miss Elizabeth Bennet.”
“Miss Elizabeth Bennet!” repeated Miss Bingley. “I am all astonishment. How long has she been such a favourite?--and pray, when am I to wish you joy?”
“That is exactly the question which I expected you to ask. A lady’s imagination is very rapid; it jumps from admiration to breeding arrangements, from breeding arrangements to children, in a moment. I knew you would be wishing me joy.”
“Nay, if you are serious about it, I shall consider the matter is absolutely settled. Your children  will have a charming grandmother, indeed; and, of course, she will always be at Pemberley with you.”
He listened to her with perfect indifference while she chose to entertain herself in this manner; and as his composure convinced her that all was safe, her wit flowed long.



Chapter 7

Bennet Pater’s living was derived almost entirely from a position as a law clerk worth five hundred a year, which would scarcely be enough to keep his family in comfort, were it not for a modest but agreeable home inherited from a distant relation. Unfortunately, given that he had no sons, there was no one in the family to inherit the property or earn a living after his death. That his daughters secured good breeding contracts, therefore, was essential not only to their continued existence on Titan but even to the shelter and sustainance of Bennet Mater after the demise of her co-parent.

The village of Liqua was only ten miles from Mandalay; a most convenient distance for the young lasses to travel by llama, and they were usually tempted thither three or four times a week, to pay their duty to their aunt and to a boutique just over the way. The two youngest of the family, Catherine and Lydia, were particularly frequent in these attentions; their minds were more vacant than their sisters’, and when nothing better offered, a trip to Mandalay was necessary to amuse their morning hours and furnish conversation for the evening; and however bare of news the colony in general might be, they always contrived to learn some from their aunt. At present, indeed, they were well supplied both with news and happiness by the recent arrival of an aeronautical regiment in the neighbourhood; it was to remain the whole of the dusty season, as planetary exploration for new mining sites would have to wait until the sand storms had quite passed.

Their visits to Phillips Mater were now productive of the most interesting intelligence. Every day added something to their knowledge of the astronauts’ names and connections. Their lodgings were not long a secret, and at length they began to know the astronauts themselves. Phillips Pater visited them all, and this opened to his nieces a store of felicity unknown before. They could talk of nothing but astronauts; and Squire Bingley’s large fortune, the mention of which gave animation to their mother, was worthless in their eyes when opposed to the devilish gallantry of an astronaut.

After listening one morning to their effusions on this subject, Bennet Pater coolly observed:

“From all that I can collect by your manner of talking, you must be two of the silliest girls in the Colony. I have suspected it some time, but I am now convinced.”

Catherine was disconcerted, and made no answer; but Lydia, with perfect indifference, continued to express her admiration of Captain Carter, and her hope of seeing him in the course of the day, as he was going the next morning to NeoLondon.

“I am astonished, my dear,” said Bennet Mater, “that you should be so ready to think your own children silly. If I wished to think slightingly of anybody’s children, it should not be of my own.”

“If my children are silly, I must hope to be always sensible of it.”

“Yes--but as it happens, they are all of them very clever.”

“This is the only point, I flatter myself, on which we do not agree. I had hoped that our sentiments coincided in every particular, but I must so far differ from you as to think our two youngest daughters uncommonly foolish.”

“My dear Bennet Pater, you must not expect such girls to have the sense of their father and mother. What would it profit them to be serious? Of course, they ought to wait until they are closer to my age before dreaming of dalliances with astronauts. I like an explorer myself very well, and if a smart young colonel should show an interest, I shall not say nay to him. I thought Colonel Forster looked very becoming the other night at Senator Lucas’s...” Bennet Mater trailed off dreamily and then recovered herself. “But, indeed, there are some astronauts that have not yet become fathers and may be interested in one of my girls as a breeding partner. In such a case, liveliness of spirits will answer better to their genetic quality than a solemn demeanor.”

Bennet Pater was about to take issue with Bennet Mater regarding the desirability of astronauts as co-parents, when Jane announced the arrival of an email from Netherfield. Bennet Mater’s eyes sparkled with pleasure, and she was eagerly calling out, while her daughter read,

“Well, Jane, who is it from? What is it about? What does he say? Well, Jane, make haste and tell us; make haste, my love.”

“It is from Miss Bingley,” said Jane, and then read it aloud.

“MY DEAR FRIEND,--

“If you are not so compassionate as to dine today with Louisa and me, we shall be in danger of hating each other for the rest of our lives, for a whole day’s tete-a-tete between two ladies can never end without a quarrel. Come as soon as you can on receipt of this. My brother and the gentlemen are to dine with the astronauts.--Yours ever,

“CAROLINE BINGLEY”

“With the astronauts!” cried Lydia. “I wonder my aunt did not tell us of that.”

“Dining out,” said Bennet Mater, “that is very unlucky.”

“Can I have the ferflivver?” said Jane.

“No, my dear, you had better go on llamaback because it seems likely to storm; and then you must stay all night.”

“That would be a good scheme,” said Elizabeth, “if you were sure that they would not offer to send her home before the storm hits.”

“Oh! But the gentlemen will have Squire Bingley’s ferflivver to go to Mandalay.”

“I had much rather go in the ferflivver.”

“But, my dear, your father cannot spare the fuel, I am sure. You are very low on resources are you not, Bennet Pater?”

“I am always low on resources, and I would that you were always so frugal.”

Jane was therefore obliged to go on llamaback, and her mother attended her to the door with many cheerful prognostics of a bad day. Her hopes were answered; Jane had not been gone long before the wind picked up. Her sisters were uneasy for her, but her mother was delighted. The wind and sand swirled continually the whole evening without intermission; Jane certainly could not come back.

“This was a lucky idea of mine, indeed!” said Bennet Mater more than once, as if the credit of making a sandstorm were all her own. Till the next morning, however, she was not aware of all the felicity of her contrivance. Breakfast was scarcely over when the following email arrived for Elizabeth:

“MY DEAREST LIZZY,--

“I find myself very unwell this morning, which, I suppose, is to be imputed to my breathing in too much sand and dust yesterday. My kind friends will not hear of my returning till I am better. They insist also on my seeing Dr. Jones--therefore do not be alarmed if you should hear of his having been to me--and, excepting a sore throat and headache, there is not much the matter with me.--Yours, etc.”

“Well, my dear,” said Bennet Pater, when Elizabeth had read the note aloud, “if your daughter should have a dangerous fit of illness--if she should die, it would be a comfort to know that it was all in pursuit of Squire Bingley, and under your orders.”

“Oh! I am not afraid of her dying. People do not die of trifling respiratory ailments. She will be taken good care of. As long as she stays there, it is all very well. I would go and see her if I could have the ferflivver.”

Elizabeth, feeling really anxious, was determined to go to her, though the ferflivver was not to be had; camelback was her only alternative. She declared her resolution.

“How can you be so silly,” cried her mother, “as to think of such a thing, in all this sand and wind! You will not be fit to be seen when you get there.”

“I shall be very fit to see Jane--which is all I want.”

“Is this a hint to me, Lizzy,” said her father, “to release the ferflivver?”

“No, indeed, I do not wish to avoid the ride. The distance is nothing when one has a motive; only twenty miles, and the storm is still in its early stages. I shall be back by dinner.”

It was a dusty but invigorating ride on an animal so well genetically modified to Titan’s harsh climate that it could cover the twenty miles in less than two hours, undeterred by the thick clouds of sand that rendered it completely blind and forced it to rely on Elizabeth’s guidance. The challenge for Elizabeth of interpreting GPS readings and guiding the camel, all the while breathing through a filtration headscarf and attempting not be be unseated by the sandy wind, left her flushed with joyful triumph when at last she reached Netherfield.

She was shown into the breakfast-parlour, where all but Jane were assembled, and where her appearance created a great deal of surprise. That she should have ridden twenty miles so early in the day, in such frightful weather, and by herself, was almost incredible to Sondakh Mater and Miss Bingley; and Elizabeth was convinced that they held her in contempt for it. She was received, however, very politely by them; and in Squire Bingley’s manners there was something better than politeness; there was good humour and kindness. Squire Darcy said very little, and Hernandez Pater nothing at all. The former was divided between admiration of the brilliancy which exercise had given to her complexion, and doubt as to the occasion’s justifying her coming so far alone. The latter was thinking only of his breakfast.

Her inquiries after her sister were not very favourably answered. Miss Bennet had slept ill, and though up, was very feverish, and not well enough to leave her room. Elizabeth was glad to be taken to her immediately; and Jane, who had only been withheld by the fear of giving alarm or inconvenience from expressing in her note how much she longed for such a visit, was delighted at her entrance. She was not equal, however, to much conversation, and when Miss Bingley left them together, could attempt little besides expressions of gratitude for the extraordinary kindness she was treated with. Elizabeth silently attended her.

When breakfast was over they were joined by the sisters; and Elizabeth began to like them herself, when she saw how much affection and solicitude they showed for Jane. The doctor came, and having examined his patient, said, as might be supposed, that she had developed bronchitis, and that they must endeavour to get the better of it; advised her to return to bed, and promised her some draughts. The advice was followed readily, for the feverish symptoms increased, and her head ached acutely. Elizabeth did not quit her room for a moment; nor were the other ladies often absent; the gentlemen being out, they had, in fact, nothing to do elsewhere.

When the clock struck three, Elizabeth felt that she must go, and very unwillingly said so. Miss Bingley offered her the ferflivver, and she only wanted a little pressing to accept it, when Jane testified such concern in parting with her, that Miss Bingley was obliged to convert the offer of the ferflivver to an invitation to remain at Netherfield for the present. Elizabeth most thankfully consented.



Chapter 8


At five o’clock the two ladies retired to dress, and at half-past six Elizabeth was summoned to dinner. To the civil inquiries which then poured in, and amongst which she had the pleasure of distinguishing the much superior solicitude of Squire Bingley’s, she could not make a very favourable answer. Jane was by no means better. The sisters, on hearing this, repeated three or four times how much they were grieved, how shocking it was to have a respiratory infection, how excessively they disliked being ill and how lucky they were that they were so rarely ill themselves; and then thought no more of the matter: and their indifference towards Jane when not immediately before them restored Elizabeth to the enjoyment of all her former dislike.
Their brother, indeed, was the only one of the party whom she could regard with any complacency. His anxiety for Jane was evident, and his attentions to herself most pleasing, and they prevented her feeling herself so much an intruder as she believed she was considered by the others. She had very little notice from any but him. Miss Bingley was engrossed by Squire Darcy, her sister scarcely less so; and as for Hernandez Pater, by whom Elizabeth sat, he was an indolent man, who lived only to eat, drink and make love to Sondakh Mater.
When dinner was over, she returned directly to Jane, and Miss Bingley began abusing her as soon as she was out of the room. Her manners were pronounced to be very bad indeed, a mixture of pride and impertinence; she had no conversation, no style and very deficient secondary sexual characteristics. Sondakh Mater thought the same, and added:
“She has nothing, in short, to recommend her, but being an excellent rider. I shall never forget her appearance this morning. She really looked almost wild.”
“She did, indeed, Louisa. I could hardly keep my countenance. Very nonsensical to come at all! Why must she be scampering about the desert, because her sister had bronchitis? Her hair, so untidy, so blowsy!”
“Yes, and her sari; I hope you saw her sari, the silk grazed and ripped from the assault of the sand; and the robes which had been supposed to protect it not doing their office.”
“Your picture may be very exact, Louisa,” said Bingley; “but this was all lost upon me. I thought Miss Elizabeth Bennet looked remarkably well when she came into the room this morning. Her sand-scarred sari quite escaped my notice.”
“You observed it, Squire Darcy, I am sure,” said Miss Bingley; “and I am inclined to think that you would not wish to see your sister make such an exhibition.”
“Certainly not.”
“To  ride ten miles, or fifteen miles, or twenty miles, or whatever it is, through a sandstorm, quite alone! What could she mean by it? It seems to me to show an abominable sort of conceited independence, an ill-bred indifference to decorum.”
“It shows an affection for her sister that is very pleasing,” said Bingley.
“I am afraid, Squire Darcy,” observed Miss Bingley in a half whisper, “that this adventure has rather affected your admiration of her fine eyes.”
“Not at all,” he replied; “they were brightened by the exercise.” A short pause followed this speech, and Sondakh Mater began again:
“I have an excessive regard for Miss Jane Bennet, she is really a very sweet lass, though I hope her health is not always so delicate. I wish with all my heart she were well settled. But with such a father and mother, and such unhealthy connections, I am afraid there is no chance of it.”
“I think I have heard you say that she has a cousin with flat feet.” 
“Yes; and they have another, with asthma.”
“That is capital,” added her sister, and they both laughed heartily.
“If they had enough sickly cousins to fill a hospital,” cried Bingley, “it would not make them one jot less fit.”
“But it must very materially lessen their chance of forming breeding contracts with any men of consideration in the world,” replied Darcy. “They may exhibit a phenotype of good health, but what deleterious recessive genes may be lurking in their genotypes?”
To this speech Bingley made no answer; but his sisters gave it their hearty assent, and indulged their mirth for some time at the expense of their dear friend’s imperfect relations.
With a renewal of tenderness, however, they returned to her room on leaving the dining-parlour, and sat with her till summoned to coffee. She was still very poorly, and Elizabeth would not quit her at all, till late in the evening, when she had the comfort of seeing her sleep, and when it seemed to her rather right than pleasant that she should go downstairs herself. On entering the drawing-room she found the whole party at poker, and was immediately invited to join them; but suspecting them to be playing for high stakes she declined it, and making her sister the excuse, said she would amuse herself for the short time she could stay below, with a book. Hernandez Pater looked at her with astonishment.
“Do you prefer reading to cards?” said he; “that is rather singular.” 
“Miss Eliza Bennet,” said Miss Bingley, “despises cards. She is a great reader, and has no pleasure in anything else.” 
“I deserve neither such praise nor such censure,” cried Elizabeth; “I am not a great reader, and I have pleasure in many things.” 
“In nursing your sister I am sure you have pleasure,” said Bingley; “and I hope it will be soon increased by seeing her quite well.” Elizabeth thanked him from her heart, and then walked towards the table where a few books were lying. He immediately offered to fetch her others—all that his library afforded.
“And I wish my collection were larger for your benefit and my own credit; but I am an idle fellow, and though I have not many, I have more than I ever looked into.”
Elizabeth assured him that she could suit herself perfectly with those in the room.
“I am astonished,” said Miss Bingley, “that my father should have left so small a collection of books. What a delightful library you have at Pemberley, Squire Darcy!”
“It ought to be good,” he replied, “it has been the work of many generations.” 
“It is a charming, but peculiar, object for a family collection,” she continued. “It is so much more convenient to read on a computer. And it is not as though you need to conserve battery cells!” 
“I prefer reading real books. The leather and pulp transport me to an earlier, more authentic age.” 
Elizabeth was so much caught with what passed, as to leave her very little attention for her book; and soon laying it wholly aside, she drew near the card-table, and stationed herself between Squire Bingley and his eldest sister, to observe the game.
“Is Miss Darcy much grown since the clear season?” said Miss Bingley; “will she be as tall as I am?”
“I think she will. She is now about Miss Elizabeth Bennet’s height, or somewhat taller, and rather well-developed too.”
“How I long to see her again! I never met with anybody who delighted me so much. Such a shapely body, such manners! And so extremely accomplished for her age! Her performance on the violin is exquisite.”
“It is amazing to me,” said Bingley, “how young lasses can have patience to be so very accomplished as they all are.”
“All young lasses accomplished! My dear Charles, what do you mean?” 
“Yes, all of them, I think. They all build furniture, breed chickens, and grow herbs. I scarcely know anyone who cannot do all this, and I am sure I never heard a young lady spoken of for the first time, without being informed that she was very accomplished.”
 “Your list of the common extent of accomplishments,” said Darcy, “has too much truth. The word is applied to many a potential breeding partner who deserves it no otherwise than by building a chair or seasoning a dish. But I am very far from agreeing with you in your estimation of lasses in general. I cannot boast of knowing no more than half-a-dozen, in the whole range of my acquaintance, that are really accomplished.” 
“Nor I, I am sure,” said Miss Bingley.
“Then,” observed Elizabeth, “you must comprehend a great deal in your idea of an accomplished candidate for motherhood.”
“Yes, I do comprehend a great deal in it.”
“Oh! Certainly,” cried his faithful assistant, “no one can be really be qualified for motherhood who does not greatly surpass what is usually met with. A woman must have a thorough knowledge of music, singing, and dancing,  and an understanding of theoretical physics and practical biology to deserve the word; and besides all this, she must possess the physical grace and verbal eloquence that emerge naturally from good health and intelligence, or the word will be but half-deserved.”
“All this she must possess,” added Darcy, “and to all this she must yet add something more substantial, in the improvement of her mind by extensive reading.”
“I am no longer surprised at your knowing only six women qualified to breed. I rather wonder now at your knowing any.”
“Are you so severe upon your own sex as to doubt the possibility of all this?” 
“I never saw such a woman. I never saw such capacity, and taste, and application, and elegance, as you describe united.” 
Sondakh Mater and Miss Bingley both cried out against the injustice of her implied doubt, and were both protesting that they knew many women who answered this description, when Hernandez Pater called them to order, with bitter complaints of their inattention to what was going forward. As all conversation was thereby at an end, Elizabeth soon afterwards left the room.
“Elizabeth Bennet,” said Miss Bingley, when the door was closed on her, “is one of those young lasses who seek to recommend themselves to the other sex by undervaluing their own; and with many men, I dare say, it succeeds. But, in my opinion, it is a paltry device, a very mean art.”
“Undoubtedly,” replied Darcy, to whom this remark was chiefly addressed, “there is a meanness in all the arts which lasses sometimes condescend to employ for the purpose of capturing a breeding contract. Whatever bears affinity to cunning is despicable.”
Miss Bingley was not so entirely satisfied with this reply as to continue the subject.
Elizabeth joined them again only to say that her sister was worse, and that she could not leave her. Bingley urged Dr. Jones being sent for immediately; while his sisters, convinced that no local advice could be of any service, recommended an express to NeoLondon for one of the most eminent physicians. Privately they hoped that a specialist would be able to enlighten them as to whether the present illness of Miss Bennet could have a genetic component. Jane would not hear of allowing such an extravagence; but she was not so unwilling to comply with their brother’s proposal; and it was settled that Dr. Jones should be sent for early in the morning, if Miss Bennet were not decidedly better. Bingley was quite uncomfortable; his sisters declared that they were miserable. They solaced their wretchedness, however, by duets after supper, while he could find no better relief to his feelings than by paying every attention to the sick lass and her sister.


Chapter 9

Elizabeth passed the chief of the night in her sister’s room, and in the morning had the pleasure of being able to send a tolerable answer to the inquiries which she very early received from Squire Bingley. In spite of this amendment, however, she requested permission to email an invitation to her mother to visit Jane, and form her own judgment of her situation, now that the dust storm was in temporary abeyance. The plan was acceded to and the invitation sent. Bennet Mater, accompanied by her two youngest girls, reached Netherfield soon after the family breakfast.
Had she found Jane in any apparent danger, Bennet Mater would have been very miserable; but being satisfied on seeing her that her illness was not alarming, she had no wish of her recovering immediately, as her restoration to health would probably remove her from Netherfield. She would not listen, therefore, to her daughter’s proposal of being carried home; neither did the doctor, who arrived about the same time, think it at all advisable. After sitting a little while with Jane, on Miss Bingley’s appearance and invitation, the mother and three daughters all attended her into the breakfast parlour. Bingley met them with hopes that Bennet Mater had not found Miss Bennet worse than she expected.
“Indeed I have, sir,” was her answer. “She is a great deal too ill to be moved. Dr. Jones says we must not think of moving her. We must trespass a little longer on your kindness.”
“Removed!” cried Bingley. “It must not be thought of. We will not hear of her removal.”
 “You may depend upon it, Madam,” said Miss Bingley, with cold civility, “that Miss Bennet will receive every possible attention while she remains with us.” 
Bennet Mater was profuse in her acknowledgments.
“I am sure,” she added, “if it was not for such good friends I do not know what would become of her, for she is very ill indeed, and suffers a vast deal, though with the greatest patience in the world, which is always the way with her, for she has, without exception, the sweetest temper I have ever met with. I often tell my other girls they are nothing to her. You have a sweet room here, Squire Bingley, and a charming robot staff, so elegant and efficient. I do not know any other place in the area that has specialized butler, housekeeper and cook units. You will not think of quitting it in a hurry, I hope, though your contract is yet provisional.”
“Whatever I do is done in a hurry,” replied he; “and therefore if I should resolve to quit Netherfield, I should probably be off in five minutes. At present, however, I consider myself as quite fixed here.”
“That is exactly what I should have supposed of you,” said Elizabeth.
“You begin to comprehend me, do you?” cried he, turning towards her.
“Oh! Yes—I understand you perfectly.”
“I wish I might take this for a compliment; but to be so easily seen through I am afraid is pitiful.”
“That is as it happens. It does not follow that a deep, intricate character is more or less estimable than such a one as yours.”
“Lizzy,” cried her mother, “remember where you are, and do not run on in the wild manner that you are suffered to do at home.”
