“I did not know before,” continued Bingley immediately, “that you were a studier of character. It must be an amusing study.”
“Yes, but intricate characters are the most amusing. They have at least that advantage.”
 “The outer colonies,” said Darcy, “can in general supply but a few subjects for such a study. In a remote neighbourhood you move in a very confined and unvarying society.”
 “But people themselves alter so much, that there is something new to be observed in them for ever.” 
“Yes, indeed,” cried Bennet Mater, offended by his manner of mentioning a remote neighbourhood. 
“I assure you there is quite as much of that going on in an outer colony as in the city.” Everybody was surprised, and Darcy, after looking at her for a moment, turned silently away.  Bennet Mater, who fancied she had gained a complete victory over him, continued her triumph.
“I cannot see that NeoLondon has any great advantage over the outer colonies, for my part, except the shops and public places. The air is a vast deal pleasanter, is it not, Squire Bingley without the sulpherous vapors and particulates of the city?”
“When I am in the country,” he replied, “I never wish to leave it; and when I am in town it is pretty much the same. They have each their advantages, and I can be equally happy in either.”
“Aye—that is because you have the right disposition. But that gentleman,” looking at Darcy, “seemed to think Mexistan Was nothing at all.”
“Indeed, Mamma, you are mistaken,” said Elizabeth, blushing for her mother. “You quite mistook Squire Darcy. He only meant that there was not such a variety of people to be met with in an outer colony as in the city, which you must acknowledge to be true.”
“Certainly, my dear, nobody said there were; but as to not meeting with many people in this neighbourhood, I believe there are few neighbourhoods larger. I know we dine with four-and-twenty families.”
Nothing but concern for Elizabeth could enable Bingley to keep his countenance. His sister was less delicate, and directed her eyes towards Squire Darcy with a very expressive smile. Elizabeth, for the sake of saying something that might turn her mother’s thoughts, now asked her if Charlotte Lucas had been at Liqua since her coming away.
“Yes, she called yesterday with her father. What an agreeable man Senator Lucas is, Squire Bingley, is not he? So much the man of fashion! So genteel and easy! He has always something to say to everybody. That is my idea of good breeding; and those persons who fancy themselves very important, and never open their mouths, quite mistake the matter.”
“Did Charlotte dine with you?”
“No, she would go home. I fancy she was wanted about the llama kebabs. For my part, Squire Bingley, any one of my daughters is quite competent in providing a meal without requiring the whole family to lend a hand. But everybody is to judge for themselves, and the Lucases are very good sort of lasses, I assure you. It is a pity they do not exude fecundity! Not that I think Charlotte so very scrawny—but then she is our particular friend.”
“She seems a very pleasant young woman.”
“Oh! Dear, yes; but you must own she is very scrawny. Lady Lucas herself has often said so, and envied me Jane’s fecundity. I do not like to boast of my own child, but to be sure, Jane—one does not often see anybody better designed for motherhood. It is what everybody says. I do not trust my own partiality. When she was only fifteen, there was a man at my brother Gardiner’s in town so much in taken with her that my brother’s breeding partner was sure he would make her an offer before we came away. But, however, he did not. Perhaps he thought her too young. He did get so far as to initiate an examination into their genetic compatibility, though, I understand.”
Her mother was innocently flattered that the man’s estimation of Jane had reached this advanced stage of consideration, but Elizabeth was mortified that Darcy might conclude that the man had found something wrong with Jane’s genetic constitution.
“And then he met another who suited him better,” said Elizabeth impatiently.
Darcy only smiled; and the general pause which ensued made Elizabeth tremble lest her mother should be exposing herself again. She longed to speak, but could think of nothing to say; and after a short silence Bennet Mater began repeating her thanks to Squire Bingley for his kindness to Jane, with an apology for troubling him also with Lizzy. Squire Bingley was unaffectedly civil in his answer, and forced his younger sister to be civil also, and say what the occasion required. She performed her part indeed without much graciousness, but Bennet Mater was satisfied, and soon afterwards spoke of fetching her llama cart. Upon this signal, the youngest of her daughters put herself forward. The two girls had been whispering to each other during the whole visit, and the result of it was, that the youngest should tax Squire Bingley with having promised on his first coming into their colony to give a ball at Netherfield.
Lydia was a stout, well-grown girl of fifteen, with a fine complexion and good-humoured countenance; a favourite with her mother, whose affection had brought her into public at an early age. She had high animal spirits, and a sort of natural self-consequence, which the attention of the astronauts had increased into assurance. She was very equal, therefore, to address Squire Bingley on the subject of the ball, and abruptly reminded him of his promise; adding, that it would be the most shameful thing in the world if he did not keep it. His answer to this sudden attack was delightful to their mother’s ear:
“I am perfectly ready, I assure you, to keep my engagement; and when your sister is recovered, you shall, if you please, name the very day of the ball. But you would not wish to be dancing when she is ill.”
Lydia declared herself satisfied. “Oh! Yes—it would be much better to wait till Jane was well, and by that time most likely Captain Carter would be at Mandalay again. And when you have given your ball,” she added, “I shall insist on their giving one also. I shall tell Colonel Forster it will be quite a shame if he does not.”
Bennet Mater and her daughters then departed, and Elizabeth returned instantly to Jane, leaving her own and her relations’ behaviour to the remarks of the two ladies and Squire Darcy; the latter of whom, however, could not be prevailed on to join in their censure of her, in spite of all Miss Bingley’s witticisms on fine eyes.


Chapter 10

The day passed much as the day before had done. Sondakh Mater and Miss Bingley had spent some hours of the morning with the invalid, who continued, though slowly, to mend; and in the evening Elizabeth joined their party in the drawing-room. The poker-table, however, did not appear. Squire Darcy was on his laptop, writing, and Miss Bingley, seated near him, was watching the progress of his letter and repeatedly calling off his attention by messages to his sister. Hernandez Pater and Squire Bingley were playing go, and Sondakh Mater was observing their game.
Elizabeth took up some needlework, and was sufficiently amused in attending to what passed between Darcy and his companion. The perpetual commendations of the lady, either on his computer, or on the facility of his typing, or on the length of his letter, with the perfect unconcern with which her praises were received, formed a curious dialogue, and was exactly in union with her opinion of each.
“How delighted Miss Darcy will be to receive such a letter!” He made no answer.
“You type uncommonly fast.”
“You are mistaken. I write rather slowly.”
“How many letters you must have occasion to write in the course of a year! Letters of business, too! How odious I should think them!”
“It is fortunate, then, that they fall to my lot instead of yours.” 
“Pray tell your sister that I long to see her.”
“I have already told her so once, by your desire.” 
“You have a very elegant laptop.” He was silent.
“Tell your sister I am delighted to hear of her improvement on the violin; and pray let her know that I am quite in raptures with her beautiful little design for a table, and I think it infinitely superior to Miss Grantley’s.”
“Will you give me leave to defer your raptures till I write again? At present I have not time to do them justice.”
“Oh! It is of no consequence. I shall see her in Primember. But do you always write such charming long letters to her, Squire Darcy?”
“They are generally long; but whether always charming it is not for me to determine.” 
“It is a rule with me, that a person who can write a long letter with ease, cannot write ill.” 
“That will not do for a compliment to Darcy, Caroline,” cried her brother, “because he does not write with ease. He studies too much for words of four syllables. Do not you, Darcy?” 
“My style of writing is very different from yours.” 
“Oh!” cried Miss Bingley, “Charles writes in the most careless way imaginable. He leaves out half his words, and misspells the rest.” 
“My ideas flow so rapidly that I have not time to express them—by which means my letters sometimes convey no ideas at all to my correspondents.” 
“Your humility, Squire Bingley,” said Elizabeth, “must disarm reproof.” 
“Nothing is more deceitful,” said Darcy, “than the appearance of humility. It is often only carelessness of opinion, and sometimes an indirect boast.” 
“And which of the two do you call my little recent piece of modesty?” 
“The indirect boast; for you are really proud of your defects in writing, because you consider them as proceeding from a rapidity of thought and carelessness of execution, which, if not estimable, you think at least highly interesting. You are far too concerned with capturing the imagination of your intimate social circle and rather too little for matters of greater objective significance.” 
“And what, pray, are these significant mattters that my vanity leads me to neglect?” said Bingley, with more curiousity than heat.
“Such things as your duties as a mineowner, the well-being of your fellow man, and the smooth functioning of the Colony.”
“I must be vain indeed to be neglectful of so much!” retorted Bingley with an injured tone.
“On the whole, I would say that you take your duties with adequate seriousness,” Darcy replied. “But when temptation calls, you are over apt to be impetuous and even thoughtless. When you told Bennet Mater this morning that if you ever resolved upon quitting Netherfield you should be gone in five minutes, you meant it to be a sort of panegyric, of compliment to yourself—and yet what is there so very laudable in a precipitousness which must leave very necessary business undone, and can be of no real advantage to yourself or anyone else?”
“Nay,” cried Bingley, “this is too much, to remember at night all the foolish things that were said in the morning. And yet, upon my honour, I believe what I said of myself to be true, and I believe it at this moment. At least, therefore, I did not assume the character of needless precipitousness merely to show off before the ladies.”
“I dare say you believed it; but I am by no means convinced that you would be gone with such celerity, even when convinced of the merit of your journey. Your conduct would be quite as dependent on whim as that of any man I know; and if, as you were entering your ferflivver to travel to a conference on mine management, a friend were to say, ‘Bingley, you had better stay till next week,’ you would probably not go—and at another word, might stay a month.”
“You have only proved by this,” cried Elizabeth, “that Squire Bingley has an affectionate heart and that he prizes the needs of his friends over the demands of business.”
“I am exceedingly gratified,” said Bingley, “by your converting what my friend says into a compliment on the sweetness of my temper. But I am afraid you are giving it a turn which that gentleman did by no means intend; for he would certainly think better of me, if under such a circumstance I were to give a flat denial, and fly off as fast as I could.”
“Yes,” said Darcy “If you were once of the belief that attending this conference would make you a better manager, thereby benefiting your workers, their families, this town and, by extention, the success of this Colony, then I do think you should maintain your intended course, even in the face of a friend’s importunity.”
“I believe you wrong me here, Darcy” said Bingley, thoughtfully, “If I were convinced of the value of the conference, and yet wished to accede to my friend’s desires, I would commission my under-manager to go in my stead. I am sure that I am not the only man in my mine who could benefit from further industrial education.”
“Well spoken,” returned Elizabeth. “The reasonableness of your solution is a testament to both your modesty and your ability to compromise. Modesty and Compromise, I venture, are concepts with which Darcy has had little acquaintance.”
Squire Darcy smiled; but Elizabeth thought she could perceive that he was rather offended, and therefore checked her laugh.
Miss Bingley warmly resented the indignity he had received and called for an end to the argument.
“What you ask,” said Elizabeth, “is no sacrifice on my side; and Squire Darcy had much better finish his letter.”
Squire Darcy took her advice, and did finish his letter.
When that business was over, he applied to Miss Bingley and Elizabeth for an indulgence of some music. Miss Bingley moved with some alacrity to the positronic organ; and, after a polite request that Elizabeth would lead the way which the other as politely and more earnestly negatived, she seated herself.
Sondakh Mater sang with her sister, and while they were thus employed, Elizabeth could not help observing, as she turned over some music-books that lay on the instrument, how frequently Squire Darcy’s eyes were fixed on her. She hardly knew how to suppose that she could be an object of consideration to so great a man; and yet that he should look at her because he disliked her, was still more strange. She could only imagine, however, at last that she drew his notice because there was something more wrong and reprehensible, according to his ideas of right, than in any other person present. The supposition did not pain her. She liked him too little to care for his approbation.
After playing some Italian songs, Miss Bingley varied the charm with a lively Martian air; and soon afterwards Squire Darcy, drawing near Elizabeth, said to her:
“Do not you feel a great inclination, Miss Bennet, to seize such an opportunity of dancing a reel?”
She smiled, but made no answer. He repeated the question, with some surprise at her silence.
“Oh!” said she, “I heard you before, but I could not immediately determine what to say in reply. You wanted me, I know, to say ‘Yes,’ that you might have the pleasure of despising my taste; but I always delight in overthrowing those kind of schemes, and cheating a person of their premeditated contempt. I have, therefore, made up my mind to tell you, that I do not want to dance a reel at all—and now despise me if you dare.”
“Indeed I do not dare.”
Elizabeth, having rather expected to affront him, was amazed at his gallantry; but there was a mixture of sweetness and archness in her manner which made it difficult for her to affront anybody; and Darcy had never been so bewitched by any lass as he was by her. He feared, that were it not for his conscious subjection to the social mores of Titan, he should be in some danger of developing a scandalous attraction to an innocent girl, a lass who has not even began to breed.
Miss Bingley saw or suspected enough to believe that Darcy was considerating Elizabeth’s potential as a co-parent; and her great anxiety for the recovery of her dear friend Jane received some assistance from her desire of getting rid of Elizabeth.
She often tried to provoke Darcy into disliking her guest, by talking of their supposed breeding contract, and planning his happiness in such an alliance.
“I hope,” said she, as they were walking together the next day in the glade of elephant trees that graced the grounds of Netherfield, “that your children will not take after your partner’s mother, when this desirable event takes place, and do not inherit Jane’s ill-health, or Charlotte’s cough, and miraculously avoid all of the other imperfections that that haunt that unfortunate family.”
“Have you anything else to propose for my paternal felicity?” 
“Oh! Yes! Do try to find out why the happy family consists solely of girls. Very odd, is it not?” 
The oddness of this circumstance had not escaped Darcy’s attention, but, as there was no possibility of his condescending to breed with Elizabeth, he paid no attention to it. Even the inappropriate inclinations that occasionally wormed their way into his consciousness were romantic and not co-parental. 
“They are, however, all remarkably nubile and fecund lasses,” he replied.
At that moment they were met from another walk by Sondakh Mater and Elizabeth herself.
“I did not know that you intended to walk,” said Miss Bingley, in some confusion, lest they had been overheard.
“You used us abominably ill,” answered Sondakh Mater, “running away without telling us that you were coming out.”
Then taking the disengaged arm of Squire Darcy, she left Elizabeth to walk by herself. The path just admitted three. Squire Darcy felt their rudeness, and immediately said:
“This walk is not wide enough for our party. We had better go into the avenue.” 
But Elizabeth, who had not the least inclination to remain with them, laughingly answered: “No, no; stay where you are. You are charmingly grouped, and appear to uncommon advantage. The picturesque? would be spoilt by admitting a fourth. Good-bye.” 
She then ran gaily off, rejoicing as she rambled about, in the hope of being at home again in a day or two. Jane was already so much recovered as to intend leaving her room for a couple of hours that evening.

Chapter 11

When the ladies removed after dinner, Elizabeth ran up to her sister, and after ensuring that her filtration headscarf protected her throat and lungs from dust, attended her into the drawing-room, where she was welcomed by her two friends with many professions of pleasure; Elizabeth had never seen them so agreeable as they were during the hour which passed before the gentlemen appeared. Their powers of conversation were considerable. They could describe an entertainment with accuracy, relate an anecdote with humour, and laugh at their acquaintance with spirit.
But when the gentlemen entered, Jane was no longer the first object; Miss Bingley’s eyes were instantly turned toward Darcy, and she had something to say to him before he had advanced many steps. He addressed himself to Miss Bennet, with a polite congratulation; Hernandez Pater also made her a slight bow, and said he was “very glad”; but diffuseness and warmth remained for Bingley’s salutation. He was full of joy and attention. The first half-hour was spent in inspecting the sealant along the edges of the windows, lest any tiny flaws should allow dust or sand into the room; and she removed at his desire to the other side of the room, that she might be further from the windows. He then sat down by her, and talked scarcely to anyone else. Elizabeth, at work in the opposite corner, saw it all with great delight.
When tea was over, Hernandez Pater reminded his paramour’s niece of the card-table—but in vain. She had obtained private intelligence that Squire Darcy did not wish for cards; and Hernandez Pater soon found even his open petition rejected. She assured him that no one intended to play, and the silence of the whole party on the subject seemed to justify her. Hernandez Pater had therefore nothing better to do than to invite Sondakh Mater upstairs for a little early evening dalliance. Miss Bingley watched them leave with a sigh, wondering if co-parental relations would bear any resemblance to paramour love, or whether she would have to wait nearly two decades for such delights.
Miss Bingley turned her attention to an admiration of Darcy’s progress through his book; and she was perpetually either making some inquiry, or looking at his page. She could not win him, however, to any conversation; he merely answered her question, and read on. At length, quite exhausted by the attempt to be amused with a book of her own, which she had only chosen because it was the second volume of his, she gave a great yawn and said, “How pleasant it is to spend an evening in this way! I declare after all there is no enjoyment like reading! How much sooner one tires of anything than of a book! When I am ensconced in a house of my own, I shall be miserable if I have not an excellent library.”
No one made any reply. She then yawned again, threw aside her book, and cast her eyes round the room in quest for some amusement; when hearing her brother mentioning a ball to Miss Bennet, she turned suddenly towards him and said:
“By the bye, Charles, are you really serious in meditating a dance at Netherfield? I would advise you, before you determine on it, to consult the wishes of the present party; I am much mistaken if there are not some among us to whom a ball would be rather a punishment than a pleasure.”
“If you mean Darcy,” cried her brother, “he may go to bed, if he chooses, before it begins—but as for the ball, it is quite a settled thing.”
“I should like balls infinitely better,” she replied, “if they were carried on in a different manner; but there is something insufferably tedious in the usual process of such a meeting. It would surely be much more rational if conversation instead of dancing were made the order of the day. After all, intelligence and sensibility are more important qualities to look for in a breeding partner than physical grace.”
“Much more rational, my dear Caroline, I dare say, but it would not be near so much like a ball. Besides, even the best sets of genes require a healthy body to realize their transmission to the next generation.”
Miss Bingley made no answer, and soon afterwards she got up and walked about the room. Her figure was elegant, and she walked well; but Darcy, at whom it was all aimed, was still inflexibly studious. In the desperation of her feelings, she resolved on one effort more, and, turning to Elizabeth, said:
“Miss Eliza Bennet, let me persuade you to follow my example, and take a turn about the room. I assure you it is very refreshing after sitting so long in one attitude.”
Elizabeth was surprised, but agreed to it immediately. Miss Bingley succeeded no less in the real object of her civility; Squire Darcy looked up. He was as much awake to the novelty of attention in that quarter as Elizabeth herself could be, and unconsciously closed his book. He was directly invited to join their party, but he declined it, observing that he could imagine but two motives for their choosing to walk up and down the room together, with either of which motives his joining them would interfere. “What could he mean? She was dying to know what could be his meaning?”—and asked Elizabeth whether she could at all understand him?
“Not at all,” was her answer; “but depend upon it, he means to be severe on us, and our surest way of disappointing him will be to ask nothing about it.”
Miss Bingley, however, was incapable of disappointing Squire Darcy in anything, and persevered therefore in requiring an explanation of his two motives.
“I have not the smallest objection to explaining them,” said he, as soon as she allowed him to speak. “You either choose this method of passing the evening because you are in each other’s confidence, and have secret affairs to discuss, or because you are conscious that your figures appear to the greatest advantage in walking; if the first, I would be completely in your way, and if the second, I can assess you much better as I sit by the window.”
“We have nothing to gain by displaying our well-shaped figures to you,” replied Elizabeth. “We all know that you have higher standards for the mother of your future children.”
“I have high standards, yes,” said Darcy. “It would be neglectful of me not to want the best for my children. Especially when I am fortunate enough to be in a position to provide the best.”
Miss Bingley looked dismayed.
“But I am sure that both of you will make excellent mothers,” he concluded with an belated attempt at gallantry.
“Now that you have reminded us of your very privileged position, Squire Darcy, I would like to ask your opinion on a political matter,” said Elizabeth.
“Yes?”
“Do you think it is time for Titan to become a democracy?” 
Darcy considered the question thoughtfully. “Democracy is a beautiful concept,” he said judiciously, “but the realities of life on Titan require a technocracy. The brightest and best educated minds on this planet are already fully occupied in tending to the challenges of making an alien environment hospitable to human life. We would be completely undone if the uneducated masses to were elect a charismatic dunce to direct our operations.” 
“Then education, surely, is the key.”
“Ultimately, yes,” Darcy replied. “Ultimately we would like a genetically healthy, intelligent and well-educated populace that could be trusted with deciding the fate of Titan. But at the moment, we don’t have the resources to provide the necessary education to everyone. We need men to work in the mines and women to raise children.”
“But don’t you think that if women were to devote more of their energy to educating themselves, they would be better able to educate their children, thereby making each successive generation of Titan closer to that ideal that you envision?”
Darcy looked surprised.
“I do think that,” he said. “That is why I think the accomplishments of a good mother should extend far beyond useful skills such as furniture design and human biology, to include an understanding of history, literature, philosophy and science.”
“So you don’t think that women’s accomplishments are solely valuable as a means to display superior genes?”
“No,  in fact, I don’t. That they effectively display superior genes is indisputable, but women also have a role to play in making our society better.”
“Spoken like a true politician.”
“You mistake me. I am not a politician. Due to our freedom from the democratic process here in Titan, we who are entrusted to manage society do not have to be politicians. We may concentrate on being technocrats.”
“And why can’t women be technocrats? At least after they have fulfillled their child-raising duties?”
Darcy smiled. “I am sure that some women could make very competent technocrats,” he said, regarding Elizabeth with warm respect, “Unfortunately for those of your sex with such ambitions, men have the power, and those with power rarely give it up voluntarily.”
“You mean some men have the power. And that privileged few is unlikely to relinquish it.”
“Exactly.”
“Do let us have a little music,” cried Miss Bingley, tired of a conversation in which she had no share. “Louisa,” she called to her aunt who had just come down the stairs looking quite invigorated, “you will not mind playing for us?”
Sondakh Mater had not the smallest objection, and the positronic organ was opened; and Darcy, after a few moments’ recollection, was not sorry for it. He began to feel the danger of paying Elizabeth too much attention.


Chapter 12

In consequence of an agreement between the sisters, Elizabeth wrote the next morning to their mother, to beg that the ferflivver might be sent for them in the course of the day. But Bennet Mater, who had calculated on her daughters remaining at Netherfield till the following Zeusday, which would exactly finish Jane’s week, could not bring herself to receive them with pleasure before then. Her answer, therefore, was not propitious, at least not to Elizabeth’s wishes, for she was impatient to get home. Bennet Mater sent them word that they could not possibly have the ferflivver before Zeusday, as her father had taken it for a few days to visit his occasional paramour; and in her postscript it was added, that if Squire Bingley and his sister pressed them to stay longer, she could spare them very well. Against staying longer, however, Elizabeth was positively resolved—nor did she much expect it would be asked; and fearful, on the contrary, as being considered as intruding themselves needlessly long, she urged Jane to borrow Squire Bingley’s ferflivver immediately, and at length it was settled that their original design of leaving Netherfield that morning should be mentioned, and the request made.
The communication excited many professions of concern; and enough was said of wishing them to stay at least till the following day to work on Jane; and till the morrow their going was deferred. Miss Bingley was then sorry that she had proposed the delay, for her jealousy and dislike of one sister much exceeded her affection for the other.
The master of the house heard with real sorrow that they were to go so soon, and repeatedly tried to persuade Miss Bennet that it would not be safe for her—that she was not enough recovered; but Jane was firm where she felt herself to be right.
To Squire Darcy it was welcome intelligence—Elizabeth had been at Netherfield long enough. She attracted him more than he liked—and he was much afraid that Miss Bingley might begin to suspect the inappropriate nature of his feelings, which had nothing to do with the rational evaluation of a potential breeding partner. He wisely resolved to be particularly careful that no sign of romantic inclination should now escape him, and equally that nothing could inspire Elizabeth to believe that he may be reconsidering her qualifications as a co-parent; sensible that if such an idea had been suggested, his behaviour during the last day must have material weight in confirming or crushing it. Steady to his purpose, he scarcely spoke ten words to her through the whole of Saturnsday, and though they were at one time left by themselves for half-an-hour, he adhered most conscientiously to his book, and would not even look at her.
On Sunday, after morning service, the separation, so agreeable to almost all, took place. Miss Bingley’s civility to Elizabeth increased at last very rapidly, as well as her affection for Jane; and when they parted, after assuring the latter of the pleasure it would always give her to see her either at Liqua or Netherfield, and embracing her most tenderly, she even shook hands with the former. Elizabeth took leave of the whole party in the liveliest of spirits.
They were not welcomed home very cordially by their mother. Bennet Mater wondered at their coming, and thought them very wrong to give so much trouble, and was sure Jane would suffer a relapse of her respiratory ailment. But their father, though very laconic in his expressions of pleasure, was really glad to see them; he had felt their importance in the family circle. The evening conversation, when they were all assembled, had lost much of its animation, and almost all its sense, by the absence of Jane and Elizabeth.
They found Mary, as usual, deep in the study of animal and human nature; and had some extracts to admire, and some new observations to listen to, concerning the mating displays of peacocks and the courtship dances of jumping spiders.
“Isn’t it odd,” remarked Kitty, “that in the animal kingdom it is the males that display, but among people is it the lasses that need to strut their stuff?”
“Oh, that is not odd at all,” Mary said condescendingly. “In species in which the female is capable of caring for the young by herself, female choice dictates mate selection. She has the power and can afford to choose only the sexiest mates. But in human society (or at least in our society), the female depends on the male to provide for the young, and even for herself. In that case, the man can be choosy, but the woman must be satisfied with any man that can offer her a living.”
“A woman can still choose among suitable candidates, though,” objected Elizabeth.
“Yes,” said Mary, reluctantly. “If she is attractive enough as a breeding partner to have a selection of offers to choose from,” and she sighed, as she thought darkly of her own prospects in that regard.
Lydia had information for them of a different sort. Much had been done and much had been said at the aeronautical base since the preceding Watersday; several of the astronauts had dined lately with their uncle, one astronaut had been killed in an exploratory mission, and it had actually been hinted that Colonel Forster was going to choose a breeding partner.


Chapter 13

“I hope, my dear,” said Bennet Pater to his co-parent, as they were at breakfast the next morning, “that you are planning a good dinner today, because I have reason to expect an addition to our family party.”
“Who do you mean, my dear? I know of nobody that is coming, I am sure, unless Charlotte Lucas should happen to call in—and I hope my dinners are good enough for her. I do not believe she often sees such at home.”
“The person of whom I speak is a gentleman, and a stranger.” 
Bennet Mater’s eyes sparkled. “A gentleman and a stranger! It is Squire Bingley, I am sure! Well, I am sure I shall be extremely glad to see Squire Bingley. But—good Lord! How unlucky! There is not a bit of ostrich to be got to-day. Lydia, my love, run out and see how the baby llamas are faring. Perhaps we could spare one.” 
“It is not Squire Bingley,” said her co-parent; “it is a person whom I never saw in the whole course of my life.” 
This roused a general astonishment; and he had the pleasure of being eagerly questioned by his co-parent and his five daughters at once.
After amusing himself some time with their curiosity, he thus explained: “About a month ago I received this email, which I have printed out for your amusement; and about a fortnight ago I answered it, for I thought it a case of some delicacy, and requiring early attention. It is from the pastor of Hanoi, Reverend Collins, who, when I am dead, may turn you all out of this house as soon as he pleases.” 
“Oh! My dear,” cried his co-parent, “I cannot bear to hear that mentioned. Pray do not talk of that odious man. I do think it is the hardest thing in the world, that your estate should be taken away from your own children and granted to the church! And just because we have no sons! How could we be so unfortunate as to have had only daughters!” 
Bennett Pater listened patiently. He was aware that she knew as well as himself why they had never been able to have sons, but he also understood that she preferred to think of it  as a matter of inexplicable bad luck.
“It certainly is most unfortunate,” said Bennet Pater, “and nothing can clear Reverend Collins from the guilt of being the benefactor of his church’s good fortune. But if you will listen to his letter, you may perhaps be a little softened by his manner of expressing himself.”
“No, that I am sure I shall not; and I think it is very impertinent of him to write to you at all, and very hypocritical. I don’t know how he can bear to speak to the family that he is expecting to dispossess.”
“Why, indeed; he does seem to have had some scruples on that head, as you will hear.” “Hanoi, near Wellington, Darwin, 15th Octember.
“Dear Sir,—
“The position of the law forbidding ownership of property by women has always given me much uneasiness, particularly in a case where there are no sons to inherit. I have frequently spoken out against this policy, but to no avail. Of course, my position of pastor in the Holy Eugenical Church does not allow me the liberty of choosing where I will live, and when the time comes, I am afraid I will be forced to take occupancy of Liqua.—’There, Bennet Mater.’—My fate is now determined on the subject, for having received ordination at the Festival of Fertility, I have been so fortunate as to be distinguished by the patronage of the Right Honourable Lady Catherine de Bourgh, formerly Sir Lewis de Bourgh, whose bounty and beneficence has preferred me to the valuable rectory of this parish, where it shall be my earnest endeavour to demean myself with grateful respect towards her ladyship, and be ever ready to perform those rites and ceremonies which are instituted by the Holy Eugenical Church. As a clergyman, moreover, I feel it my duty to promote and establish the blessing of peace in all families within the reach of my influence; and on these grounds I flatter myself that my present overtures are highly commendable, and that the circumstance of my being next in the occupancy of the Liqua estate will be kindly overlooked on your side, and not lead you to reject the offered olive-branch. I cannot be otherwise than concerned at being the means of injuring your amiable daughters, and beg leave to apologise for it, as well as to assure you of my readiness to make them every possible amends—but of this hereafter. If you should have no objection to receive me into your house, I propose myself the satisfaction of waiting on you and your family, Moonday, Nonember 18th, by four o’clock, and shall probably trespass on your hospitality till the next Saturnsday night, which I can do without any inconvenience, as Lady Catherine is far from objecting to my occasional absence on a Sunday, provided that some other clergyman is engaged to do the duty of the day.—I remain, dear sir, with respectful compliments to your co-parent and daughters, your well-wisher and friend,
“WINONO COLLINS”
“At four o’clock, therefore, we may expect this peace-making gentleman,” said Bennet Pater, as he folded up the letter. “He seems to be a most conscientious and polite young man, upon my word, and I doubt not will prove a valuable acquaintance, especially if Lady Catherine should be so indulgent as to let him come to us again.”
“There is some sense in what he says about the girls, however, and if he is disposed to make them any amends, I shall not be the person to discourage him.”
“Though it is difficult,” said Jane, “to guess in what way he can mean to make us the atonement he thinks our due, the wish is certainly to his credit.”
Elizabeth was chiefly struck by his extraordinary deference for Lady Catherine, and his kind intention of baptising and burying his parishioners whenever it were required.
“He must be an oddity, I think,” said she. “I cannot make him out.—There is something very pompous in his style.—And what can he mean by apologising for being next in the occupancy?—We cannot suppose he would help it if he could.—Could he be a sensible man, sir?”
“No, my dear, I think not. I have great hopes of finding him quite the reverse. There is a mixture of servility and self-importance in his letter, which promises well. I am impatient to see him.”
“In point of composition,” said Mary, “the letter does not seem defective. And his reservations regarding the equity of gender-based inheritance laws I think are well expressed.”
To Catherine and Lydia, neither the letter nor its writer were in any degree interesting. It was next to impossible that the reverend should be as dashing as an astronaut, and it was now some weeks since they had received pleasure from the society of a man in any profession. As for their mother, Reverend Collins’s letter had done away much of her ill-will, and she was preparing to see him with a degree of composure which astonished her co-parent and daughters.
Reverend Collins was punctual to his time, and was received with great politeness by the whole family. Bennet Pater indeed said little; but the ladies were ready enough to talk, and Reverend Collins seemed neither in need of encouragement, nor inclined to be silent himself. He was a tall, heavy-looking young man of five-and-thirty. His air was grave and stately, and his manners were very formal. He had not been long seated before he complimented Bennet Mater on having so fine a family of daughters; said he had heard much of their fecundity, but that in this instance fame had fallen short of the truth; and added, that he did not doubt her seeing them all in due time disposed of in breeding contracts. This gallantry was not much to the taste of some of his hearers; but Bennet Mater, who quarreled with no compliments, answered most readily.
“You are very kind, I am sure; and I wish with all my heart it may prove so, for else they will be in a desperate situation. Things are settled so oddly.”
“The law may appear draconian on this point, but I hope you appreciate its necessity. Titan is a struggling colony and we just cannot afford to support members of our community that do not contribute to population growth.”
“Ah! Sir, I do indeed appreciate that. But it is a grievous affair to my poor girls, you must confess. Not that I mean to find fault with you, for such things. I know that pastors have little influence with the law-makers.”
“Indeed we do not, madam. We have in fact our own concessions to make. There was a time when our church required its clergy to remain celibate, but we have since come to see that it is more important for us to set a good example in the question of breeding, than to remain aloof and avoid the inevitable emotional ties that result from producing offspring. We are, after all, the Holy Eugenical Church, and thus we can hardly be unaware of the importance of maximizing genetic diversity in Titan. We cannot afford to squander even one poor pastor’s genetic resources. Now, I am very sensible, madam, of the hardship to your fair daughters, and could say much on the subject, but that I am cautious of appearing forward and precipitate. But I can assure the young lasses that I come prepared to admire their nubility. At present I will not say more; but, perhaps, when we are better acquainted—”
He was interrupted by a summons to dinner; and the girls smiled on each other. They were not the only objects of Reverend Collins’s admiration. The hall, the dining-room, and all its furniture, were examined and praised; and his commendation of everything would have touched Bennet Mater’s heart, but for the mortifying supposition of his viewing it all as his own future property. He admired too the natural ease and apparent affection between Bennet Pater and Bennet Mater and hazarded a conjecture that they were so complementary to each other that they had no need of paramours. But he was set right there by Bennet Mater, who assured him with some asperity that they were both very well able to attract and entertain lovers. He begged pardon for having displeased her. In a softened tone she declared herself not at all offended; but he continued to apologise for about a quarter of an hour.


Chapter 14

During dinner, Bennet Pater scarcely spoke at all; but when the women had finished clearing up, he thought it time to have some conversation with his guest, and therefore started a subject in which he expected him to shine, by observing that he seemed very fortunate in his patroness. Lady Catherine de Bourgh’s attention to his wishes, and consideration for his comfort, appeared very remarkable. Bennet Pater could not have chosen better. Reverend Collins was eloquent in her praise. The subject elevated him to more than usual solemnity of manner, and with a most important aspect he protested that he had never in his life witnessed such behaviour in a person of status—such affability and condescension, as he had himself experienced from Lady Catherine. She had been graciously pleased to approve of both of the discourses which he had already had the honour of preaching before her. She had also asked him twice to dine at Rosings, and had sent for him only the Saturnsday before, to make up her table of mahjong in the evening. Lady Catherine was reckoned proud by many people he knew, but he had never seen anything but affability in her. She had always spoken to him as she would to any other gentleman; she made not the smallest objection to his joining in the society of the neighbourhood nor to his leaving the parish occasionally for a week or two, to visit his future home. She had even condescended to advise him to find a breeding partner as soon as he could, provided he chose with discretion; and had once paid him a visit in his humble parsonage, where she had perfectly approved all the alterations he had been making, and had even vouchsafed to suggest some herself—a humidifying vent in the sitting room.
“That is all very proper and civil, I am sure,” said Bennet Mater, “and I dare say she is a very agreeable woman. It is a pity that there are not more great ladies like her. Does she live near you, sir?”
“The grounds in which stands my humble abode is separated only by a lane from Rosings Park, her ladyship’s residence.”
“How is it that a woman is able to be mistress of such an estate?” asked Bennet Mater.
“I am surprised that you not familiar with her history, madam,” replied Squire Collins. “Lady Catherine is, if I may put it delicately, anatomically and legally male. She was formerly known as Sir Lewis de Bourgh but adopted her present style some twenty years ago.”
“I see. Has she any family?”
“She had a breeding contract with a young lass in her youth, but most distressingly proved to be quite sterile. The breeding contract was, of course, dissolved, and she never had children of her own. As it happened, however, a good friend of hers died in childbirth, shortly after her co-parent had met his demise in a mining accident, and the pair left behind two young children. Lady Catherine had the good fortune to adopt them, the boy becoming the heir of Rosings and the girl living under the protection of her brother and her poweful new mother.
“Ah!” said Bennet Mater, shaking her head, “then she is better off than many girls. And what sort of young lass is she? Is she handsome?”
“She is a most charming young lass indeed. Lady Catherine herself says that, in point of true beauty, Miss de Bourgh is far superior to the handsomest of her sex, because there is that in her features which marks the young lass of distinguished birth. She is unfortunately of a sickly constitution and does not exude fertility. In  another lass, this fact might portend difficulty in securing a breeding contract, but with the benefits that would accrue through a family connection with Lady Catherine de Bourgh, some slight imperfections in genetic quality can be easily overlooked by potential breeding partners. The result of genetic reassortment is so difficult to predict at the best of times, after all, and ‘many a stout lad has been bred from a scrawny woman’, as my uncle used to say.”
“Is she on the market? I do not remember her name in the roll of lasses attending the recent Fertility Ball in NeoLondon.”
“Her indifferent state of health unhappily prevents her attending many large events; and by that means, as I told Lady Catherine one day, has deprived the high society of Titan of its brightest ornament. Her ladyship seemed pleased with the idea; and you may imagine that I am happy on every occasion to offer those little delicate compliments which are always acceptable to ladies. I have more than once observed to Lady Catherine, that her charming daughter seemed born to be a Martian princess, and that such an elevated rank, instead of giving her consequence, would have been adorned by her. These are the kind of little things which please her ladyship, and it is a sort of attention which I conceive myself peculiarly bound to pay.”
“You judge very properly,” said Bennet Pater, “and it is happy for you that you possess the talent of flattering with delicacy. May I ask whether these pleasing attentions proceed from the impulse of the moment, or are the result of previous study?”
“They arise chiefly from what is passing at the time, and though I sometimes amuse myself with suggesting and arranging such little elegant compliments as may be adapted to ordinary occasions, I always wish to give them as unstudied an air as possible.”
Bennet Pater’s expectations were fully answered. The reverend was as absurd as he had hoped, and he listened to him with the keenest enjoyment, maintaining at the same time the most resolute composure of countenance, and, except in an occasional glance at Elizabeth, requiring no partner in his pleasure.
By tea-time, however, the dose had been enough, and Bennet Pater was glad to take his guest into the drawing-room again, and, when tea was over, glad to invite him to read aloud to the ladies. Reverend Collins readily assented, and a book was produced; but, on beholding it, he started back, and begging pardon, protested that he never read novels. Kitty stared at him, and Lydia exclaimed. Other books were produced, and after some deliberation he chose Fordyce’s Sermons on Eugenics and Good Breeding. Lydia gaped as he opened the volume, and before he had, with very monotonous solemnity, read three pages, she interrupted him with:
“Do you know, mamma, that my uncle Phillips has quite fallen in love with the co-parent of one of the generals. My aunt told me so herself on Saturnsday. I shall walk to Mandalay tomorrow to hear more about it, and to ask when Squire Denny comes back from town.”
Lydia was bid by her two eldest sisters to hold her tongue; but Reverend Collins, much offended, laid aside his book, and said:
“I have often observed how little young lasses are interested by books of a serious stamp, though written solely for their benefit. It amazes me, I confess; for, certainly, there can be nothing so advantageous to them as instruction in the art of breeding. But I will no longer importune my young acquaintance.”
Then turning to Bennet Pater, he offered himself as his antagonist at backgammon. Bennet Pater accepted the challenge, observing that he acted very wisely in leaving the girls to their own trifling amusements. Bennet Mater and her daughters apologised most civilly for Lydia’s interruption, and promised that it should not occur again, if he would resume his book; but Reverend Collins, after assuring them that he bore his young acquaintance no ill-will, and should never resent her behaviour as any affront, seated himself at another table with Bennet Pater, and prepared for backgammon.


Chapter 15

Reverend Collins was not a sensible man, and the deficiency of nature had been but little assisted by education or society. Although, in order to quality for his profession, he had studied religion, pastoral care and eugenics, he had only learnt sufficient to pass his examinations and had never developed any true understanding. His father had been uneducated and evil-tempered. Collins senior been a tunnel laborer in a copper mine until his early death in a mining accident, succumbing to an all too common fate among the workers of Titan. The early subjection in which his father had brought him up had given him originally great humility of manner; but having risen in the world by dint of his own application, that initial humility was now a good deal counteracted by the self-conceit of a weak head and the consequential feelings of early and unexpected prosperity. A fortunate chance had recommended him to Lady Catherine de Bourgh when the living of Hanoi was vacant; and the respect which he felt for her high status, and his veneration for her as his patroness, mingling with a very good opinion of himself, of his authority as a clergyman, and his right as a rector, made him altogether a mixture of pride and obsequiousness, self-importance and humility.
Having now a good house and a very sufficient income, he needed to find a lass with whom to breed; and in seeking an acquaintance with the Liqua family he had a breeding partner in view, as he meant to choose one of the daughters, if he found them as fecund and amiable as they were represented by common report. This was his plan of amends—of atonement—for being granted their father’s estate upon his death; and he thought it an excellent one, full of eligibility and suitableness, and excessively generous and disinterested on his own part.
His plan did not vary on seeing them. Miss Bennet’s motherly figure confirmed his views, and established all his strictest notions of what was due to seniority; and for the first evening she was his settled choice. The next morning, however, made an alteration; for in a quarter of an hour’s tete-a-tete with Bennet Mater before breakfast, a conversation about his parsonage-house, and leading naturally to the avowal of his hopes, that he might find a breeding partner at Liqua, produced from her, amid very complaisant smiles and general encouragement, a caution against the very Jane he had fixed on. “As to her younger daughters, she could not take it upon her to say—she could not positively answer—but she did not know of any prepossession; her eldest daughter, she must just mention—she felt it incumbent on her to hint, was likely to be very soon to be offered a contract.”
Reverend Collins had only to change from Jane to Elizabeth—and it was soon done—while Bennet Mater was preparing lunch. Elizabeth, equally next to Jane in birth and nubility, succeeded her of course.
Bennet Mater treasured up the hint, and trusted that she might soon have two daughters with settled contracts; and the man whom she could not bear to speak of the day before was now high in her good graces.
Lydia’s intention of riding to Mandalay was not forgotten, and as the sky was unseasonably clear, it was an attractive plan; every sister except Mary agreed to go with her; and Reverend Collins was to attend them, at the request of Bennet Pater, who was most anxious to get rid of him, and have his den to himself; for thither Reverend Collins had followed him after breakfast; his civility, therefore, was most prompt in inviting Reverend Collins to join his daughters in their ride; and Reverend Collins, being in fact much better fitted for a rider than a reader, was extremely pleased to go.
In pompous nothings on his side, and civil assents on that of the Bennet sisters, their time passed till they entered Mandalay. They tied up their llamas, took off their riding robes and strolled down the street in their gay sarongs. The attention of the younger ones was then no longer to be gained by Reverend Collins. Their eyes were immediately wandering up and down in quest of the astronauts, and nothing less than a beautiful silken sash, or a jewel-encrusted water flask in a shop window, could recall them.
But the attention of every lady was soon caught by a young man, whom they had never seen before, of most gentlemanlike appearance, walking with another astronaut on the other side of the way. The astronaut was the very Squire Denny concerning whose return from NeoLondon Lydia came to inquire, and he bowed as they passed. All were struck with the stranger’s air, all wondered who he could be; and Kitty and Lydia, determined if possible to find out, led the way across the street, under pretense of wanting something in an opposite shop, and fortunately had just gained the pavement when the two gentlemen, turning back, had reached the same spot. Squire Denny addressed them directly, and entreated permission to introduce his friend, Squire Wickham, who had returned with him the day before from NeoLondon, and he was happy to say had accepted a commission in the Aeronautics Corps. This was exactly as it should be; for the young man wanted only a high ranking position to make him completely charming. His appearance was greatly in his favour; he had all the best part of beauty, a fine countenance, a good figure, an aura of prosperity and very pleasing address. The introduction was followed up on his side by a happy readiness of conversation—a readiness at the same time perfectly correct and unassuming; and the whole party were still standing and talking together very agreeably, when the sound of camels drew their notice, and Darcy and Bingley were seen riding down the street. On distinguishing the ladies of the group, the two gentlemen came directly towards them, and began the usual civilities. Bingley was the principal spokesman, and Miss Bennet the principal object. He was then, he said, on his way to Liqua on purpose to inquire after her. Squire Darcy corroborated it with a bow of his head, and was beginning to determine not to fix his eyes on Elizabeth, when they were suddenly arrested by the sight of the stranger, and Elizabeth happening to see the countenance of both as they looked at each other, was all astonishment at the effect of the meeting. Both changed colour, one looked white, the other red. Squire Wickham, after a few moments, touched his headdress—a salutation which Squire Darcy just deigned to return. What could be the meaning of it? It was impossible to imagine; it was impossible not to long to know.
In another minute, Squire Bingley, but without seeming to have noticed what passed, took leave and rode on with his friend.
Squire Denny and Squire Wickham walked with the young lasses to the door of Phillip Mater’s house, and then made their bows, in spite of Miss Lydia’s pressing entreaties that they should come in, and even in spite of Phillips Mater’s throwing up the parlour window and loudly seconding the invitation.
Phillips Mater was always glad to see her nieces; and the two eldest, from their recent absence, were particularly welcome, and she was eagerly expressing her surprise at their sudden return home, when her civility was claimed towards Reverend Collins by Jane’s introduction of him. She received him with her very best politeness, which he returned with as much more, apologising for his intrusion, without any previous acquaintance with her, which he could not help flattering himself, however, might be justified by his acquaintance with the young lasses who introduced him to her notice. Phillips Mater was quite awed by such an excess of good manners; but her contemplation of one stranger was soon put to an end by exclamations and inquiries about the other; of whom, however, she could only tell her nieces what they already knew, that Squire Denny had brought him from London, and that he was to have a lieutenant’s commission in the Aeronautics Corp. She had been watching him the last hour, she said, as he walked up and down the street, and had Squire Wickham appeared, Kitty and Lydia would certainly have continued the occupation, but unluckily no one passed the windows now except a few of the astronauts, who, in comparison with the stranger, were become “stupid, disagreeable fellows.” Some of them were to dine with the Phillipses the next day, and their aunt promised to make her co-parent call on Squire Wickham, and give him an invitation also, if the family from Liqua would come in the evening. This was agreed to, and Phillips Mater protested that they would have a nice comfortable noisy game of monopoly, and a little bit of hot supper afterwards. The prospect of such delights was very cheering, and they parted in mutual good spirits. Reverend Collins repeated his apologies in quitting the room, and was assured with unwearying civility that they were perfectly needless.
As they rode home under the bright desert stars, their robes wrapped tight against the stinging chill in the air, Elizabeth related to Jane what she had seen pass between the two gentlemen; but though Jane would have defended either or both, had they appeared to be in the wrong, she could no more explain such behaviour than her sister.
Reverend Collins on his return highly gratified Bennet Mater by admiring Phillips Mater’s manners and politeness. He protested that, except Lady Catherine and her daughter, he had never seen a more elegant woman; for she had not only received him with the utmost civility, but even pointedly included him in her invitation for the next evening, although utterly unknown to her before. Something, he supposed, might be attributed to his connection with them, but yet he had never met with so much attention in the whole course of his life.



Chapter 16


As no objection was made to the young people’s engagement with their aunt, and all Reverend Collins’s scruples of leaving Bennet Pater and Bennet Mater for a single evening during his visit were most steadily resisted, the ferflivver conveyed him and the five lasses at a suitable hour to Mandalay; and the girls had the pleasure of hearing, as they entered the drawing-room, that Squire Wickham had accepted their uncle’s invitation, and was then in the house.
When this information was given, and they had all taken their seats, Reverend Collins was at leisure to look around him and admire, and he was so much struck with the size and furniture of the apartment, that he declared he might almost have supposed himself in the small saddle room at Rosings; a comparison that did not at first convey much gratification; but when Phillips Mater understood from him what Rosings was, and who was its proprietor—when she had listened to the description of only one of Lady Catherine’s drawing-rooms, and found that the air-conditioner alone had cost eight hundred plats, she felt all the force of the compliment, and would hardly have resented a comparison with the robotic repair workshop.
In describing to her all the grandeur of Lady Catherine and her estate, with occasional digressions in praise of his own humble abode, and the improvements it was receiving, he was happily employed until the gentlemen joined them; and he found in Phillips Mater a very attentive listener, whose opinion of his consequence increased with what she heard, and who was resolving to retail it all among her neighbours as soon as she could. To the girls, who had no appetite for the conversation of the reverend, and who had nothing to do but fan themselves and wish that Phillips Mater could afford air-conditioning, the interval of waiting appeared very long. It was over at last, however. The gentlemen did approach, and when Squire Wickham walked into the room, Elizabeth felt that she had neither been seeing him before, nor thinking of him since, with the smallest degree of unreasonable admiration. The astronauts of the Ngala Corps were in general a very creditable, gentlemanlike set, and the best of them were of the present party; but Squire Wickham was as far beyond them all in person, countenance, air, and walk, as they were superior to the broad-faced, stuffy uncle Phillips, who followed them into the room.
Squire Wickham was the happy man towards whom almost every female eye was turned, and Elizabeth was the happy woman by whom he finally seated himself; and the agreeable manner in which he immediately fell into conversation, though it was only on the likely intensity and duration of the dusty season, made her feel that the commonest, dullest, most threadbare topic might be rendered interesting by the skill of the speaker.
With such rivals for the notice of the nubile as Squire Wickham and the astronauts, Reverend Collins seemed to sink into insignificance; to the young lasses he certainly was nothing; but he had still at intervals a kind listener in Phillips Mater, and was by her watchfulness, most abundantly supplied with cactus tea and cornbread. It is probable that Phillips Mater was unaware that paramours were proscribed for pastors. When the game-tables were placed, he had the opportunity of obliging her in turn, by sitting down to mahjong.
“I know little of the game at present,” said he, “but I shall be glad to improve myself.” 
“I would be very happy to help you improve yourself whenever you are free,” said Phillips Mater. “I have a lot of leisure time now that my children are mostly grown,” she added. She was pleased with her own ingenuity in informing Reverend Collins in this subtle manner that she was now sufficiently free from child-rearing duties to be able to take a paramour.
Squire Wickham did not play at mahjong, and with ready delight was he received at the other table between Elizabeth and Lydia. At first there seemed danger of Lydia’s engrossing him entirely, for she was a most determined talker; but being likewise extremely fond of Monopoly, she soon grew too much interested in the game, too eager in buying property and building houses to have attention for anyone in particular. Allowing for the common demands of the game, Squire Wickham was therefore at leisure to talk to Elizabeth, and she was very willing to hear him, though what she chiefly wished to hear she could not hope to be told—the history of his acquaintance with Squire Darcy. She dared not even mention that gentleman. Her curiosity, however, was unexpectedly relieved. Squire Wickham began the subject himself. He inquired how far Netherfield was from Mandalay; and, after receiving her answer, asked in a hesitating manner how long Squire Darcy had been staying there.
“About a month,” said Elizabeth; and then, unwilling to let the subject drop, added, “He is a man of very large property in New Arabia, I understand.”
“Yes,” replied Squire Wickham; “his estate there is a noble one with mine interests that clear ten thousand per annum. You could not have met with a person more capable of giving you certain information on that head than myself, for I have been connected with his family in a particular manner from my infancy.”
Elizabeth could not but look surprised.
“You may well be surprised, Miss Bennet, at such an assertion, after seeing, as you probably might, the very cold manner of our meeting yesterday. Are you much acquainted with Squire Darcy?”
“As much as I ever wish to be,” cried Elizabeth very warmly. “I have spent four days in the same house with him, and I think him very disagreeable.”
“I have no right to give my opinion,” said Wickham, “as to his being agreeable or otherwise. I am not qualified to form one. I have known him too long and too well to be a fair judge. It is impossible for me to be impartial. But I believe your opinion of him would in general astonish—and perhaps you would not express it quite so strongly anywhere else as here you are in your own family.”
“Upon my word, I say no more here than I might say in any house in the neighbourhood, except Netherfield. He is not at all liked in Mexistan. Everybody is disgusted with his pride. You will not find him more favourably spoken of by anyone.”
“I cannot pretend to be sorry,” said Wickham, after a short interruption, “that he or that any man should not be estimated beyond their deserts; but with him I believe it does not often happen. The world is blinded by his fortune and consequence, or frightened by his high and imposing manners, and sees him only as he chooses to be seen.”
“I should take him, even on my slight acquaintance, to be an ill-tempered man.” 
Wickham only shook his head.
“I wonder,” said he, at the next opportunity of speaking, “whether he is likely to be in this colony much longer.”
“I do not at all know; but I heard nothing of his going away when I was at Netherfield. I hope your plans in favour of the Ngala Corps will not be affected by his being in the neighbourhood.”
“Oh! No—it is not for me to be driven away by Squire Darcy. If he wishes to avoid seeing me, he must go. We are not on friendly terms, and it always gives me pain to meet him, but I have no reason for avoiding him but what I might proclaim before all the world, a sense of very great ill-usage, and most painful regrets at his being what he is. His father, Miss Bennet, the late Darcy Pater, was one of the best men that ever breathed, and the truest friend I ever had; and I can never be in company with this Squire Darcy without reflecting with sadness on how unreliable genes are in transmitting character. His behaviour to myself has been scandalous; but I verily believe I could forgive him anything and everything but his disgracing the pedigree of his father.”
Elizabeth found the interest of the subject increase, and listened with all her heart; but the delicacy of it prevented further inquiry.
Squire Wickham began to speak on more general topics, Mandalay, the neighbourhood, the society, appearing highly pleased with all that he had yet seen, and speaking of the latter with gentle but very intelligible gallantry.
“It was the prospect of constant society, and good society,” he added, “which was my chief inducement to enter the Ngala. I knew it to be a most respectable, agreeable prospecting corps, and my friend Denny tempted me further by his account of their present quarters, and the very great attentions and excellent acquaintances Mandalay had procured them. Society, I own, is necessary to me. I have been a disappointed man, and my spirits will not bear solitude. I must have employment and society. A life of prospecting and excavation is not what I was intended for, but circumstances have now made it eligible. The church ought to have been my profession—I was brought up for the church, and I should at this time have been in possession of a most valuable living, had it pleased the gentleman we were speaking of just now.”
“Indeed!”
“Yes—the late Darcy Pater promised on his deathbed that his family’s influence would secure me a pastorage. He was my godfather, and excessively attached to me. I cannot do justice to his kindness. He meant to provide for me amply, and thought he had done it; but when the living fell, it was given elsewhere.” 
“Good heavens!” cried Elizabeth; “but how could that be? How could his family disregard his will?”
“There was just such an informality in the terms of the promise as to give me no hope from law. A man of honour could not have doubted the intention, but Squire Darcy chose to doubt it—or to treat it as a merely conditional recommendation, and to assert that I had forfeited all claim to it by extravagance, imprudence—in short anything or nothing. Certain it is, that the living became vacant two years ago, exactly as I was of an age to hold it, and that it was given to another man; and no less certain is it, that I cannot accuse myself of having really done anything to deserve to lose it. I have a warm, unguarded temper, and I may have spoken my opinion of him, and to him, too freely. I can recall nothing worse. But the fact is, that we are very different sort of men, and that he hates me.”
“This is quite shocking! He deserves to be publicly disgraced.” 
“Some time or other he will be—but it shall not be by me. Till I can forget his father, I can never defy or expose him.” 
Elizabeth honoured him for such feelings, and thought him handsomer than ever as he expressed them.
“But what,” said she, after a pause, “can have been his motive? What can have induced him to behave so cruelly?”
“A thorough, determined dislike of me—a dislike which I cannot but attribute in some measure to jealousy. Had the late Darcy Pater liked me less, his son might have borne with me better; but his father’s uncommon attachment to me irritated him, I believe, very early in life. He had not a temper to bear the sort of competition in which we stood—the sort of preference which was often given me.’
“I had not thought Squire Darcy so bad as this—though I have never liked him. I had not thought so very ill of him. I had supposed him to be despising his fellow-creatures in general, but did not suspect him of descending to such malicious revenge, such injustice, such inhumanity as this.”
“I will not trust myself on the subject,” replied Wickham; “I can hardly be just to him.” 
Elizabeth was again deep in thought, and after a time exclaimed, “To treat in such a manner the godson, the friend, the favourite of his father!” She could have added, “A young man, too, like you, whose very countenance may vouch for your being amiable”—but she contented herself with, “and one, too, who had probably been his companion from childhood, connected together, as I think you said, in the closest manner!” 
“We were born in the same parish, within the same park; the greatest part of our youth was passed together; inmates of the same house, engaged together in the same activities – in camel racing, lizard hunting, and holodeck adventures, we were alternately rivals and allies. The relationship between my father and Darcy Pater began in a professional capacity, but soon evolved into an intimate and confidential friendship, and finally into a love affair that lasted until my father’s death. My father began life in the profession that your father does such credit to, but he gave up everything to be of use to the late Darcy Pater and devoted all his time to the care of the Pemberley property and to the happiness his paramour. Darcy Pater often acknowledged himself to be under the greatest obligations to my father’s loving care, and when, immediately before my father’s death, Darcy Pater gave him a voluntary promise of providing for me, I am convinced that he felt it to be as much a debt of gratitude to him, as of his affection to myself.” 
“How strange!” cried Elizabeth. “How abominable! I wonder that the very pride of this Squire Darcy has not made him just to you! If from no better motive, that he should not have been too proud to be dishonest—for dishonesty I must call it.” 
“It is wonderful,” replied Wickham, “for almost all his actions may be traced to pride; and pride had often been his best friend. It has connected him nearer with virtue than with any other feeling. But we are none of us consistent, and in his behaviour to me there were stronger impulses even than pride.” 
“Can such abominable pride as his have ever done him good?” 
“Yes. It has often led him to be liberal and generous, to give his money freely, to display hospitality, to assist his employees, and contribute to the development of Titan. Family pride, and filial pride—for he is very proud of what his father was—have done this. Not to appear to disgrace his family, to degenerate from the popular qualities, or lose the influence of the Pemberley family, is a powerful motive. He has also brotherly pride, which, with some brotherly affection, makes him a very kind and careful guardian of his sister, and you will hear him generally cried up as the most attentive and best of brothers.” 
“What sort of lass is Miss Darcy?”
He shook his head. “I wish I could call her amiable. It gives me pain to speak ill of a Darcy. But she is too much like her brother—very, very proud. As a child, she was affectionate and pleasing, and extremely fond of me; and I have devoted hours and hours to her amusement. But she is nothing to me now. She is a nubile lass, about fifteen or sixteen, and, I understand, highly accomplished. Since her father’s death, her home has been NeoLondon, where a lady lives with her, and superintends her education.”
After many pauses and many trials of other subjects, Elizabeth could not help reverting once more to the first, and saying:
“I am astonished at his intimacy with Squire Bingley! How can Squire Bingley, who seems good humour itself, and is, I really believe, truly amiable, be in friendship with such a man? How can they suit each other? Do you know Squire Bingley?”
“Not at all.”
“He is a sweet-tempered, amiable, charming man. He cannot know what Squire Darcy is.”
“Probably not; but Darcy can please where he chooses. He does not want abilities. He can be a conversible companion if he thinks it worth his while. Among those who are at all his equals in consequence, he is a very different man from what he is to the less prosperous. His pride never deserts him; but with the rich he is liberal-minded, just, sincere, rational, honourable, and perhaps agreeable—allowing something for fortune and figure.”
The mahjong party soon afterwards breaking up, the players gathered round the other table and Reverend Collins took his station between his cousin Elizabeth and Phillips Mater. The usual inquiries as to his success were made by the latter. It had not been very great; he had lost every round; but when Phillips Mater began to express her concern thereupon, he assured her with much earnest gravity that it was not of the least importance, that he considered the money as a mere trifle, and begged that she would not make herself uneasy.
“I know very well, madam,” said he, “that when persons sit down to a mahjong-table, they must take their chances of these things, and happily I am not in such circumstances as to make five silvers any object. There are undoubtedly many who could not say the same, but thanks to Lady Catherine de Bourgh, I am removed far beyond the necessity of regarding little matters.”
Squire Wickham’s attention was caught; and after observing Reverend Collins for a few moments, he asked Elizabeth in a low voice whether her relation was very intimately acquainted with the family of de Bourgh.
“Lady Catherine de Bourgh,” she replied, “has very lately given him a living. I hardly know how Reverend Collins was first introduced to her notice, but he certainly has not known her long.”
“You know of course that Lady Catherine de Bourgh and Lady Anne Darcy were brother and sister; consequently that she is aunt to the present Squire Darcy.”
“No, indeed, I did not. I knew nothing at all of Lady Catherine’s connections. I never heard of her existence till the day before yesterday.”
“Her daughter, Miss de Bourgh, will have the protection of a brother of very large fortune, and it is believed that she will breed with her cousin.”
“Breed with her cousin!” Elizabeth could not hide her shock.
“Oh, of course, you don’t know,” explained Wickham quickly. “They are not genetically related. She was adopted as a young girl.”
“Yes, of course,” Elizabeth replied, remembering the story from Reverend Collins. Learning of the understanding between the families made Elizabeth smile, as she thought of poor Miss Bingley. Vain indeed must be all her attentions and her attempts to flaunt her genetic quality, if he were already destined to father the children of another.
“Reverend Collins,” said she, “speaks highly both of Lady Catherine and her daughter; but from some particulars that he has related of her ladyship, I suspect his gratitude misleads him, and that in spite of her being his patroness, she is an arrogant, conceited woman.”
“I believe her to be both in a great degree,” replied Wickham; “I have not seen her for many years, but I very well remember that I never liked her, even when she was Lewis De Burgh, and that her manners have always been dictatorial and insolent. She has the reputation of being remarkably sensible and clever; but I rather believe she derives this reputation partly from the influence of her status and fortune, and partly from the report of her nephew, who chooses that everyone connected with him should have an understanding of the first class.”
Elizabeth allowed that he had given a very rational account of it, and they continued talking together, with mutual satisfaction till supper put an end to games, and gave the rest of the lasses their share of Squire Wickham’s attentions. There could be no conversation in the noise of Phillips Mater’s supper party, but his manners recommended him to everybody. Whatever he said, was said well; and whatever he did, done gracefully. Elizabeth went away with her head full of him. She could think of nothing but of Squire Wickham, and of what he had told her, all the way home; but there was not time for her even to mention his name as they went, for neither Lydia nor Reverend Collins were once silent. Lydia talked incessantly of the thrill of having owned both Boardwalk and Park Place and the number of hotels she had purchased; and Reverend Collins in describing the remarkable civility of Phillips Mater, her warmth and solicitousness, had more to say than he could well manage before the cart arrived at Liqua House.



Chapter 17

Elizabeth related to Jane the next day what had passed between Squire Wickham and herself.
