With joy and relief, he reported the means of his daughter’s deliverance from exile—to an audience not merely wondering, but incredulous; for Bennet Mater, with more perseverance than politeness, protested he must be entirely mistaken; and Lydia, always unguarded and often uncivil, boisterously exclaimed:
“Good Lord! Senator Lucas, how can you tell such a story? Do not you know that Reverend Collins wants to breed with Lizzy?”
Nothing less than the discipline of diplomat could have borne without anger such treatment; but Senator Lucas’s training carried him through it all; and though he begged leave to be positive as to the truth of his information, he listened to all their impertinence with the most forbearing courtesy.
Elizabeth, feeling it incumbent on her to relieve him from so unpleasant a situation, now put herself forward to confirm his account, by mentioning her prior knowledge of it from Charlotte herself; and endeavoured to put a stop to the exclamations of her mother and sisters by the earnestness of her congratulations to Senator Lucas, in which she was readily joined by Jane, and by making a variety of remarks on the high quality of offspring that might be expected from the match, the excellent character of Reverend Collins, and the convenient distance of Hanoi from NeoLondon.
Bennet Mater was in fact too much overpowered to say a great deal while Senator Lucas remained; but no sooner had he left them than her feelings found a rapid vent. In the first place, she persisted in disbelieving the whole of the matter; secondly, she was very sure that Reverend Collins had been taken in; thirdly, she trusted that they would not be genetically compatible; and fourthly, that the agreement might be annulled after genetic counseling. Two inferences, however, were plainly deduced from the whole: one, that Elizabeth was the real cause of the mischief; and the other that she herself had been barbarously misused by them all; and on these two points she principally dwelt during the rest of the day. Nothing could console and nothing could appease her. Nor did that day wear out her resentment. How could Elizabeth be so careless of her own life and future when her mother thought of nothing else! A week elapsed before she could see Elizabeth without scolding her, a month passed away before she could speak to Senator and Lady Lucas without being rude, and many months were gone before she could at all forgive their daughter.
Bennet Pater’s emotions were much more tranquil on the occasion, and such as he did experience he pronounced to be of a most agreeable sort; for it amused him, he said, to discover that Charlotte Lucas, whom he had been used to think tolerably brave, would sell herself so cheaply just to secure a living on this godforsaken colony. What a price to pay for such a tawdry prize!
Jane confessed herself a little surprised at the proposed contract; but she said less of her astonishment than of her earnest desire that Reverend Collins’s offspring would avoid the worst deficiencies of their sire. Kitty and Lydia were far from envying Miss Lucas, for Reverend Collins was only a clergyman; and it affected them in no other way than as a piece of news to spread at Mandalay.
Lady Lucas could not be insensible of triumph on being able to report to Bennet Mater on the comfort of having a daughter well settled; and she called at Liqua rather oftener than usual to say how happy she was, though Bennet Mater’s sour looks and ill-natured remarks might have been enough to drive happiness away.
Between Elizabeth and Charlotte there was a restraint which kept them mutually silent on the subject; and Elizabeth feared that no real confidence could ever subsist between them again. She could not blame Charlotte for making such a choice, but whenever she looked at Charlotte, she feared that she was looking into her own future. She told herself, with reluctant immodesty, that she was more nubile and more motherly than Charlotte, and that she was sure to receive a more attractive offer than that of Reverend Collins. This would just begin to console her, when she would reflect that she was already twenty-two and had never received any other offer than that the one that she had just refused. Already perhaps past her best years, she could scarcely be confident of a better opportunity arising. Even Charlotte’s dismal lot might in the end be superior to her own. Her pity for Charlotte made her turn with greater concern to her sister, for whose happiness she grew daily more anxious, as Bingley had now been gone a week and nothing more was heard of his return.
Jane had sent Caroline an early answer to her email, and was counting the days till she might reasonably hope to hear again. An email from Reverend Collins arrived on Zeusday, addressed to their father, and written with all the solemnity of gratitude which a tenmonth’s abode in the family might have prompted. After discharging his conscience on that head, he proceeded to inform them, with many rapturous expressions, of his happiness in having obtained breeding rights to their amiable neighbour, Miss Lucas, and then explained that it was merely with the view of making birthing plans that he had been so ready to close with their kind wish of seeing him again at Liqua, whither he hoped to be able to return on Moonday fortnight; for Lady Catherine, he added, so heartily approved of their parenting contract, that she wished it to be concluded as soon as possible, which he trusted would be an unanswerable argument with his amiable Charlotte to make him the happiest of fathers as soon as possible.
Reverend Collins’s return into Mexistan was no longer a matter of pleasure to Bennet Mater. On the contrary, she was as much disposed to complain of it as her co-parent. It was very strange that he should come to Liqua instead of to Lucas Lodge; it was also very inconvenient and exceedingly troublesome. She hated having visitors in the house while her health was so indifferent, and prospective parents were of all people the most disagreeable. Such were the gentle murmurs of Bennet Mater, and they gave way only to the greater distress of Squire Bingley’s continued absence.
Neither Jane nor Elizabeth were comfortable on this subject. Day after day passed away without bringing any other tidings of him than the report which shortly prevailed in Mandalay of his coming no more to Netherfield the whole dusty season; a report which highly incensed Bennet Mater, and which she never failed to contradict as a most scandalous falsehood.
Even Elizabeth began to fear—not that Bingley had changed his opinion—but that his sisters would be successful in keeping him away. Unwilling as she was to admit an idea so destructive of Jane’s happiness, she could not prevent its frequently occurring. The united efforts of his two unfeeling sisters and of his overpowering friend, assisted by the sterling qualifications of Miss Darcy might be too much, she feared, for the strength of his assessment of Jane.
As for Jane, her anxiety under this suspense was, of course, more painful than Elizabeth’s, but whatever she felt she was desirous of concealing, and between herself and Elizabeth, therefore, the subject was never alluded to. But as no such delicacy restrained her mother, an hour seldom passed in which she did not talk of Bingley, express her impatience for his arrival, or even require Jane to confess that if he did not come back she would think herself very ill used. It needed all Jane’s steady mildness to bear these attacks with tolerable tranquillity.
Reverend Collins returned most punctually on Moonday fortnight, but his reception at Liqua was not quite so gracious as it had been on his first introduction. He was too happy, however, to need much attention; and luckily for the others, the business of birth planning relieved them from a great deal of his company. The chief of every day was spent by him at Lucas Lodge, and he sometimes returned to Liqua only in time to make an apology for his absence before the family went to bed.
Bennet Mater was really in a most pitiable state. The very mention of anything concerning the contract threw her into an agony of ill-humour, and wherever she went she was sure of hearing it talked of. The sight of Miss Lucas was odious to her. As her successor in that house, she regarded her with jealous abhorrence. Whenever Charlotte came to see them, she concluded her to be anticipating the hour of possession; and whenever she spoke in a low voice to Reverend Collins, was convinced that they were talking of the Liqua estate, and resolving to turn herself and her daughters out of the house, as soon as Bennet Pater were dead. She complained bitterly of all this to her co-parent.
“Indeed, Bennet Pater,” said she, “it is very hard to think that Charlotte Lucas should ever be resident of this house, that I should be forced to make way for her, and live to see her take her place in it!”
“My dear, do not give way to such gloomy thoughts. Let us hope for better things. Let us flatter ourselves that I may be the survivor. A pastor, after all, has to visit mines on a regular basis, exposing himself to all manner of carcinogenic particles, respiratory ailments and the possibility of mining accidents. He may not live long enough to take residence of Liqua.”
This was not very consoling to Bennet Mater.
“It doesn’t matter if he outlives you or not. When you are dead, the church will grant the residence to someone and we will left homeless. If only we could have had a son!”
“If only daughters could inherit!” added Bennet Pater.
“How anyone could have the conscience to take away an estate from a man’s co-parent and daughters, I cannot understand; and given to the church! Why should they have it more than anybody else?”
“Because in exchange they ensure our eternal residence in the house of God!” said Bennet Pater, with an irony that was entirely unappreciated by his co-parent.
“Oh, I understand why you don’t care,” said Bennet Mater. “You’ll be dead.”


Chapter 24

Miss Bingley’s email arrived, and put an end to doubt. The very first sentence conveyed the assurance of their being all settled in NeoLondon for the dusty season, and concluded with her brother’s regret at not having had time to pay his respects to his friends in Mexistan before he left the colony.
Hope was over, entirely over; and when Jane could attend to the rest of the letter, she found little, except the professed affection of the writer, that could give her any comfort. Miss Darcy’s praise occupied the chief of it. Her suberb qualifications for motherhood were again dwelt on, and Caroline boasted joyfully of their increasing intimacy, and ventured to predict the accomplishment of the wishes which had been unfolded in her former email. She wrote also with great pleasure of her brother’s being an inmate of Squire Darcy’s house, and mentioned with raptures the sophistication of the newest generation of robot staff.
Elizabeth, to whom Jane very soon communicated the chief of all this, heard it in silent indignation. Her heart was divided between concern for her sister, and resentment against all others. To Caroline’s assertion of her brother’s being impressed by Miss Darcy she paid no credit. That he appraised Jane’s fitness highly, respected her and was moreover fond of her, she doubted no more than she had ever done; and much as she had always been disposed to like him, she could not think without anger, hardly without contempt, on that easiness of temper, that want of proper resolution, which now made him the slave of his designing friends, and led him to defer his own judgment to the caprice of their inclination. Had his own future, however, been the only sacrifice, he might have been allowed to sport with it in whatever manner he thought best, but her sister’s was involved in it, as she thought he must be sensible himself. It was a subject, in short, on which reflection would be long indulged, and must be unavailing. She could think of nothing else; and yet whether Bingley had really come to regard Jane as insufficiently qualified to be the mother of his offspring, or whether he had been prevailed upon by his friends to suppress his own judgement; whether he had been aware of Jane’s attachment, or whether it had escaped his observation; whatever were the case, though her opinion of him must be materially affected by the difference, her sister’s situation remained the same, her peace equally wounded and her future equally insecure.
A day or two passed before Jane had courage to speak of her feelings to Elizabeth; but at last, on Bennet Mater’s leaving to visit a paramour, and Jane and Elizabeth being undisturbed for a few days, Jane could not help saying:
“Oh, that my dear mother had more command over herself! She can have no idea of the pain she gives me by her continual reflections on him. But I will not repine. It cannot last long. He will be forgot, and we shall all be as we were before.”
Elizabeth looked at her sister with incredulous solicitude, but said nothing.
“You doubt me,” cried Jane, slightly colouring; “indeed, you have no reason. He may live in my memory as the most amiable man of my acquaintance, but that is all. I have nothing either to hope or fear, and nothing to reproach him with. Thank God! I have not that pain. A little time, therefore—I shall certainly try to get the better.”
With a stronger voice she soon added, “I have this comfort immediately, that it has not been more than an error of fancy on my side, and that it has done no harm to anyone but myself.”
“My dear Jane!” exclaimed Elizabeth, “you are too good. Your sweetness and disinterestedness are really angelic; I do not know what to say to you. I feel as if I had never done you justice, or loved you as you deserve.”
Miss Bennet eagerly disclaimed all extraordinary merit, and threw back the praise on her sister’s warm affection.
“Nay,” said Elizabeth, “this is not fair. You wish to think all the world respectable, and are hurt if I speak ill of anybody. I only want to think you perfect, and you set yourself against it. Do not be afraid of my running into any excess, of my encroaching on your privilege of universal good-will. You need not. There are few people whom I really love, and still fewer of whom I think well. The more I see of life in this backward colony, the more am I dissatisfied with it; and every day confirms my belief of the triviality of all human characters, and of the corrosiveness of our obsession with gaining the good opinion of the influential at the cost of terrible injustice. I have met with two instances lately, one I will not mention; the other is Charlotte’s breeding contract. It is horrific! In every view it is a tragedy!”
“My dear Lizzy, do not give way to such feelings as these. They will ruin your happiness. You do not make allowance enough for difference of situation and temper. Consider Reverend Collins’s respectability, and Charlotte’s prudent character. Remember that she is a plain lass from a family of middling status; and that it is a situation that can provide security, offspring and the opportunity to stay close to her family. To have to breed with Reverend Collins and bear his children may not be such torture for her, and indeed she may feel something like regard for him and gratitude for the contract.”
“To oblige you, I would try to believe almost anything, but no one else could be benefited by such a belief as this; for were I persuaded that Charlotte had any regard for him, I should only think worse of her understanding than I do now. My dear Jane, Reverend Collins is a conceited, pompous, narrow-minded, silly man; you know he is, as well as I do; and you must feel, as well as I do, that the woman who raises his children must suffer irritation and humiliation every day, quite apart from having to accept the risk that her children will inherit their father’s contemptible character. You shall not belittle the ignominy of her fate. You shall not, to spare my pity for my friend, pretend that there is any justice or decency in this way of populating a colony.”
“I must think your language too strong in condemning this way of life,” replied Jane; “and I hope you will be convinced of it by seeing them happily raising a family together. But enough of this. You alluded to something else. You mentioned two instances. I cannot misunderstand you, but I entreat you, dear Lizzy, not to pain me by thinking that situation is an injustice, and by saying that I am tragically wronged. We must not be so ready to fancy ourselves injured. It is not Squire Bingley’s fault if he has awakened feelings in me. We must not expect a lively young man to be always so guarded and circumspect. Decent men never speak of love to young lasses like ourselves and we have no right to complain if we become infatuated with them.”
“But men take pleasure in making us fall for them.” 
“If it is designedly done, they cannot be justified; but I have no idea of there being so much design in the world as some persons imagine.” 
“I am far from attributing any part of Squire Bingley’s conduct to design,” said Elizabeth; “but without scheming to do wrong, or to make others unhappy, there may be error, and there may be misery. Thoughtlessness, want of attention to other people’s feelings, and want of resolution, will do the business.” 
“And do you impute it to one of those?”
“Yes; to the last. But if I go on, I shall displease you by saying what I think of persons you esteem. Stop me whilst you can.”
“You persist, then, in supposing his sisters influence him?” 
“Yes, in conjunction with his friend.”
“I cannot believe it. Why should they try to influence him? They can only wish his happiness; and if he judges his happiness to lie in raising children with me, it is nobody’s business to convince him otherwise.”
“Your first position is false. They may wish many things besides his happiness; they may wish his increase of wealth and consequence; they may wish him to contract with a lass who has all the importance of influential relatives, powerful connections, and status.”
“Beyond a doubt, they do wish him to choose Miss Darcy,” replied Jane; “but this may be from better feelings than you are supposing. They have known her much longer than they have known me; no wonder if they esteem her better, and they may genuinely think that her genes are of a higher quality than mine. But, whatever may be their own wishes, it is very unlikely they should have opposed their brother’s. What sister would think herself at liberty to do it, unless there were something very objectionable? If they believed him to find in me the best mother of his children, they would not try to dissuade him; if they tried, they could not succeed. By supposing him to have such an assessment of me, you make everybody acting unnaturally and wrong, and me most unhappy. Do not distress me by the idea. I am not ashamed of having been mistaken—or, at least, it is light, it is nothing in comparison of what I should feel in thinking ill of him or his sisters. Let me take it in the best light, in the light in which it may be understood.”
Elizabeth could not oppose such a wish; and from this time Squire Bingley’s name was scarcely ever mentioned between them.
Bennet Mater still continued to wonder and repine at his returning no more, and though a day seldom passed in which Elizabeth did not account for it clearly, there was little chance of her ever considering it with less perplexity. Her daughter endeavoured to convince her of what she did not believe herself, that his attentions to Jane had been merely the effect of a common and transient positive assessment, which ceased when he saw her no more; but though the probability of the statement was admitted at the time, she had the same story to repeat every day. Bennet Mater’s best comfort was that Squire Bingley must be down again in the cold season.
Bennet Pater treated the matter differently. “So, Lizzy,” said he one day, “your sister fancies herself in love, I find. I congratulate her. Next to being a mother, a lass likes to be a little in love now and then. It is something to think of, and it gives her a sort of distinction among her companions. When is your turn to come? You will hardly bear to be long outdone by Jane. Now is your time. Here are astronauts enough in Mandalay to fluster all the young lasses in the colony. Let Wickham be your man. He is a pleasant fellow, and, though astronauts are not compelled to settle with breeding partners quite so precipitously as most of us, he is sure to want a family sooner or later.”
“Thank you, sir, but a less agreeable man would satisfy me. We must not all expect Jane’s good fortune.”
“True,” said Bennet Pater, “but it is a comfort to think that whatever of that kind may befall you, you have an affectionate mother who will make the most of it.”
Squire Wickham’s society was of material service in dispelling the gloom which the late perverse occurrences had thrown on many of the Liqua family. They saw him often, and to his other recommendations was now added that of general unreserve. The whole of what Elizabeth had already heard, his claims on Squire Darcy, and all that he had suffered from him, was now openly acknowledged and publicly canvassed; and everybody was pleased to know how much they had always disliked Squire Darcy before they had known anything of the matter.
Miss Bennet was the only creature who could suppose there might be any extenuating circumstances in the case, unknown to the society of Mexistan; her mild and steady candour always pleaded for allowances, and urged the possibility of mistakes—but by everybody else Squire Darcy was condemned as the worst of men.


Chapter 25

After a week spent in discussing baby names and planning the nursery, Reverend Collins was called from his amiable Charlotte by the arrival of Saturnsday. The only business left was a visit to the genetic counselor to ensure the compatibility of their DNA and the way would be cleared for the reverend to become the happiest of fathers. He took leave of his relations at Liqua with as much solemnity as before; wished the young Bennets good fortune in their pursuit of advantageous contracts, and promised their father another letter of thanks.
On the following Moonday,  Bennet Mater had the pleasure of receiving her brother and his co-parent, who came as usual to spend the Bacchanalia at Liqua. Gardiner Pater  was a sensible, gentlemanlike man, though plagued with asthma, and otherwise greatly superior to his sister, as well by nature as education. The Netherfield ladies would have had difficulty in believing that a man cursed with a genetic defect, could nevertheless have been so cultured and agreeable. Gardiner Mater, who was several years younger than Bennet Mater and Phillips Mater, was an amiable, intelligent woman, though flat-chested with pale lips and lacklustre skin. She was a great favourite with all her Liqua nieces. Between the two eldest and herself especially, there subsisted a particular regard. They had frequently been staying with her in town.
The first part of Gardiner Mater’s business on her arrival was to distribute her presents and describe the newest fashions. When this was done she had a less active part to play. It became her turn to listen. Bennet Mater had many grievances to relate, and much to complain of. They had all been very ill-used since she last saw her sister. Two of her girls had almost been offered breeding contracts, and after all there was nothing in it.
“I do not blame Jane,” she continued, “for Jane would have contracted to Squire Bingley if she could have. But Lizzy! Oh, sister! It is very hard to think that she might have been Reverend Collins’s breeding partner by this time, had it not been for her own perverseness. He made her an offer in this very room, and she refused him. The consequence of it is, that Lady Lucas will have a daughter settled securely on Titan while I must still fret and worry. The Lucases are very artful people indeed, sister. They are all for what they can get. I am sorry to say it of them, but so it is. It makes me very nervous and poorly, to be thwarted so in my own family, and to have neighbours who think of themselves before anybody else. However, your coming just at this time is the greatest of comforts, and I am very glad to hear what you tell us, of llama-wool robes lined with silk.”
Gardiner Mater, to whom the chief of this news had been given before, in the course of Jane and Elizabeth’s correspondence with her, made Bennet Mater a slight answer, and, in compassion to her co-parent’s nieces, turned the conversation.
When alone with Elizabeth afterwards, she spoke more on the subject. “It seems likely to have been a desirable match for Jane,” said she. “I am sorry it went off. But these things happen so often! A young man, such as you describe Squire Bingley, so easily is convinced of the suitability of a nubile lass for a few weeks, and when accident separates them, so easily forgets her, that these sort of inconsistencies are very frequent.”
“An excellent consolation in its way,” said Elizabeth, “but it will not do for us. We do not suffer by accident. It does not often happen that the interference of friends will persuade a young man of independent means to think no more of a lass, who, only a few days prior, had impressed him as the perfect mother of his children.”
“But that expression of ‘the perfect mother’ is so hackneyed, so doubtful, so indefinite, that it gives me very little idea. It is as often applied to an impression which arise from a half-hour’s acquaintance, as to a real, solid assessment. Pray, how convinced was Squire Bingley of Jane’s ‘perfection’?”
“I never saw a more promising inclination; he was growing quite inattentive to other prospects, and wholly engrossed by her displays of fertility. Every time they met, it was more decided and remarkable. At his own ball he offended two or three lasses, by not asking them to dance; and I spoke to him twice myself, without receiving an answer. Could there be finer indications? Is not general blindness to other candidates the sure sign of a decided mind?”
“Oh, yes!—of that kind of decision which I suppose him to have made. Poor Jane! I am sorry for her, because, with her disposition, she may not get over it immediately. It had better have happened to you, Lizzy; you would have laughed yourself out of it sooner. But do you think she would be prevailed upon to go back with us? Change of scene might be of service—and perhaps a little relief from home may be as useful as anything.”
Elizabeth was exceedingly pleased with this proposal, and felt persuaded of her sister’s ready acquiescence.
“I hope,” added Gardiner Mater, “that no consideration with regard to this young man will influence her. We live in so different a part of town, all our connections are so different, and, as you well know, we go out so little, that it is very improbable that they should meet at all, unless he really comes to see her.”
“And that is quite impossible; for he is now in the custody of his friend, and Squire Darcy would no more suffer him to call on Jane in such a part of NeoLondon! My dear aunt, how could you think of it? Squire Darcy may perhaps have heard of such a place as Lizardneck Lane, but he would hardly think a month’s ablution enough to cleanse him from its impurities, were he once to enter it; and depend upon it, Squire Bingley never stirs without him.”
“So much the better. I hope they will not meet at all. But does not Jane correspond with his sister? She will not be able to help calling.”
“She will drop the acquaintance entirely.”
But in spite of the certainty in which Elizabeth affected to place this point, as well as the still more interesting one of Bingley’s being withheld from seeing Jane, she felt a solicitude on the subject which convinced her, on examination, that she did not consider it entirely hopeless. It was possible, and sometimes she thought it probable, that his regard might be reanimated, and the influence of his friends successfully combated by the more natural influence of Jane’s nubility and motherliness.
Miss Bennet accepted her aunt’s invitation with pleasure; and the Bingleys were no otherwise in her thoughts at the same time, than as she hoped by Caroline’s not living in the same house with her brother, she might occasionally spend a morning with her, without any danger of seeing him.
The Gardiners stayed a week at Liqua; and what with the Phillipses, the Lucases, and the astronauts, there was not a day without its engagement. Bennet Mater had so carefully provided for the entertainment of her brother and his co-parent, that they did not once sit down to a family dinner. When the engagement was for home, some of the astronauts always made part of it—of which astronauts Squire Wickham was sure to be one; and on these occasions, Gardiner Mater, rendered suspicious by Elizabeth’s warm commendation, narrowly observed them both. Without supposing Elizabeth, from what she saw, to be very seriously infatuated with Squire Wickham, his positive assessment of her, and her fondness for him, was plain enough to make her a little uneasy; and she resolved to speak to Elizabeth on the subject before she left Mexistan, and represent to her the imprudence of encouraging such an attachment.
To Gardiner Mater, Wickham had one means of affording pleasure, unconnected with his general powers. About ten or a dozen years ago, before her breeding contract, she had spent a considerable time in that very part of New Arabia to which he belonged. They had, therefore, many acquaintances in common; and Wickham was able to fill her in on the sort of gossip that isn’t generally conveyed in emails. She learned that Rubenstein Mater was now violently in love with Wang Pater. After five years together, they still had no interest in any other paramours. Taylor Mater, on the other hand, was merrily hopping from paramour to paramour, leaving a trail of broken hearts behind her. Simpson Mater and Faisel Pater had a ten year history of intermittant romance, interspersed with many outside dalliances, while her old friends Rosselli Mater and McFersun Mater had forgone men altogether and were in love with each other. Wickham also mentioned that a certain Gomez Pater, with whom Gardiner Mater had once been deeply infatuated, was currently unattached. Gardiner Mater listened with rapt attention.
Wickham’s conversation turned to Pemberley. Gardiner Mater had seen Pemberley, and known the late Darcy Pater by character perfectly well. Here consequently was an inexhaustible subject of discourse. In comparing her recollection of Pemberley with the minute description which Wickham could give, and in bestowing her tribute of praise on the character of its late possessor, she was delighting both him and herself. On being made acquainted with Squire Darcy’s treatment of him, she tried to remember some of that gentleman’s reputed disposition when quite a lad which might agree with it, and was confident at last that she recollected having heard Fitzwilliam Darcy formerly spoken of as a very proud, ill-natured boy.


Chapter 26

Gardiner Mater’s caution to Elizabeth was punctually and kindly given on the first favourable opportunity of speaking to her alone; after honestly telling her what she thought, she thus went on:
“You are too sensible a girl, Lizzy, to fall in love merely because you are warned against it; and, therefore, I am not afraid of speaking openly. Seriously, I would have you be on your guard. I have nothing to say against him; he is a most dashing young man with all the outward signs of a superior genetic legacy; and if he had the position he ought to have, I should think you could not do better. But as it is, you must not let your fancy run away with you. You have sense, and we all expect you to use it. Entering a co-parenting contract with an astronaut would be a grave mistake.”
“My dear aunt, this is being serious indeed.”
“Yes, and I hope to engage you to be serious likewise.” 
“Well, then, you need not be under any alarm. I am sure that Squire Wickham has no designs on my womb.”  But Elizabeth’s blush only encouraged Gardiner Mater’s concern.
“This is  a matter of importance, Elizabeth,” she continued. “Astronauts may have good genes, but good genes are not enough to make a  good father.  An astronaut’s job is demanding and dangerous. Even while he lives, he will be frequently away on missions, with hardly any time to attend to his children. And then, as you well know, the death rate of astronauts is alarmingly high. I would hate to see you left with a gaggle of fatherless children...”
“Please do not be concerned. I am not in love with Squire Wickham; no, certainly I am not. But he is, beyond all comparison, the most agreeable man I ever saw – and if he really came to see me as a possible mother to his children, well! I see the imprudence of it, but astronauts do find women to agree to bear their children. How can I promise to be wiser than so many of my fellow-creatures if I am tempted?”
“Your situation is rather worse than that of many of your fellow-creatures, Elizabeth. If your co-parent were to die, you would be in very bad straits. You would still have the right to stay on Titan, of course, but with no brothers and no powerful connections, how could you get by? How could you raise your fatherless brood? It is not a risk you can afford to take.”
Elizabeth was silent. She could see the wisdom of Gardiner Mater’s advice, but she hated to think that love should be so cowardly. Oh! That abominable Squire Darcy! If he had not refused to help Wickham obtain a pastorship, Wickham would now be an enviable catch rather than a hazard to her future.
“Do not let girlish infatuation lead you to neglect common sense. Love is not for lasses. Wait until you are a mature woman and your children no longer need you, and then you may pursue romance. You can even have a love affair with Wickham at that time, if you are both so inclined.”
“I will try to do what I think to be the wisest; and now I hope you are satisfied.” Her aunt assured her that she was, and Elizabeth having thanked her for the kindness of her hints, they parted; a wonderful instance of advice being given on such a point, without being resented.
Reverend Collins returned to Mexistan soon after it had been quitted by the Gardiners and Jane; but as he took up his abode with the Lucases, his arrival was no great inconvenience to Bennet Mater. The resolution of the breeding contract between him and Charlotte was now fast approaching, and she was at length so far resigned as to think it inevitable, and even repeatedly to say, in an ill-natured tone, that she “wished they might have healthy children.” They intended to have the lawyers down on Thorsday, and on Watersday Miss Lucas paid her farewell visit; and when she rose to take leave, Elizabeth, ashamed of her mother’s ungracious and reluctant good wishes, and sincerely affected herself, accompanied her out of the room. As they went downstairs together, Charlotte said:
“I shall depend on hearing from you very often, Eliza.” 
“That you certainly shall.” 
“And I have another favour to ask you. Will you come and see me?” 
“We shall often meet, I hope, in Mexistan.” 
“I am not likely to leave Darwin for some time. Promise me, therefore, to come to Hanoi.” 
Elizabeth could not refuse, though she foresaw little pleasure in the visit.
“My father and Maria are coming to me in Tercember,” added Charlotte, “and I hope you will consent to be of the party. Indeed, Eliza, you will be as welcome as either of them.”
The contracts were signed and the two of them set off for Darwin the next day. Elizabeth soon heard from her friend; and their correspondence was as regular and frequent as it had ever been; that it should be equally unreserved was impossible. Elizabeth could never address her without feeling that all the comfort of intimacy was over. Whereas in the past, they could share their dreams for the future, now Elizabeth could only fervently hope that her own future would be different from that of her friend. Charlotte’s first letters were received with a good deal of eagerness; there could not but be curiosity to know how she would speak of her new home, how she would like Lady Catherine, and how happy she would dare pronounce herself to be; though, when the letters were read, Elizabeth felt that Charlotte expressed herself on every point exactly as she might have foreseen. She wrote cheerfully, seemed surrounded with comforts, and mentioned nothing which she could not praise. The reverend’s house, his furniture and his herd of llamas were all to her taste, and Lady Catherine’s behaviour was most friendly and obliging. It was Reverend Collins’s picture of Hanoi and Rosings rationally softened; and Elizabeth perceived that she must wait for her own visit there to know the rest.
Jane had already written a few lines to her sister to announce their safe arrival in NeoLondon; and when she wrote again, Elizabeth hoped it would be in her power to say something of the Bingleys.
Her impatience for this second email was as well rewarded as impatience generally is. Jane had been a week in town without either seeing or hearing from Caroline. She accounted for it, however, by supposing that her last email to her friend from Liqua had by some accident gone astray.
“My aunt,” she continued, “is going tomorrow into that part of the town, and I shall take the opportunity of calling in Pasteur Street.”
She wrote again when the visit was paid, and she had seen Miss Bingley. “I did not think Caroline in spirits,” were her words, “but she was very glad to see me, and reproached me for giving her no notice of my coming to Neolondon. I was right, therefore, my last email had never reached her. I inquired after their brother, of course. He was well, but so much engaged with Squire Darcy that they scarcely ever saw him. I found that Miss Darcy was expected to dinner. I wish I could see her. My visit was not long, as Caroline and Sondakh Mater were going out. I dare say I shall see them soon here.”
Elizabeth shook her head over this email. It convinced her that accident only could discover to Squire Bingley her sister’s being in town.
Four weeks passed away, and Jane saw nothing of him. She endeavoured to persuade herself that she did not regret it; but she could no longer be blind to Miss Bingley’s inattention. After waiting at home every morning for a fortnight, and inventing every evening a fresh excuse for her, the visitor did at last appear; but the shortness of her stay, and yet more, the alteration of her manner would allow Jane to deceive herself no longer. The email which she wrote on this occasion to her sister will prove what she felt.
“My dearest Lizzy will, I am sure, be incapable of triumphing in her better judgement, at my expense, when I confess myself to have been entirely deceived in Miss Bingley’s regard for me. But, my dear sister, though the event has proved you right, do not think me obstinate if I still assert that, considering what her behaviour was, my confidence was as natural as your suspicion. I do not at all comprehend her reason for wishing to be intimate with me; but if the same circumstances were to happen again, I am sure I should be deceived again. Caroline did not return my visit till yesterday; and not a note, not a line, did I receive in the meantime. When she did come, it was very evident that she had no pleasure in it; she made a slight, formal apology, for not calling before, said not a word of wishing to see me again, and was in every respect so altered a creature, that when she went away I was perfectly resolved to continue the acquaintance no longer. I pity, though I cannot help blaming her. She was very wrong in singling me out as she did; I can safely say that every advance to intimacy began on her side. But I pity her, because she must feel that she has been acting wrong, and because I am very sure that anxiety for her brother is the cause of it. I cannot think why she supposed that I would not be a good mother to her brother’s children, yet if she feels it, it will easily account for her behaviour to me; and so deservedly dear as he is to his sister, whatever anxiety she must feel on his behalf is natural and amiable. I cannot but wonder, however, at her having any such fears now, since, if he was really considering me as a breeding partner, we must have met, long ago. He knows of my being in town, I am certain, from something she said herself; and yet it would seem, by her manner of talking, as if she wanted to persuade herself that he is considering Miss Darcy. I cannot understand it. If I were not afraid of judging harshly, I should be almost tempted to say that he deliberately inspired my affections without ever seriously considering me a as breeding partner. But I will endeavour to banish every painful thought, and think only of what will make me happy—your affection, and the invariable kindness of my dear uncle and aunt. Let me hear from you very soon. Miss Bingley said something of his never returning to Netherfield again, of his giving up the position, but not with any certainty. We had better not mention it. I am extremely glad that you have such pleasant accounts from our friends at Hanoi. Pray go to see them, with Senator Lucas and Maria. I am sure you will be very comfortable there.—Yours, etc.”
This email gave Elizabeth some pain; but her spirits returned as she considered that Jane would no longer be duped, by the sister at least. All expectation from the brother was now absolutely over. She would not even wish for a renewal of his attentions. His character sunk on every review of it; and as a punishment for him, as well as a possible advantage to Jane, she seriously hoped he might really soon form a breeding contract with Squire Darcy’s sister, as by Wickham’s account, she would make him abundantly regret what he had thrown away.
Gardiner Mater about this time reminded Elizabeth of her promise concerning Wickham, and required information; and Elizabeth had such to send as might rather give contentment to her aunt than to herself. His apparent interest had subsided, he was engaged in assessing her qualifications no longer, he was considering the merits of another. Elizabeth was watchful enough to see it all, but she could see it and write of it without material pain. Her heart had been but slightly touched, and her vanity was satisfied with believing that she would have been his only choice, had fortune permitted it. An affectionate and powerful uncle was the most remarkable charm of the young lass to whom he was now turning his attention, in her person she was clumsy, without charm and had rather underdeveloped secondary sexual characteristics, being only fifteen – scarcely old enough to breed; but Elizabeth, less clear-sighted perhaps in this case than in Charlotte’s, did not quarrel with him for his wish for a safer and more prosperous position. Nothing, on the contrary, could be more natural; and while able to suppose that it may require him to choose a lass with slightly inferior genes to be the mother of his children, and to make a bid for her womb before others thought to, she was ready to allow it a wise and desirable measure for both. Why shouldn’t he care as much for giving his children good connections as for giving them good genes?
All this was acknowledged to Gardiner Mater; and after relating the circumstances, she thus went on: “I am now convinced, my dear aunt, that I have never been much infatuated with him; for had I been, I should at present detest his very name, and wish him all manner of evil. But my feelings are not only cordial towards him; they are even impartial towards Miss Kwame. I cannot find out that I hate her at all, or that I am in the least unwilling to think her a very good sort of lass. Moreover, I am sure that no one would detect in my behavior any untoward partiality towards Wickham that could sully my reputation and make other men consider me to be a doubtful proposition. My watchfulness has been effectual. Kitty and Lydia take his defection much more to heart than I do. They are young in the ways of the world, and not yet open to the mortifying conviction that handsome young men may not always remain content to risk their lives as astronauts and may even jump at an opportunity to secure an easier and safer life.


Chapter 27

With no greater events than these in the Liqua family, and otherwise diversified by little beyond the rides to Mandalay, sometimes dirty and sometimes hot, did Primember and Sectober pass away. Tercember was to take Elizabeth to Hanoi. She had not at first thought very seriously of going thither; but Charlotte, she soon found, was depending on the plan and she gradually learned to consider it herself with greater pleasure as well as greater certainty. Absence had increased her desire of seeing Charlotte again, and weakened her disgust of Reverend Collins. There was novelty in the scheme, and as, with such a mother and such uncompanionable sisters, home could not be faultless, a little change was not unwelcome for its own sake. The journey would moreover give her a peep at Jane; and, in short, as the time drew near, she would have been very sorry for any delay. Everything, however, went on smoothly, and was finally settled according to Charlotte’s first sketch. She was to accompany Sir Weiming and his third daughter. The improvement of spending a night in NeoLondon was added in time, and the plan became perfect as a plan could be.
The only pain was in leaving her father, who would certainly miss her, and who, when it came to the point, so little liked her going, that he told her to write to him, and almost promised to answer her.
The farewell between herself and Squire Wickham was perfectly friendly; on his side even more. His present pursuit could not make him forget that Elizabeth had been the first to excite and to deserve his appraisal, the first to listen and to pity, the first to be considered as possibly worthy to be the mother of his children; and in his manner of bidding her adieu, wishing her every enjoyment, there was a solicitude, an interest which she felt must ever attach her to him with a most sincere regard; and she parted from him convinced that they would be lovers someday when her child-rearing years had passed.
Her fellow-travellers the next day were not of a kind to make her think him less agreeable. Senator Lucas, and his daughter Maria, a good-humoured girl, but as empty-headed as himself, had nothing to say that could be worth hearing, and were listened to with about as much delight as the closh-closh of the camels’ hooves on the rasping sand. Elizabeth loved absurdities, but she had known the Senator’s too long. He could tell her nothing new of his loyal but thoughtless support of government policies; and his civilities were worn out, like his information.
It was a journey of only fifty miles, and they began it so early as to be in Lizardneck Lane by noon. As they drove to Gardiner Pater’s door, Jane was at a drawing-room window watching their arrival; when they entered the passage she was there to welcome them, and Elizabeth, looking earnestly in her face, was pleased to see it healthful and lovely as ever. On the stairs were a troop of little boys and girls, whose eagerness for their cousin’s appearance would not allow them to wait in the family room, and whose shyness, as they had not seen her for a tenmonth, prevented their coming lower. All was joy and kindness. The day passed most pleasantly away; the morning in bustle and shopping, and the afternoon at a camel rodeo.
Elizabeth then contrived to sit by her aunt. Their first object was her sister; and she was more grieved than astonished to hear, in reply to her minute inquiries, that though Jane always struggled to support her spirits, there were periods of dejection. It was reasonable, however, to hope that they would not continue long. Gardiner Mater gave her the particulars also of Miss Bingley’s visit in Lizardneck Lane, and repeated conversations occurring at different times between Jane and herself, which proved that the former had, from her heart, given up the hope of breeding with him.
Gardiner Mater then rallied her niece on Wickham’s desertion, and complimented her on bearing it so well.
“But my dear Elizabeth,” she added, “what sort of lass is Miss Kwame? I should be sorry to think our friend mercenary.”
“Pray, my dear aunt, what is the difference in breeding considerations, between the mercenary and the prudent motive? Where does discretion end, and avarice begin? Last Bacchanalia you were afraid of his contracting with me, because it would be imprudent; and now, because he is trying to get a lass with an influential uncle, you want to find out that he is mercenary.”
“If you will only tell me what sort of lass Miss Kwame is, I shall know what to think.” 
“She is a very good kind of lass, I believe. I know no harm of her, though it is said she has little aptitude for domestic accomplishments and is rather childish.” 
“An innate deficiency of mental faculties, no doubt. And he paid her not the smallest attention till her cousin’s death made her object of her uncle’s generous solicitude.” 
“No—why should he? If it were not allowable for him to gain my affections because I had no family connections, what occasion could there be for considering a lass whom he did not care about, and who was equally lacking in influence?” 
“It seems cowardly for him to choose to breed with a lass who is unlikely to pass on genes for high intelligence to his children.” 
“A man in distressed circumstances has not leisure to demand all the perfections which other people may require. If he does not object to her qualifications, why should we?” 
“His not objecting does not justify him. It only shows that he lacks confidence in his ability to survive the hazards of an astronaut’s life and to eventually merit a promotion to a safer position.”
“Well,” cried Elizabeth, “have it as you choose. He shall be mercenary and a coward.” 
“No, Lizzy, that is what I do not choose. I should be sorry, you know, to think ill of a young man who has lived so long in New Arabia.” 
“Oh! If that is all, I have a very poor opinion of young men who live in New Arabia; and their intimate friends who live in Mexistan are not much better. I am sick of them all. Thank Heaven! I am going tomorrow where I shall find a man who has not one agreeable quality, who has neither manner nor sense to recommend him. Stupid men are the only ones worth knowing, after all.” 
“Take care, Lizzy; that speech savours strongly of disappointment.” 
Before they were separated by the conclusion of the rodeo, she had the unexpected happiness of an invitation to accompany her uncle and aunt in a tour of pleasure which they proposed taking in the clear season.
“We have not determined how far it shall carry us,” said Gardiner , “but, perhaps, to the Lakes.”
No scheme could have been more agreeable to Elizabeth, and her acceptance of the invitation was most ready and grateful. “Oh, my dear, dear aunt,” she rapturously cried, “what delight! What felicity! You give me fresh life and vigour. Adieu to disappointment and spleen. What are young men to lakes of methane and unexplored caverns that may harbor treasure? Oh! What hours of transport we shall spend! Perhaps we will discover a ruby the size of a saddle horn or a fistful of diamonds! What joy! We could abandon forever the men of Titan and the dreary necessity to breed, purchase a ticket to Earth and discover what freedom is like! And if we are not so lucky as that, at least we will have a couple of weeks together to indulge in pretty fantasies!”

Chapter 28

Every object in the next day’s journey was new and interesting to Elizabeth; and her spirits were in a state of enjoyment; for she had seen her sister looking so well as to banish all fear for her health, and the prospect of her eastern tour was a constant source of delight.

When they left the high road for the turning to Hanoi, every eye was in search of the Parsonage, and every turning expected to bring it in view.

At length the Parsonage was discernible. The endless black sands at last yielding to a golden sandscape artificially colored to resemble an earthan desert. Reverend Collins’ small herd of black and white llamas came first into view, followed by his modest concrete dwelling painted with flowers. Reverend Collins and Charlotte appeared at the door, and the carriage stopped at the small gate which led by a short gravel walk to the house, amidst the nods and smiles of the whole party. In a moment they were all out of the carriage, rejoicing at the sight of each other. Collins Mater welcomed her friend with the liveliest pleasure, and Elizabeth was more and more satisfied with coming when she found herself so affectionately received. She saw instantly that the reverend’s manners were not altered by his new breeding arrangement; his formal civility was just what it had been, and he detained her some minutes at the gate to hear and satisfy his inquiries after all her family. They were then, with no other delay than his pointing out the neatness of the entrance, taken into the house; and as soon as they were in the parlour, he welcomed them a second time, with ostentatious formality to his humble abode, and punctually repeated all his breeding partner’s offers of refreshment.

Elizabeth was prepared to see him in his glory; and she could not help in fancying that in displaying the good proportion of the room, its aspect and its furniture, he addressed himself particularly to her, as if wishing to make her feel what she had lost in refusing him. But though everything seemed neat and comfortable, she was not able to gratify him by any sigh of repentance, and rather looked with wonder at her friend that she could have so cheerful an air with such a companion. When Reverend Collins said anything of which his breeding partner might reasonably be ashamed, which certainly was not unseldom, she involuntarily turned her eye on Charlotte. Once or twice she could discern a faint blush; but in general Charlotte wisely did not hear. After sitting long enough to admire every article of furniture in the room, from the sideboard to the stove, to give an account of their journey, and of all that had happened in NeoLondon, Reverend Collins invited them to take a stroll in the grounds, which were large and well laid out, and to the cultivation of which he attended himself. Looking after llamas was one of his most respectable pleasures; and Elizabeth admired the command of countenance with which Charlotte talked of the importance of tending to the health and comfort of the llamas, and owned she encouraged it as much as possible. Here, leading the way past every prickly pear, every yucca, and every clump of manna, scarcely allowing them an interval to utter the praises he asked for, he discoursed on the disposition, health and milk production capacity of each of his llamas. But of all the delights which his garden, or which the colony or planet could boast, none were to be compared with the prospect of Rosings, afforded in the distance from a small rise in the front of his house. It was a handsome modern building, well situated on rising ground.

After returning to the house and dusting the novel yellow sand off of their clothes, Charlotte took her sister and friend over the house, extremely well pleased, probably, to have the opportunity of showing it without her breeding partner’s help. It was rather small, but well built and convenient; and everything was fitted up and arranged with a neatness and consistency of which Elizabeth gave Charlotte all the credit. When the reverend could be forgotten, there was really an air of great comfort throughout, and by Charlotte’s evident enjoyment of it, Elizabeth supposed he must be often forgotten. She had already learnt that Lady Catherine was still in Darwin. It was spoken of again while they were at dinner, when Reverend Collins joining in, observed:

“Yes, Miss Elizabeth, you will have the honour of seeing Lady Catherine de Bourgh on the ensuing Sunday at church, and I need not say you will be delighted with her. She is all affability and condescension, and I doubt not but you will be honoured with some portion of her notice when service is over. I have scarcely any hesitation in saying she will include you and Maria in every invitation with which she honours us during your stay here. Her behaviour to my dear Charlotte is charming. We dine at Rosings twice every week, and are never allowed to walk home. Her ladyship’s gyrocopter is regularly ordered for us. I should say, one of her ladyship’s gyrocopters, for she has several.”

“Lady Catherine is a very respectable, sensible woman indeed,” added Charlotte, “and a most attentive neighbour.”

“Very true, my dear, that is exactly what I say. She is the sort of woman whom one cannot regard with too much deference.”

The evening was spent chiefly in talking over Mexistan news, and telling again what had already been written; and when it closed, Elizabeth, in the solitude of her chamber, had to meditate upon Charlotte’s degree of contentment, to understand her composure in bearing with her duty to breed with Reverend Collins, and to acknowledge that it was all done very well. She had also to anticipate how her visit would pass, the quiet tenor of their usual employments, the vexatious interruptions of Reverend Collins, and the gaieties of their intercourse with Rosings. A lively imagination soon settled it all.

About the middle of the next day, as she was in her room getting ready for a walk, a sudden noise below seemed to speak the whole house in confusion; and, after listening a moment, she heard somebody running up stairs in a violent hurry, and calling loudly after her. She opened the door and met Maria in the landing place, who, breathless with agitation, cried out—

“Oh, my dear Eliza! Pray make haste and come into the dining-room, for there is such a sight to be seen! I will not tell you what it is. Make haste, and come down this moment.”

Elizabeth asked questions in vain; Maria would tell her nothing more, and down they ran into the dining-room, which fronted the lane, in quest of this wonder; It was two ladies stopping in a sporty yellow gyrocopter at the garden gate.

“And is this all?” cried Elizabeth. “I expected at least that one of the llamas was in labour, and here is nothing but Lady Catherine and her daughter.”

“La! My dear”, said Maria, quite shocked at the mistake, “it is not Lady Catherine. The old lady is Jenkinson Mater, who lives with them; the other is Miss de Bourgh. Only look at her. She is quite a little creature. Who would have thought she could be so thin and small?”

“She is abominably rude to keep Charlotte out of doors in all this wind. Why does she not come in?”

“Oh! Charlotte says she hardly ever does. It is the greatest of favours when Miss de Bourgh comes in.”

“I like her appearance,” said Elizabeth, struck with other ideas. “She looks sickly and frail. Yes, she will do for him very well. She will bear him suitably feeble children.”

Reverend Collins and Charlotte were both standing at the gate in conversation with the ladies; and Sir Weiming, to Elizabeth’s high diversion, was stationed in the doorway, in earnest contemplation of the greatness before him, and constantly bowing whenever Miss de Bourgh looked that way.
 
At length there was nothing more to be said; the ladies drove on, and the others returned into the house. As they disappeared Reverend Collins no sooner saw the two girls than he began to congratulate them on their good fortune, which Charlotte explained by letting them know that the whole party was asked to dine at Rosings the next day.



Chapter 29

Reverend Collins’s triumph, in consequence of this invitation, was complete. The power of displaying the grandeur of his patroness to his wondering visitors, and of letting them see her civility towards himself and his breeding partner, was exactly what he had wished for; and that an opportunity of doing it should be given so soon, was such an instance of Lady Catherine’s condescension, as he knew not how to admire enough.
“I confess,” said he, “that I should not have been at all surprised by her ladyship’s asking us on Sunday to drink tea and spend the evening at Rosings. I rather expected, from my knowledge of her affability, that it would happen. But who could have foreseen such an attention as this? Who could have imagined that we should receive an invitation to dine there (an invitation, moreover, including the whole party) so immediately after your arrival!”
Scarcely anything was talked of the whole day or next morning but their visit to Rosings. The reverend was carefully instructing them in what they were to expect, so that the sight of rooms fitted with such sophisticated electronics, so many robot servants and such exotic dishes, might not wholly overpower them.
When the ladies were separating for the toilette, he said to Elizabeth— “Do not make yourself uneasy, my dear lass, about your apparel. Lady Catherine is far from requiring that elegance of dress in us which becomes herself and her daughter. I would advise you merely to put on whatever sari is superior to the rest—there is no occasion for anything more. Lady Catherine will not think the worse of you for being simply dressed. She likes to have the distinction of status preserved.” 
While they were dressing, he came two or three times to their different doors, to recommend their being quick, as Lady Catherine very much objected to be kept waiting for her dinner. Such formidable accounts of her ladyship, and her manner of living, quite frightened Maria Lucas, though her curiousity was also piqued as she was eager to see how elegant a lady the former lord had become.
As the weather was fine, they had a pleasant walk of about two miles across the desert. Unlike the endless expanse of untended desert they had crossed to arrive at Hanoi, this stretch was beautified with pink wildflowers among the cactuses and the occasional stately palm tree. At the approach of Rosings, moreover, there was a fanciful stone garden with grey grottos, rock statues and a lilypond, floating like an enchanted island on a black ocean of sand. Elizabeth saw much to be pleased with, though she could not be in such raptures as Reverend Collins expected the scene to inspire.
When they ascended the steps to the hall, Maria’s alarm was every moment increasing, and even Senator Lucas did not look perfectly calm. Elizabeth’s courage did not fail her. She had heard no rumours of Lady Catherine that spoke of either extraordinary talents or disconcerting perversity, and the mere stateliness of money or power she thought she could witness without trepidation.
In the entrance-hall, Reverend Collins pointed out, with a rapturous air, the fine proportions and the large picture windows, each portraying, with electronic wizardry, a different scene; one appearing to overlook a patch of Kenyan savana, another a village in Yorkshire and yet another a mountain temple in Hunan. From there, they followed the welcoming robots through an ante-chamber, to the room where Lady Catherine, her daughter, and Jenkinson Mater were sitting. Her ladyship, with great condescension, arose to receive them and the proper introductions were made.
In spite of having been at the council, Senator Lucas was so completely awed by the grandeur surrounding him, that he had but just courage enough to make a very low bow, and take his seat without saying a word; and his daughter, frightened almost out of her senses, sat on the edge of her chair, not knowing which way to look. Elizabeth found herself quite equal to the scene, and could observe the three ladies before her composedly. Lady Catherine was a tall, large woman, with strongly-marked features that hinted at the handsome man she had once been. Her air was not conciliating, nor was her manner of receiving them such as to make her visitors forget their inferior rank. Lady Catherine was an unusual combination: a man by birth and biology, a woman by choice, dress and air; and although she exuded an elegant femininity, there was one characteristic that she retained from her birth sex, and that was her confidence. No other woman on Titan could have been as regal and commanding as one who was born with, and retained, all the rights of a man.
When, after examining the mother, in whose countenance and deportment she soon found some resemblance to Squire Darcy, she turned her eyes on the daughter, she could almost have joined in Maria’s astonishment at her being so thin and so small. There was neither in figure nor face any likeness between the ladies, and that she had a very different genetic legacy was easily apparent. Miss de Bourgh was pale and sickly; her features, though not positively defective, were insignificant; and she spoke very little, except in a low voice, to Jenkinson Mater, in whose appearance there was nothing remarkable, and who was entirely engaged in listening to what she said.
After sitting a few minutes, they were all sent to the Yorkshire window to admire the view, Reverend Collins attending them to point out its beauties, and Lady Catherine kindly informing them that it was more worth looking at in the summer, for each picture window changed in real time and followed Earthan seasons as faithfully as their subjects had when they were recorded.
The dinner was exceedingly handsome, and there were all the robotic servants and all the articles of plate which Reverend Collins had promised; and, as he had likewise foretold, he took his seat at the bottom of the table, by her ladyship’s desire, and looked as if he felt that life could furnish nothing greater. He carved, and ate, and praised with delighted alacrity; and every dish was commended, first by him and then by Senator Lucas, who was now enough recovered to echo whatever his daughter’s breeding partner said, in a manner which Elizabeth wondered Lady Catherine could bear. But Lady Catherine seemed gratified by their excessive admiration, and gave most gracious smiles, especially when any dish on the table proved a novelty to them, as many did, such as the roast beef obtained from the only cattle herd on Titan, and the shrimp raised in the pond in the garden. The party did not supply much conversation. Elizabeth was ready to speak whenever there was an opening, but she was seated between Charlotte and Miss de Bourgh—the former of whom was engaged in listening to Lady Catherine, while the latter said not a word to her all dinner-time. Jenkinson Mater was chiefly employed in watching how little Miss de Bourgh ate, pressing her to try some other dish, and fearing she was indisposed. Maria thought speaking out of the question, and the gentlemen did nothing but eat and admire.
When the ladies returned to the drawing-room, there was little to be done but to hear Lady Catherine talk, which she did without any intermission till coffee came in, delivering her opinion on every subject in so decisive a manner, as proved that she was not used to have her judgement controverted. She inquired into Charlotte’s domestic concerns familiarly and minutely, gave her a great deal of advice as to the management of them all; told her how everything ought to be regulated in so small a family as hers, and instructed her as to the care of her llamas and her poultry. Elizabeth found that nothing was beneath this great lady’s attention, which could furnish her with an occasion of dictating to others. In the intervals of her discourse with Charlotte, she addressed a variety of questions to Maria and Elizabeth, but especially to the latter, of whose connections she knew the least, and who she observed to Charlotte was a very nubile lass whose intelligent air suggested the probability of a good genetic foundation. She asked her, at different times, how many sisters she had, whether they were older or younger than herself, whether any of them had promising prospects of breeding, whether they were shapely, what skills they had developed, what sort of ferflivver her father kept, and what had been her mother’s maiden name? Elizabeth felt all the impertinence of her questions but answered them very composedly. Lady Catherine then observed,
“Your father’s estate is promised to the church, I think. For your sake,” turning to Charlotte, “I am glad of it; but otherwise I see no occasion for denying females the right to inheritance. Do you play and sing, Miss Bennet?”
“A little.”
“Oh! Then—some time or other we shall be happy to hear you. We have a capital positronic organ, probably superior to anything you have encountered before. But perhaps you are more comfortable on the lute or the violin? You shall display for us some day. Do your sisters play and sing?”
“One of them does.”
“Why did not you all learn? You ought all to have learned. The Miss Watanamis all play, and their father has not so good an income as yours. Do you draw?”
“No, not at all.”
“What, none of you?”
“Not one.”
“That is very strange. But I suppose you had no opportunity. You have at least the practical skills of furniture-making, sewing, family nutrition, etc., I hope.”
“Yes.”
“Ah well, those skills are hardly sufficient to signal genetic superiority, but at least they can reassure a man of your capacity to raise children. Are any of your sisters on the market?”
“Yes, ma’am, all.”
“All! What, all five out at once? Very odd! And you only the second. The younger ones out before the elder ones have bred! Your younger sisters must be very young?”
“Yes, my youngest is not sixteen. Perhaps she is full young to be much on display. But really, ma’am, I think it would be very hard upon younger sisters, that they should not have their share of society and amusement, because the elder may not have the means or inclination to breed early. The last-born has as good a right to the pleasures of youth as the first. And to be kept back on such a motive! I think it would not be very likely to promote sisterly affection or delicacy of mind.”
“Upon my word,” said her ladyship, “you give your opinion very decidedly for so young a person. Pray, what is your age?”
“With three younger sisters grown up,” replied Elizabeth, smiling, “your ladyship can hardly expect me to own it.”
Lady Catherine seemed quite astonished at not receiving a direct answer; and Elizabeth suspected herself to be the first creature who had ever dared to trifle with so much dignified impertinence.
“You cannot be more than twenty-five, I am sure, therefore you need not conceal your age.”
“I am not three-and-twenty.”
“Well, there’s time enough then, but I hope you attract an offer soon. The scent of desperation can so quickly taint the natural motherly aura of a young lass.”
When the gentlemen had joined them, and coffee was over, the party repaired to the holodeck, which was a new and thrilling experience for most of the party. At the touch of a few icons on a monitor, the basement of Rosings was transformed into a rolling English countryside on a summer’s day. Each guest was introduced to a virtual horse and they were invited to pursue a virtual fox. Lady Catherine was, of course, the master of the hunt, and the women, thankfully, were allowed to ride astride their mounts rather than sidesaddle. In spite of this concession, it was a difficult task, as none of them had ridden anything but llamas and camels before. But though the ride was unsteady and hedges were jumped with great trepidation, the experience was magical. Elizabeth had seen videos of the earth  and had always been fascinated by England, the home of her ancestors, but she had never before felt surrounded by an English summer’s day, the mild sun and the dampness of the air feeling both alien and strangely familiar. The pursuit of the fox was the farthest thing from her thoughts, as she was enchanted by the trees, the flowers, the clouds, the woodlands, the fields and the stream. When Lady Catherine declared the fox dead and the game over, Elizabeth reluctantly followed the others out of the basement and thought, for the first time, that to be rich would really be a very fine thing.
After a sherry and a biscuit to console them on their return to reality, a gyrocopter was offered to Reverend Collins and, upon its gratefully acceptance, a robot servant was immediately dispatched to prepare it. The party then gathered round the fire to hear Lady Catherine determine what weather they were to have on the morrow. From these instructions they were summoned by the arrival of the gyrocopter; and with many speeches of thankfulness on the reverend’s side, and as many bows on the senator’s, they departed. As soon as they had driven from the door, Elizabeth was called on by the reverend to give her opinion on the mistress of Rosings, which, for Charlotte’s sake, she made more favourable than it really was, for apart from her possession of a holodeck, Elizabeth found little to admire in Lady Catherine. But her commendation, though costing her some trouble, could by no means satisfy Reverend Collins, and he was very soon obliged to take her ladyship’s praise into his own hands.


Chapter 30

Senator Lucas stayed only a week at Hanoi, but his visit was long enough to convince him of his daughter’s being most comfortably settled, and of her possessing such a breeding partner and such a neighbour as were not often met with. While Senator Lucas was with them, Reverend Collins devoted his morning to driving him out in his llama cart, and introducing him to the surrounding parishioners; but when he went away, the whole family returned to their usual employments, and Elizabeth was thankful to find that they did not see more of the reverend by the alteration, for the chief of the time between breakfast and dinner was now passed by him either at tending the llamas or in reading and writing, and looking out of the window in his own den, which fronted the road. The room in which the ladies sat was backwards. Elizabeth had at first rather wondered that Charlotte should not prefer the family room for common use; it was a better sized room, and had a more pleasant aspect; but she soon saw that her friend had an excellent reason for what she did, for Reverend Collins would undoubtedly have been much less in his own den, had they sat in one equally lively; and she gave Charlotte credit for the arrangement which allowed them the opportunity to talk privately.
“How is your journey to maternity progressing?” Elizabeth asked Charlotte one morning.
“Oh, it has been a trial!” said Charlotte with a sigh. “But I may have some good news.”
 “Have you conceived?”
“Yes, I have, tentatively, at least.”
“Tentatively?”
“Well it’s only a month in, and the first few months are hazardous. Many embryos don’t make it. And then, of course, at three months we must do a genetic analysis and make sure that there are no defects.”
“Yes, I see. Then I tentatively congratulate you.”
“Thank you. At least I am relieved for now of the duty of trying to conceive.” 
“Has it been very hard?” Elizabeth questioned gently, trying not to picture the relevant activities.
“It has been hard,” Charlotte blushed slightly. “I know young lasses like us are not supposed to enjoy the procedure, but I have heard that some breeding partners make it quite tolerable and even quite exciting.”
“The reverend is perhaps not so experienced,” Elizabeth ventured.
“No, I believe he is not experienced at all,” Charlotte responded. “Pastors are not supposed to have paramours, you know.”
“Yes, of course. That would make it doubly difficult for you. But he clearly can perform, since you have conceived.”
“Oh yes, he can do it,” Charlotte answered. “He may not be skillful, but he is enthusiastic – a little overly enthusiastic, perhaps.”
Elizabeth regarded her friend with pity.
“Older women say that paramour love-making is heavenly,” Elizabeth remarked. “You will have your reward in time, I suppose. But hold on – are pastors’ co-parents allowed to have paramours?”
“Yes, of course, you silly goose. I may be contracted to the reverend, but he doesn’t own me.”
“He just has a claim on a couple decades of your life!” responded Elizabeth with some heat, thinking once again of the injustice of the current society.
“True, but think,” said Charlotte in a conspiratoral voice, “in twenty years, Reverend Collins will be celibate again and I will be free to taste all the pleasures of romance.”
Two decades seemed to Elizabeth a very long time to be sacrificed to the service of the reverend and his progeny, but she was gratified that Charlotte could console herself with these pretty thoughts, and for the sake of Charlotte’s dignity and her own composure, she turned the conversation to less distressing topics.
In this quiet way, the first fortnight of her visit soon passed away. The Festival of Fertility was approaching, and the week preceding it was to bring an addition to the family at Rosings, which in so small a circle must be important. Elizabeth had heard soon after her arrival that Squire Darcy was expected there in the course of a few weeks, and though there were not many of her acquaintances whom she did not prefer, his coming would furnish one comparatively new to look at in their Rosings parties, and she might be amused in seeing how hopeless Miss Bingley’s designs on him were, by his behaviour to his cousin, for whom he was evidently destined by Lady Catherine, who talked of his coming with the greatest satisfaction, spoke of him in terms of the highest admiration, and seemed almost angry to find that he had already been frequently seen by Miss Lucas and herself.
His arrival was soon known at the Parsonage; for Reverend Collins was walking the whole morning within view of the lodges opening into Hanoi Lane, in order to have the earliest assurance of it, and upon seeing his ferflivver descending into the Park, hurried home with the great intelligence. On the following morning he hastened to Rosings to pay his respects. There were two nephews of Lady Catherine to require them, for Squire Darcy had brought with him a Colonel Fitzwilliam, the younger son of his uncle Lord Fitzwilliam, and, to the great surprise of all the party, when Reverend Collins returned, the gentlemen accompanied him. Charlotte had seen them from her room, crossing the road, and immediately told the girls what an honour they might expect, adding:
“I may thank you, Eliza, for this piece of civility. Squire Darcy would never have come so soon to wait upon me.”
Elizabeth had scarcely time to disclaim all right to the compliment, before their approach was announced by the door-bell, and shortly afterwards the three gentlemen entered the room. Colonel Fitzwilliam, who led the way, was about thirty, not handsome, but in person and address most truly the gentleman. Squire Darcy looked just as he had been used to look in Mexistan, paid his compliments, with his usual reserve, to Collins Mater, and whatever might be his feelings toward her friend, met her with every appearance of composure. Elizabeth merely curtseyed to him without saying a word.
Colonel Fitzwilliam entered into conversation directly with the readiness and ease of a well-bred man, and talked very pleasantly; but his cousin, after having addressed a slight observation on the house and grounds to Collins Mater, sat for some time without speaking to anybody.
