Until recently, Elizabeth had never seriously doubted that she, Jane and her sisters would all be able to find financially stable, pleasant and genetically well-endowed co-parents. Now, however, she was plagued with doubts. Did their genomes really harbour a dark secret? Were they all doomed to be partnered with men flawed enough to be desperate? Or, worse still, not to be partnered at all? With the precipitous mortality rate of miners and astronauts, even desperate men were in short supply. Would they be forced to leave the safety and familiarity of Titan to take their chances on the wild frontier of the new colonies? And had she proudly and piously dismissed the only opportunity she would ever be offered for a normal life?
She thought again of Darcy’s letter, which she was in a fair way of soon knowing by heart. She meditated on every sentence; and her feelings towards its writer were at times widely different. When she remembered the style of his address, she was still full of indignation; but when she considered how unjustly she had condemned and upbraided him, her anger was turned against herself; and his disappointed feelings became the object of compassion. His attachment excited gratitude, his general character respect; but she could not approve him; nor could she for a moment repent her refusal, or feel the slightest inclination ever to see him again. Yes, he could have provided her the opportunity to stay in Titan, but at what cost? His love was of that excessive kind that could only end in tragedy. To have a normal life would be to pair with someone who admired and respected her, not to enter into a frightening pact of passion with a man would sacrifice all he valued and esteemed for the chance to breed with her.
Her case was hopeless, she was sure. No rational man would take her, and to partner with an irrational man was to play dice with the devil. Dismissing thoughts of her own future, she thought about her sisters.
Poor Jane! Squire Darcy’s explanation, by restoring Bingley to all her former good opinion, heightened the sense of what Jane had lost. His admiration was proved to have been sincere and appropriate, and his conduct cleared of all blame, unless any could attach to the implicitness of his confidence in his friend. How grievous then was the thought that, of a situation so desirable in every respect, so replete with advantage, so promising for happiness, Jane had been deprived by some quirk of her family’s genetic makeup!
Jane, however, was so nubile, so motherly and so accomplished in the arts of running a household, that she would surely be able to contract with some man who, being beget with flaws himself, would be willing to accept a future deprived of male offspring. A bleak future, perhaps, but a future, nonetheless.
Catherine and Lydia, on the other hand, had even less to recommend themselves. They were nubile and spirited, but they would do nothing to improve themselves. They refused to take seriously any of the arts that would advertise their genetic fitness, nor would they learn any of the practical skills of running a household. They were hopeless of remedy. Her father, contented with laughing at them, would never exert himself to restrain the wild giddiness of his youngest daughters; and her mother was entirely insensible of the evil. Elizabeth had frequently united with Jane in an endeavour to check the imprudence of Catherine and Lydia; but while they were supported by their mother’s indulgence, what chance could there be of improvement? Catherine, weak-spirited, irritable, and completely under Lydia’s guidance, had been always affronted by their advice; and Lydia, self-willed and careless, would scarcely give them a hearing. They were ignorant, idle, and vain. While there was an astronaut in Mandalay, they would flirt with him. But not even an astronaut would be willing to offer a breeding contract to a lass that was not able to promise him a son.
And then there was Mary. Mary did not even possess the abundant nubility and spirit of her younger sisters. Her only hope had been to attract a serious man who valued intelligence more than well-developed secondary sexual characteristics or charm. But such a man, such an intelligent, earnest and discerning man, would never be desperate enough to accept a lass with a blighted genome.
When to these recollections was added the development of Wickham’s character, it may be easily believed that the happy spirits which had seldom been depressed before, were now so much affected as to make it almost impossible for her to  tolerably cheerful.
Her unpleasant reflections were interrupted at last by the return of the fox-hunting party. Lady Catherine and her other guests were all in high spirits, having caught and de-tailed a virtual fox in record time. Closing up the holodeck, they returned upstairs and celebrated with cocktails of cactus juice and fermented camel milk.
When they parted, Lady Catherine, with great condescension, wished them a good journey, and invited them to come to Hanoi again next year; and Miss de Bourgh exerted herself so far as to curtsey and hold out her hand to Elizabeth and Charlotte.


Chapter 38

On Saturnsday morning Elizabeth and Reverend Collins met for breakfast a few minutes before the others appeared; and he took the opportunity of paying the parting civilities which he deemed indispensably necessary.
“I know not, Miss Elizabeth,” said he, “whether. Collins Mater has yet expressed her sense of your kindness in coming to us; but I am very certain you will not leave the house without receiving her thanks for it. The favour of your company has been much felt, I assure you. We know how little there is to tempt anyone to our humble abode. Our plain manner of living, our small rooms with few electronics and no robots, and the little we see of the world, must make Hanoi extremely dull to a young lass like yourself; but I hope you will believe us grateful for the condescension, and that we have done everything in our power to prevent your spending your time unpleasantly.”
Elizabeth was eager with her thanks and assurances of happiness. She had spent six weeks with great enjoyment; and the pleasure of being with Charlotte, and the kind attentions she had received, must make her feel the obliged. Reverend Collins was gratified, and with a more smiling solemnity replied:
“It gives me great pleasure to hear that you have passed your time not disagreeably. We have certainly done our best; and most fortunately having it in our power to introduce you to very superior society, and, from our connection with Rosings, the frequent means of varying the humble home scene, I think we may flatter ourselves that your Hanoi visit cannot have been entirely irksome. Our situation with regard to Lady Catherine’s family is indeed the sort of extraordinary advantage and blessing which few can boast. You see on what a footing we are. You see how continually we are engaged there. In truth I must acknowledge that, with all the disadvantages of this humble parsonage, I should not think anyone abiding in it an object of compassion, while they are of our entertainments at Rosings.”
Words were insufficient for the elevation of his feelings; and he was obliged to walk about the room, while Elizabeth tried to unite civility and truth in a few short sentences. “Indeed our visits to Rosings were among the highlights of my visit here,” she said, thinking about her rides in the virtual English countryside.
“You may, in fact, carry a very favourable report of us into Mexistan. I flatter myself at least that you will be able to do so, and that your relatives will be pleased to hear of our successful pregnancy. Only let me assure you, my dear Miss Elizabeth, that I can from my heart most cordially wish you equal felicity in breeding.  Our geneticist assures us that dear Charlotte and I are remarkably well-matched. We seem to have been designed for each other.”
Elizabeth could safely say that it was a great happiness where that was the case, and with equal sincerity could add that she rejoiced in the early success of his breeding efforts. Privately she reflected that it was a comfort too know that Charlotte was freed for some time from the duty of sharing his bed. She was not sorry to have the civilities interrupted by the lady in question. Poor Charlotte! It was melancholy to leave her to such society! But she had chosen it with her eyes open; and though evidently regretting that her visitors were to go, she did not seem to ask for compassion. Her home and her housekeeping, her parish and her pregnancy, and all their dependent concerns, had not yet lost their charms. A week ago, Elizabeth’s sympathy for Charlotte had been unqualified, but now, fearing for her own future, Elizabeth wondered if Charlotte’s fate might in the end prove kinder than her own.
At length the camel carriage arrived, the trunks were fastened on, the parcels placed within, and it was pronounced to be ready. After an affectionate parting between the friends, Elizabeth was attended to the carriage by Reverend Collins, and as they walked down the garden he was commissioning her with his best respects to all her family, not forgetting his thanks for the kindness he had received at Liqua in the dusty season, and his compliments to the Gardiners, though unknown. He then handed her in, Maria followed, and the door was on the point of being closed, when he suddenly reminded them, with some consternation, that they had hitherto forgotten to leave any message for the ladies at Rosings.
“But,” he added, “you will of course wish to have your humble respects delivered to them, with your grateful thanks for their kindness to you while you have been here.”
Elizabeth made no objection; the door was then allowed to be shut, and the carriage drove off.
“Good gracious!” cried Maria, after a few minutes’ silence, “it seems but a day or two since we first came! And yet how many things have happened!”
“A great many indeed,” said her companion with a sigh.
“We have dined nine times at Rosings, and played in the holodeck twice! How much I shall have to tell!”
Elizabeth added privately, “And how much I shall have to conceal!” Their journey was performed without much conversation, or any alarm; and after a wearisome journey of twelve hours across the desert, they reached Gardiner Pater’s house, where they were to remain a few days.
Jane looked well, and Elizabeth had little opportunity of studying her spirits, amidst the various engagements which the kindness of her aunt had reserved for them. But Jane was to go home with her, and at Liqua there would be leisure enough for observation.
It was not without an effort, meanwhile, that she could wait even for Liqua, before she told her sister of Squire Darcy’s proposals. To know that she had the power of revealing what would so exceedingly astonish Jane, and must, at the same time, so highly gratify whatever of her own vanity she had not yet been able to reason away, was such a temptation to openness as nothing could have conquered but the state of indecision in which she remained as to the extent of what she should communicate; she could hardly discuss the proposal without mentioning the dark suspicions that Darcy harbored concerning the  genetic soundness of Bennet family, and she was determined not to burden Jane with all the attendant horror of this possibility until she had confronted her father on the matter. She must know the truth.


Chapter 39

It was the second week in Quintober, in which the three young lasses set out together from Lizardneck Lane for the town of Bukit Lawang, in Mexistan; and, as they drew near the appointed inn where Bennet Pater’s carriage was to meet them, they quickly perceived, in token of the coachman’s punctuality, both Kitty and Lydia looking out of a dining-room up stairs. These two girls had been above an hour in the place, happily employed in visiting an opposite haberdashers, watching the sentinel on guard, and carving a capybara.
After welcoming their sisters, they triumphantly displayed a table set out with a capybara dressed with herbs and yams, exclaiming, “Is not this nice? Is not this an agreeable surprise?”
“And we mean to treat you all,” added Lydia, “but you must lend us the money, for we have just spent ours at the shop out there.” Then, showing her purchases—”Look here, I have bought this armlet. I do not think it is very pretty; but I thought I might as well buy it as not. I shall pull it to pieces as soon as I get home, and see if I can make I nice pair of anklets out of it.”
And when her sisters abused it as ugly, she added, with perfect unconcern, “Oh! But there were two or three much uglier in the shop; and when I have bought some prettier gold filament to redesign it with, I think it will be very tolerable. Besides, it will not much signify what one wears this clear season, after the Ngaya have left Mexistan, and they are going in a fortnight.”
“Are they indeed!” cried Elizabeth, with the greatest satisfaction.
“They are going to be undergoing training near Orleans; and I do so want Mamma to take us all there for the cold season! She is going there anyway to visit a paramour, and it would be such a delicious scheme if she were to take us with her; and I dare say would hardly cost anything at all! Only think what a miserable cold season else we shall have!”
“Yes,” thought Elizabeth, “that would be a delightful scheme indeed, and completely do for us at once. Good Heaven! Orleans, and a whole campful of astronauts, to us, who have been overset already by one poor aeronautics corps, and the monthly balls of Mandalay!”
“Now I have got some news for you,” said Lydia, as they sat down at table. “What do you think? It is excellent news—capital news—and about a certain person we all like!”
Jane and Elizabeth looked at each other, and the waiter was told he need not stay. Lydia laughed, and said:
“Aye, that is just like your formality and discretion. You thought the waiter must not hear, as if he cared! I dare say he often hears worse things said than I am going to say. But he is an ugly fellow! I am glad he is gone. I never saw such a long chin in my life. Well, but now for my news; it is about dear Wickham; too good for the waiter, is it not? There is no danger of Wickham’s offering a contract to Mary Kwame. There’s for you! She is gone down to her uncle at Liverpool: gone to stay. Wickham is safe.”
“And Mary Kwame is safe!” added Elizabeth.
“Apparently her father said she was too young for breeding! Have you ever heard of anything more ridiculous!”
“But she is too young,” said Elizabeth seriously. “She is only fifteen.” 
“Only fifteen! I am fifteen,” said Lydia proudly, “And I’m sure my reproductive system is fully mature!” 
“Yes, but Mary Kwame looks even younger,” said Kitty. “I saw her at Nara. She’s just a skinny little freckled thing. Certainly no suitable mother for Wickham’s children!” 
Elizabeth silently reflected how this incident further verified Darcy’s story. She no longer had any doubt as to his veracity.
As soon as all had ate, and the elder ones paid, the carriage was ordered; and after some contrivance, the whole party, with all their boxes, work-bags, and parcels, were seated in it.
“How nicely we are all crammed in,” cried Lydia. “Well, now let us be quite comfortable and snug, and talk and laugh all the way home. And in the first place, let us hear what has happened to you all since you went away. Have you seen any pleasant men? Did you have any chance to display your nubility? I was in great hopes that one of you would have got a breeding contract before you came back. Jane will be getting quite desperate soon, I declare. She is almost six-and-twenty!? She’ll have to start looking for jobs on Ganymede soon! Lord, how worried I should be of not having a contract before six-and-twenty! My aunt Phillips wants you so to get contracts as quickly as you can. She says Lizzy had better have taken Reverend Collins; but I do not think there would have been any fun in it. Lord! How I should like to be a mother before any of you; and then I would have my own house and be the queen of the family. Dear me! We had such a good piece of fun the other day at Colonel Forster’s. Kitty and me were to spend the day there, and Forster Mater promised to have a little dance in the evening; (by the bye, Forster Mater and me are such friends!) and so she asked the two Harringtons to come, but Harriet was ill, and so Pen was forced to come by herself; and then, what do you think we did? We dressed up Chamberlayne – one of the astronauts, you know – in woman’s clothes on purpose to pass for a lass, only think what fun! Not a soul knew of it, but Colonel and Forster Mater, and Kitty and me, except my aunt, for we were forced to borrow one of her gowns; and you cannot imagine how well he looked! When Wickham and Santiago, and two or three more of the men came in, they did not know him in the least. Lord! How I laughed! I thought I should have died. Only Denny recognized him. He’d seen him dressed up as a lass before, so that wasn’t fair. Apparently astronauts do that quite often when they have a party. I guess they get bored when there are no ladies around!”
With such kinds of histories of their parties and good jokes, did Lydia, assisted by Kitty’s hints and additions, endeavour to amuse her companions all the way to Liqua. Elizabeth listened as little as she could, but there was no escaping the frequent mention of Wickham’s name.
Their reception at home was most kind. Bennet Mater rejoiced to see that Jane’s outward displays of fertility were not diminished by her inward distress; and more than once during dinner did Bennet Pater say voluntarily to Elizabeth:
“I am glad you are come back, Lizzy.”
Their party in the dining-room was large, for almost all the Lucases came to meet Maria and hear the news; and various were the subjects that occupied them: Lady Lucas was inquiring of Maria, after the welfare of her eldest daughter and the progress of her pregnancy; Bennet Mater was doubly engaged, on one hand collecting an account of the present fashions from Jane, who sat some way below her, and, on the other, retailing them all to the younger Lucases; and Lydia, in a voice rather louder than any other person’s, was enumerating the various pleasures of the morning to anybody who would hear her.
“Oh! Mary,” said she, “I wish you had gone with us, for we had such fun! As we went along, Kitty and I drew up the shutters, and pretended there was nobody in the carriage; and I should have gone so all the way, if Kitty had not been sick; and when we got to the George, I do think we behaved very handsomely, for we treated the other three with the nicest cold luncheon in the world, and if you would have gone, we would have treated you too. And then when we came away it was such fun! I thought we never should have got into the carriage. I was ready to die of laughter. And then we were so merry all the way home! We talked and laughed so loud, that anybody might have heard us ten miles off!”
To this Mary very gravely replied, “Far be it from me, my dear sister, to depreciate such pleasures! They would doubtless be congenial with the generality of female minds. But I confess they would have no charms for me—I should infinitely prefer a book.”
But of this answer Lydia heard not a word. She seldom listened to anybody for more than half a minute, and never attended to Mary at all.
In the afternoon Lydia was urgent with the rest of the girls to walk to Mandalay, and to see how everybody went on; but Elizabeth steadily opposed the scheme. It should not be said that the Miss Bennets could not be at home half a day before they were in pursuit of the astronauts. There was another reason too for her opposition. She dreaded seeing Squire Wickham again, and was resolved to avoid it as long as possible. The comfort to her of the corps approaching removal was indeed beyond expression. In a fortnight they were to go—and once gone, she hoped there could be nothing more to plague her on his account.
She had not been many hours at home before she found an opportunity to confront her father in his den.
“Did you have encounter any trouble at Hanoi,” said her father with concern. “You seemed a bit serious at lunch.”
“There has been something on my mind,” Elizabeth admitted.
“Didn’t they treat you well? Did Lady Catherine look down her nose at you?” “It’s nothing like that,” Elizabeth said, trying to decide how to broach the subject without mentioning Darcy.
“Well what is it then?” said her father encouragingly.
“Only this,” said Elizabeth with determination. “I need to know the truth from you – why do you and my mother have only girl children.”
“Well!” said her father. “What’s put that into your head? Has someone been making enquiries into your suitability as a breeding partner,” her father said with a chuckle.
“How can you laugh!” said Elizabeth, shocked. “How can you be amused at the prospect of your daughters being unable to form breeding contracts because of an inherited genetic defect!”
“Lizzy!” said her father in a gentle voice, surprised at her vehemence and the seriousness of the conclusion she had jumped to. “It is nothing so serious. Nothing that can affect the breeding prospects of my girls.”
“It isn’t?” said Elizabeth, hoping, and yet fearing to hope, that this was true.
“No, not at all! The defect is in me,” said her father. “In my Y chromosome. I can’t father sons, but my daughters are perfectly fine.”
“Oh thank goodness,” said Elizabeth, sitting down in relief. She paused to take in this new information.
“But why have kept this from us?,” she said with a distressed voice, “why not let us know?”
“I didn’t think it of any importance,” said her father. “It doesn’t affect you, you see. You would clear any pre-breeding genetic analysis.”
“But father,” said Elizabeth, “Don’t you realize the cloud of suspicion you have put this family under?”
“Have I?” said Bennet Pater. “Who is suspicious?” 
“Everyone!” said Elizabeth. “Everyone can see that there are no boys in our family and so everyone has reason to think that there is a genetic flaw in our heritage!” 
“But that’s ridiculous,” said her father. “You never suspected before, and you’ve been in this family all of your life.” 
“But I’m not a man looking for a lass to bear his children!” said Elizabeth. “Of course such men think of such things!” 
“I see,” said her father gravely. “Yes, I see. That is a problem.” Bennet Pater pondered.
“I tell you what I’ll do,” he said with sudden inspiration. “I’ll tell Senator Lucas about my broken chromosome. Between him and his gossipy co-parent, the whole Colony of Mexistan will know about it before the end of the week!’
“Yes, that’s a good idea,” said Elizabeth with an attempt at a smile.
“I hope I haven’t inadvertently obstructed the progress of any breeding contracts,” said her father, uncertainly.
“No, don’t, worry about that” said Elizabeth. “There are no breeding contracts in the offing.” She felt that it would be useless to blame her father for his insensibility, but she bitterly reflected on what her father’s thoughtlessness had cost Jane. If only Squire Bingley had known that Jane’s genes were as flawless as Jane herself.


Chapter 40

Elizabeth’s impatience to acquaint Jane with what had happened no longer needed to be overcome; and at length, resolving to suppress every particular in which her sister was concerned, and preparing her to be surprised, she related to her the next morning the chief of the scene between Squire Darcy and herself.
Miss Bennet’s astonishment was considerable, though somewhat lessened by the strong sisterly partiality which made any admiration of Elizabeth appear perfectly natural. “As shocking as it is for a mature man to fall in love with a lass,” said Jane, “it is perhaps more understandable that he could have fallen for you. With your maturity, intelligence and natural elegance, you are more like a woman than a lass.” She was sorry that Squire Darcy should have developed such inappropriate sentiments; but still more was she grieved for the unhappiness which her sister’s refusal must have given him.
“His giving way to such a scandalous passion was wrong,” said she, “and certainly ought not to have admitted; but consider how much it must increase his disappointment!”
“Indeed,” replied Elizabeth, “I am heartily sorry for him; but he has other feelings, which will probably soon drive away his regard for me. You do not blame me, however, for refusing him?”
“Blame you! Oh, no.”
“But you blame me for having spoken so warmly of Wickham?” 
“No—I do not know that you were wrong in saying what you did.” “But you will know it, when I tell you what happened the very next day.” She then spoke of the letter, repeating the whole of its contents as far as they concerned George Wickham. What a stroke was this for poor Jane! Who would willingly have gone through the world without believing that so much wickedness existed in the whole race of mankind, as was here collected in one individual. Nor was Darcy’s vindication, though grateful to her feelings, capable of consoling her for such discovery. Most earnestly did she labour to prove the probability of error, and seek to clear the one without involving the other.
“This will not do,” said Elizabeth; “you never will be able to make both of them good for anything. Take your choice, but you must be satisfied with only one. There is but such a quantity of merit between them; just enough to make one good sort of man; and of late it has been shifting about pretty much. For my part, I am inclined to believe it all Darcy’s; but you shall do as you choose.”
It was some time, however, before a smile could be extorted from Jane.
“I do not know when I have been more shocked,” said she. “Wickham so very bad! It is almost past belief. And poor Squire Darcy! Dear Lizzy, only consider what he must have suffered. Such a disappointment! And with the knowledge of your ill opinion, too! And having to relate such a thing of his sister! It is really too distressing. I am sure you must feel it so.”
“Oh! No, my regret and compassion are all done away by seeing you so full of both. I know you will do him such ample justice, that I am growing every moment more unconcerned and indifferent. Your profusion makes me saving; and if you lament over him much longer, my heart will be as light as a feather.”
“Poor Wickham! There is such an expression of goodness in his countenance! Such an openness and gentleness in his manner!”
“There certainly was some great mismanagement in the education of those two young men. One has got all the goodness, and the other all the appearance of it.”
“I never thought Squire Darcy so deficient in the appearance of it as you used to do.”
“And yet I meant to be uncommonly clever in taking so decided a dislike to him, without any reason. It is such a spur to one’s genius, such an opening for wit, to have a dislike of that kind. One may be continually abusive without saying anything just; but one cannot always be laughing at a man without now and then stumbling on something witty.” 
“Lizzy, when you first read that letter, I am sure you could not treat the matter as you do now.” 
“Indeed, I could not. I was uncomfortable enough, I may say unhappy. And with no one to speak to about what I felt, no Jane to comfort me and say that I had not been so very weak and vain and nonsensical as I knew I had! Oh! How I wanted you!” 
“How unfortunate that you should have used such very strong expressions in speaking of Wickham to Squire Darcy, for now they do appear wholly undeserved.” 
“Certainly. But the misfortune of speaking with bitterness is a most natural consequence of the prejudices I had been encouraging. There is one point on which I want your advice. I want to be told whether I ought, or ought not, to make our acquaintances in general understand Wickham’s character.” 
Miss Bennet paused a little, and then replied, “Surely there can be no occasion for exposing him so dreadfully. What is your opinion?” 
“That it ought not to be attempted. Squire Darcy has not authorised me to make his communication public. On the contrary, every particular relative to his sister was meant to be kept as much as possible to myself; and if I endeavour to undeceive people as to his conduct, who will believe me? The general prejudice against Squire Darcy is so violent, that it would be the death of half the good people in Mandalay to attempt to place him in an amiable light. I am not equal to it. Wickham will soon be gone; and therefore it will not signify to anyone here what he really is.” 
Elizabeth paused. “I can only fervently hope that he does not ruin the life of any other young lass, but with nothing more than rumour on our side, I don’t see how we can prevent it.” 
“You are quite right. To have his errors made public might ruin him for ever. He is now, perhaps, sorry for what he has done, and anxious to reform his character. We must not make him desperate.” 
“I don’t know that a man like that can reform his own character,” said Elizabeth with foreboding. “But perhaps he can learn to suppress his proclivities.” 
The tumult of Elizabeth’s mind was allayed by this conversation. She had got rid of two of the secrets which had weighed on her for a fortnight, and was certain of a willing listener in Jane, whenever she might wish to talk again of either. But there was still something lurking behind, of which prudence forbade the disclosure. She dared not relate the other half of Squire Darcy’s letter, nor explain to her sister how sincerely she had been valued by his friend. Here was knowledge in which no one could partake; and she was sensible that nothing less than a perfect understanding between the parties could justify her in throwing off this last encumbrance of mystery. It would perhaps be some comfort to Jane to know that Squire Bingley had been dissuaded from breeding with her due to a misunderstanding about the nature of their family’s genetic inheritance, but the bitterness of losing Bingley because of the stubborn obliviousness of their own father to the delicacy of their situation would cause more pain than it alleviated. “And then,” she thought, “perhaps Bingley will reconsider her as a co-parent when he learns the truth that is now to be promulgated by the loose-lipped Lucases. If that very improbable event should ever take place, I shall merely be able to tell what Bingley may tell in a much more agreeable manner. The liberty of communication cannot be mine till it has lost all its value!” 
She was now, on being settled at home, at leisure to observe the real state of her sister’s spirits. Jane was not happy. She still cherished a very tender affection for Bingley. Having never even fancied herself in love before, her regard had all the warmth of first attachment, and, from her age and disposition, greater steadiness than most first attachments often boast; and so fervently did she value his remembrance, and prefer him to every other man, that all her good sense, and all her attention to the feelings of her friends, were requisite to check the indulgence of those regrets which must have been injurious to her own health and to her reputation.
“Well, Lizzy,” said Bennet Mater one day, “what is your opinion now of this sad business of Jane’s? For my part, I am determined never to speak of it again to anybody. I told my sister Phillips so the other day. But I cannot find out that Jane saw anything of him in NeoLondon. Well, he is a very undeserving young man—and I do not suppose there’s the least chance in the world of her ever getting him now. There is no talk of his coming to Netherfield again in the cold season; and I have inquired of everybody, too, who is likely to know.”
“I do not believe he will ever live at Netherfield any more.” 
“Oh well! It is just as he chooses. Nobody wants him to come. Though I shall always say he used my daughter extremely ill; and if I was her, I would not have put up with it. Well, my comfort is, I am sure that when Jane is a mature woman unencumbered by small children, Bingley will wish to be her paramour and she will refuse him; and then he will be sorry for what he has done.” 
But as Elizabeth could not receive comfort from such distant possibilities, she made no answer.
“Well, Lizzy,” continued her mother, soon afterwards, “and so the Collinses live very comfortable, do they? Well, well, I only hope it will last. And what sort of table do they keep? Charlotte is an excellent manager, I dare say. If she is half as sharp as her mother, she is saving enough. There is nothing extravagant in their housekeeping, I dare say.”
“No, nothing at all.”
“A great deal of good management, depend upon it. Yes, yes, they will take care not to outrun their income. They will never be distressed for money. Well, much good may it do them! And so, I suppose, they often talk of filling Liqua with their progeny when your father is dead. They look upon it as quite their own, I dare say, whenever that happens.”
“It was a subject which they could not mention before me.” 
“No; it would have been strange if they had; but I make no doubt they often talk of it between themselves. Well, if they can be easy with an estate which devolves on them as a consequence of a shamefully sexist system, so much the better. I should be embarrassed to benefit from such injustice.”


Chapter 41

The first week of their return was soon gone. The second began. It was the last of the Ngala Corps stay in Mandalay, and all the young lasses in the neighbourhood, and all the mature women as well, were drooping apace. The dejection was almost universal. The elder Miss Bennets alone were still able to eat, drink, and sleep, and pursue the usual course of their employments. Very frequently were they reproached for this insensibility by Kitty and Lydia, whose own misery was extreme, and who could not comprehend such hard-heartedness in any of the family.
“Good Heaven! What is to become of us? What are we to do?” would they often exclaim in the bitterness of woe. “How can you be smiling so, Lizzy?”
Their affectionate mother shared all their grief; she remembered what she had herself endured on a similar occasion, thirty years ago.
“I am sure,” said she, “I cried for two days together when Colonel Miller’s aeronautics corps went away. I thought I should have broken my heart.”
“I am sure I shall break mine,” said Lydia. “If we could but go to Orleans!” she continued.
“Oh, yes!—if you could but go to Orleans! But Colonel Fitzwilliam wants to have me alone with him. He is quite insistent on it!”
“A visit to the carnival would set me up forever.” “And my aunt Phillips is sure it would do me a great deal of good,” added Kitty.
Such were the kind of lamentations resounding perpetually through Liqua House. But the gloom of Lydia’s prospect was shortly cleared away; for she received an invitation from Forster Mater, the breeding partner of the colonel of the regiment, to accompany her to Orleans. This invaluable friend was a very young woman, who was pregnant with her first child and thus eager to enjoy her last few months of freedom. A resemblance in good humour and good spirits had recommended her and Lydia to each other, and out of their three months’ acquaintance they had been intimate two.
The rapture of Lydia on this occasion, her adoration of Forster Mater, the delight of Bennet Mater, and the mortification of Kitty, are scarcely to be described. Wholly inattentive to her sister’s feelings, Lydia flew about the house in restless ecstasy, calling for everyone’s congratulations, and laughing and talking with more violence than ever; whilst the luckless Kitty continued in the parlour repining at her fate in terms as unreasonable as her accent was peevish.
“I cannot see why Forster Mater should not ask me as well as Lydia,” said she, “Though I am not her particular friend. I have just as much right to be asked as she has, and more too, for I am two years older.”
In vain did Elizabeth attempt to make her reasonable, and Jane to make her resigned. As for Elizabeth herself, this invitation was so far from exciting in her the same feelings as in her mother and Lydia, that she considered it as the death warrant of all possibility of common sense for the latter; and detestable as such a step must make her were it known, she could not help secretly advising her father not to let her go. She represented to him all the improprieties of Lydia’s general behaviour, the little advantage she could derive from the friendship of such a woman as Forster Mater, and the probability of her being yet more imprudent with such a companion at Orleans, where the temptations must be greater than at home. He heard her attentively, and then said:
“Lydia will never be easy until she has exposed herself in some public place or other, and we can never expect her to do it with so little expense or inconvenience to her family as under the present circumstances.”
“If you were aware,” said Elizabeth, “of the very great disadvantage to us all which must arise from the public notice of Lydia’s unguarded and imprudent manner—nay, which has already arisen from it, I am sure you would judge differently in the affair.”
“Already arisen?” repeated Bennet Pater. “What, has she frightened away some prospective breeding partner? Poor little Lizzy! But do not be cast down. Such squeamish youths as cannot bear to be connected with a little absurdity are not worth a regret. Come, let me see the list of pitiful fellows who have been kept aloof by Lydia’s folly.”
“Indeed you are mistaken. I have no such injuries to resent. It is not of particular, but of general evils, which I am now complaining. Our importance, our respectability in the world must be affected by the wild volatility, the assurance and disdain of all restraint which mark Lydia’s character. Excuse me, for I must speak plainly. If you, my dear father, will not take the trouble of checking her exuberant spirits, and of teaching her that her present pursuits are not to be the business of her life, she will soon be beyond the reach of amendment. Her character will be fixed, and she will, at sixteen, be the most ignorant and empty-headed lass that ever made herself or her family ridiculous; she will be judged to have inherited the dullest of genes and to have no qualification beyond youth and a tolerable person; and, for all her exuberant display of secondary sexual characteristics, she will end up wholly unable to attract an offer of a breeding contract from any but the most desperate. In this danger Kitty also is comprehended. She will follow wherever Lydia leads. Vain, ignorant, idle, and absolutely uncontrolled! Oh! My dear father, can you suppose it possible that they will not be censured and despised wherever they are known, and that their sisters will not be often involved in the disgrace?”
Bennet Pater saw that her whole heart was in the subject, and affectionately taking her hand said in reply:
“Do not make yourself uneasy, my love. Wherever you and Jane are known you must be respected and valued; and you will not appear to less advantage for having a couple of—or I may say, three—very silly sisters. We shall have no peace at Liqua if Lydia does not go to Orleans. Let her go, then. Colonel Forster is a sensible man, and will keep her out of any real mischief; and she is luckily from too uninfluential a family to be an object of prey to anybody. At Orleans she will having even less success in parading her nubility than she has been here. The officers will find lasses better worth their notice. Let us hope, therefore, that her being there may teach her her own insignificance. At any rate, she cannot grow many degrees worse, without authorising us to lock her up for the rest of her life.”
With this answer Elizabeth was forced to be content; but her own opinion continued the same, and she left him disappointed and sorry. It was not in her nature, however, to increase her vexations by dwelling on them. She was confident of having performed her duty, and to fret over unavoidable evils, or augment them by anxiety, was no part of her disposition.
Had Lydia and her mother known the substance of her conference with her father, their indignation would hardly have found expression in their united volubility. In Lydia’s imagination, a visit to Orleans comprised every possibility of earthly happiness. She saw, with the creative eye of fancy, ferris wheels festooned with ribbons, dances in the gaily lit streets, games of chance and skill, thrilling rides and, finally, the space agency milling with astronauts. She saw herself the object of attention, to tens and to scores of them at present unknown. She saw all the glories of the launch pads – rockets stretching forth in beauteous uniformity of lines, crowds of young and virile men; and, to complete the view, she saw herself seated in a control room, displaying her fertility to at least six astronauts at once.
Had she known her sister sought to tear her from such prospects and such realities as these, what would have been her sensations? But she was entirely ignorant of what had passed; and her raptures continued, with little intermission, to the very day of her leaving home.
Elizabeth was now to see Squire Wickham for the last time. Having been frequently in company with him since her return, agitation was pretty well over; she had even become used to the repugnance that had completely replaced her former partiality. She had learnt to detect, in the same gentle attentions which had first delighted her, an unseemliness that disgusted her.
On the very last day of the Ngala Corps remaining at Mandalay, he dined, with other of the officers, at Liqua; and so little was Elizabeth disposed to part from him in good humour, that on his making some inquiry as to the manner in which her time had passed at Hanoi, she mentioned Colonel Fitzwilliam’s and Squire Darcy’s having both spent three weeks at Rosings, and asked him, if he was acquainted with the former.
He looked surprised, displeased, alarmed; but with a moment’s recollection and a returning smile, replied, that he had formerly seen him often; and, after observing that he was a very gentlemanlike man, asked her how she had liked him. Her answer was warmly in his favour. With an air of indifference he soon afterwards added:
“How long did you say he was at Rosings?”
“Nearly three weeks.”
“And you saw him frequently?”
“Yes, almost every day.”
“His manners are very different from his cousin’s.” 
“Yes, very different. But I think Squire Darcy improves upon acquaintance.” 
“Indeed!” cried Squire Wickham with a look which did not escape her. “And pray, may I ask?—” But checking himself, he added, in a careless tone, “Is it in address that he improves? Has he deigned to add aught of civility to his ordinary style?—for I dare not hope,” he continued in a lower and more serious tone, “that he is improved in essentials.”
 “Oh, no!” said Elizabeth. “In essentials, I believe, he is very much what he ever was.”
 While she spoke, Wickham looked as if scarcely knowing whether to rejoice over her words, or to distrust their meaning. There was a something in her countenance which made him listen with an apprehensive and anxious attention, while she added: “When I said that he improved on acquaintance, I did not mean that his mind or his manners were in a state of improvement, but that, from knowing him better, his disposition was better understood.” 
Wickham’s alarm now appeared in a heightened complexion and agitated look; for a few minutes he was silent, till, shaking off his embarrassment, he turned to her again, and said in the gentlest of accents: “You, who so well know my feeling towards Squire Darcy, will readily comprehend how sincerely I must rejoice that he is wise enough to assume even the appearance of what is right. His pride, in that direction, may be of service, if not to himself, to many others, for it must only deter him from such foul misconduct as I have suffered by. I only fear that the sort of cautiousness to which you, I imagine, have been alluding, is merely adopted on his visits to his aunt, of whose good opinion and judgement he stands much in awe. His fear of her has always operated, I know, when they were together; and a good deal is to be imputed to his wish of forwarding the match with Miss de Bourgh, which I am certain he has very much at heart.” 
Elizabeth could not repress a smile at this, but she answered only by a slight inclination of the head. She saw that he wanted to engage her on the old subject of his grievances, and she was in no humour to indulge him. The rest of the evening passed with the appearance, on his side, of usual cheerfulness, but with no further attempt to distinguish Elizabeth; and they parted at last with mutual civility, and possibly a mutual desire of never meeting again.
When the party broke up, Lydia returned with Forster Mater to Mandalay, from whence they were to set out early the next morning. The separation between her and her family was rather noisy than pathetic. Kitty was the only one who shed tears; but she did weep from vexation and envy. Bennet Mater promised to meet Lydia for lunch the following week during her own visit to Orleans, and concluded with effusive good wishes for the felicity of her daughter, and impressive injunctions that she should not miss the opportunity of enjoying herself as much as possible—advice which there was every reason to believe would be well attended to; and in the clamorous happiness of Lydia herself in bidding farewell, the more gentle adieus of her sisters were uttered without being heard.


Chapter 42

Had Elizabeth’s opinion been all drawn from her own family, she could not have formed a very pleasing opinion of breeding arrangements or domestic comfort. She had never before understood why her father had chosen such a silly, empty-headed lass to bear his children but now she realized that with his defective Y-chromosome, he had had little choice. Her mother must have been desperate indeed to agree to such a precarious future as a life with no sons would bring. The contract was agreed to without any real affection on either side, and arrangements for child-rearing had to be conducted in a relationship almost completely devoid of respect or esteem. But Bennet Pater was not of a disposition to complain of the disappointments fate had burdened him with. In his youth, he found distraction in a string of attractive paramours, but as he grew older, he found himself fonder of the country and of books; and from these tastes had arisen his principal enjoyments. To his co-parent he was very little otherwise indebted, than to her having borne him Elizabeth and Jane. As to his three younger daughters, he felt little to be grateful for other than the occasional amusement their ignorance and folly afforded. This is not the sort of happiness which a father would in general wish; but where other powers of entertainment are wanting, the true philosopher will derive benefit from such as are given.
Elizabeth, however, had never been blind to the impropriety of Bennet Pater’s behaviour as a father. She had always seen it with pain; but respecting his abilities, and grateful for his affectionate treatment of herself, she endeavoured to forget what she could not overlook, and to banish from her thoughts that continual breach of obligation and decorum which, in exposing his own children to contempt, was so highly reprehensible. But she had never felt so strongly as now the disadvantages which must attend the such children, nor ever been so fully aware of the evils arising from so ill-judged a direction of talents; talents, which, rightly used, might at least have preserved the respectability of his daughters and, through the development of demonstrable accomplishments, enhanced their appearance of genetic quality.
When Elizabeth had rejoiced over Wickham’s departure she found little other cause for satisfaction in the loss of the aeronautics corps. Their parties abroad were less varied than before, and at home, though there was the welcome respite gained by her mother’s visit to Orleans with Colonel Fitzwilliam, this relief was more than outweighed by the presence of a sister whose constant repinings at the dullness of everything around them threw a real gloom over their domestic circle; and, though Kitty might in time regain her natural degree of sense, since the disturbers of her brain were removed, her other sister, from whose disposition greater evil might be apprehended, was likely to be hardened in all her folly and assurance by her prolonged immersion within a throng of displaced astronauts. Upon the whole, therefore, she found, what has been sometimes found before, that an event to which she had been looking with impatient desire did not, in taking place, bring all the satisfaction she had promised herself. It was consequently necessary to name some other period for the commencement of actual felicity—to have some other point on which her wishes and hopes might be fixed, and by again enjoying the pleasure of anticipation, console herself for the present, and prepare for another disappointment. Her tour to the Methane Lakes was now the object of her happiest thoughts; it was her best consolation for all the uncomfortable hours which the discontentedness of Kitty made inevitable; and could she have included Jane in the scheme, every part of it would have been perfect.
“But it is fortunate,” thought she, “that I have something to wish for. Were the whole arrangement complete, my disappointment would be certain. But here, by carrying with me one ceaseless source of regret in my sister’s absence, I may reasonably hope to have all my expectations of pleasure realised. A scheme of which every part promises delight can never be successful; and general disappointment is only warded off by the defence of some little peculiar vexation.”
When Lydia went away she promised to write very often and very minutely; but her emails were always long expected, and always very short. Her letters to Elizabeth and Jane contained little else than that they were just returned from the carnival, where such and such astronauts had attended them, and where she had seen such beautiful trifles as made her quite wild; that she had a new sari, or a new veil, which she would have described more fully, but was obliged to leave off in a violent hurry, as Forster Mater called her, and they were going off to a street dance; and from her correspondence with her sister, there was still less to be learnt—for her letters to Kitty, though rather longer, were much too full of exclamation marks and emojis to be made public.
After the first fortnight of her absence and the return of Bennet Mater, health, good humour, and cheerfulness began to reappear at Liqua. Everything wore a happier aspect. The families who had been in town for the dusty season came back again, and the finery and engagements of the cold  arose. Bennet Mater was restored to her usual querulous serenity; and, by the middle of Sextober, Kitty was so much recovered as to be able to enter Mandalay without tears; an event of such happy promise as to make Elizabeth hope that by the following Bacchanalia she might be so tolerably reasonable as not to mention an astronaut above once a day, unless, by some cruel and malicious arrangement at the Space Agency, another aeronautics corps should be quartered in Mandalay.
The time fixed for the beginning of their eastern tour was now fast approaching, and a fortnight only was wanting of it, when a letter arrived from Gardiner Mater, which at once delayed its commencement and curtailed its extent. Gardiner Pater would be prevented by business from setting out till a fortnight later in Heptober, and must be in NeoLondon again within a month, and as that left too short a period for them to go so far, and see so much as they had proposed, or at least to see it with the leisure and comfort they had built on, they were obliged to give up the Methane Lakes, and substitute a more contracted tour, and, according to the present plan, were to go no farther eastwards than New Arabia. In that colony there was enough to be seen to occupy the chief of their three weeks; and to Gardiner Mater it had a peculiarly strong attraction. The town where she had formerly passed some years of her life, and where they were now to spend a few days, was probably as great an object of her curiosity as all the treasures potentially hidden in the caverns near the Methane Lakes.
Elizabeth was excessively disappointed; she had set her heart on seeing the Methane Lakes, and still thought there might have been time enough. But it was her business to be satisfied—and certainly her temper to be happy; and all was soon right again.
With the mention of New Arabia there were many ideas connected. It was impossible for her to see the word without thinking of Pemberley and its owner. “But surely,” said she, “I may enter his colony with impunity, and visit the odd crater or two, without his perceiving me.”
The period of expectation was now doubled. Four weeks were to pass away before her uncle and aunt’s arrival. But they did pass away, and the Gardiners with their four children did at length appear at Liqua. The children, two girls of six and eight years old, and two younger boys, were to be left under the particular care of their cousin Jane, who was the general favourite, and whose motherliness and sweetness of temper exactly adapted her for attending to them in every way—teaching them, playing with them, and loving them.
The Gardiners stayed only one night at Liqua, and set off the next morning with Elizabeth in pursuit of novelty and amusement. One enjoyment was certain—that of suitableness of companions; a suitableness which comprehended health and temper to bear inconveniences—cheerfulness to enhance every pleasure—and affection and intelligence, which might supply it among themselves if there were disappointments abroad.
It is not the object of this work to give a description of New Arabia, nor of any of the remarkable places through which their route thither lay; the cryovolcano at Cairo, the string of small impact craters between Jericho and Monoqua, the Earthtown at Kingston, the methane river and delta, etc. are sufficiently known. A small part of New Arabia is all the present concern. To the little town of Alpaca, the scene of Gardiner Mater’s former residence, and where she had lately learned some acquaintance still remained, they bent their steps, after having seen all the principal wonders of the country; and Elizabeth found from her aunt that Pemberley was situated within five miles of Alpaca. It was not in their direct road, nor more than a mile or two out of it. In talking over their route the evening before, Gardiner Mater expressed an inclination to see the place again. Gardiner Pater declared his willingness, and Elizabeth was applied to for her approbation.
“My love, should not you like to see a place of which you have heard so much?” said her aunt; “a place, too, with which so many of your acquaintances are connected. Wickham passed all his youth there, you know.”
Elizabeth was distressed. She felt that she had no business at Pemberley, and was obliged to assume a disinclination for seeing it. She must own that she was tired of seeing great houses; after Rosings, she had no interest in seeing more elegant furnishings, robot servants or electronic picture windows.
Gardiner Mater abused her stupidity. “If it were merely a technologically enhanced and well-furnished house,” said she, “I should not care about it myself; but the grounds are delightful. They have some of the only real woods in the Colony.”
Elizabeth said no more—but her mind could not acquiesce. The possibility of meeting Squire Darcy, while viewing the place, instantly occurred. It would be dreadful! She blushed at the very idea, and thought it would be better to speak openly to her aunt than to run such a risk. But against this there were objections; and she finally resolved that it could be the last resource, if her private inquiries to the absence of the family were unfavourably answered.
Accordingly, when she retired at night, she asked the innkeeper whether Pemberley were not a very fine place? What was the name of its proprietor? And, with no little alarm, whether the family were down for the cold season? A most welcome negative followed the last question—and her alarms now being removed, she was at leisure to feel a great deal of curiosity to see the house herself; and when the subject was revived the next morning, and she was again applied to, could readily answer, and with a proper air of indifference, that she had not really any dislike to the scheme. Pemberley, therefore, they were to go.


Chapter 43
Elizabeth, as they drove along, watched for the first appearance of Pemberley Woods with some perturbation; and when at length they turned in at the lodge, her spirits were in a high flutter.
The park was very large, and contained, as Gardiner Mater had promised, one of the only real woods on Titan. They entered it in one of its lowest points, and drove for some time through a beautiful park stretching over a wide extent. The woods were planted with fig trees, monkey-puzzles and sausage trees interspersed with flowering cacti, creeping jenny and other wild blossoms, all genetically engineering to thrive in Titan’s terraformed climate, though maintained only at considerable cost.  The effect was reminiscent of the English woods that Elizabeth had experienced in the holodeck at Rosings, though the trees were less leafy and the air was dryer. Even the ground under the camels’ feet, composed of a mixture of common black sand and rare white sand, evoked an English soil.
Elizabeth’s mind was too full for conversation, but she saw and admired every remarkable spot and point of view. They gradually ascended for half-a-mile, and then found themselves at the top of a considerable eminence, where the wood ceased, and the eye was instantly caught by Pemberley House, situated on the opposite side of a valley, into which the road with some abruptness wound. It was a large, handsome stone building, standing well on rising ground, and backed by a large holographic projection of verdant hills; and in front, a stream was artfully constructed to look natural, its banks neither formal nor falsely adorned. Elizabeth was delighted. She had never seen a place more exotically Earth-like or more beautiful. They were all of them warm in their admiration; and at that moment she felt that to be mistress of Pemberley might be something!
They descended the hill, crossed the bridge, and drove to the door; and, while examining the nearer aspect of the house, all her apprehension of meeting its owner returned. She dreaded lest the innkeeper had been mistaken. On knocking at the door, they were admitted by an elderly woman who introduced herself as Squire Darcy’s maternal grandmother, Reynolds Mater. They followed her into the dining-parlour. It was a large, well-proportioned room, handsomely fitted up. Elizabeth, after slightly surveying it, went to a window to enjoy its prospect. The hill, crowned with wood, which they had descended, receiving increased abruptness from the distance, was a beautiful object. Every disposition of the ground was good; and she looked on the whole scene, the river, the trees scattered on its banks and the winding of the valley, as far as she could trace it, with delight. As they passed into other rooms these objects were taking different positions; but from every window there were beauties to be seen. The rooms were lofty and handsome, and their furniture suitable to the fortune of its proprietor; but Elizabeth saw, with admiration of his taste, that it was neither gaudy nor uselessly fine; with less of splendour, and more real elegance, than the furniture of Rosings.
“And in this place,” thought she, “I might have been enscounced! With these rooms I might now have been familiarly acquainted! Instead of viewing them as a stranger, I might have rejoiced in them as my own son’s future estate, and welcomed to them as visitors my uncle and aunt. But no,”—recollecting herself—”that could never be; my uncle and aunt would have been lost to me; I should not have been allowed to invite them.”
This was a lucky recollection—it saved her from something very like regret.
She longed to inquire of Reynolds Mater whether her grandson were really absent, but had not the courage for it. At length however, the question was asked by her uncle; and she turned away with alarm, while Reynolds Mater replied that he was, adding, “But we expect him tomorrow, with a large party of friends.” How rejoiced was Elizabeth that their own journey had not by any circumstance been delayed a day!
Her aunt now called her to look at a picture window. She approached and saw a scene with two little boys playing cowboys and aliens on the lawn outside. It was Squire Wickham and Squire Darcy, explained Reynolds Mater, recorded about twenty years ago.
Her aunt said they were adorable, and commented how wonderful it must be to savor this idyllic scene of the past whenever you glanced out of a window.
Another picture window showed a young man in the winner’s circle after a camel race, and Reynolds Mater told them it was Squire Wickham, the son of her Darcy Pater’s estate manager, who had been brought up by him at his own expense. “He is now gone into the army,” she added; “but I am afraid he has turned out very wild.”
Gardiner Mater looked at her niece with a smile, but Elizabeth could not return it.
“And that,” said Reynolds Pater, pointing to another of the picture windows, “is my grandson giving a speech in the Senate. It was taken at the same time as the other—about eight years ago.”
“I have heard much of your grandson’s fine person,” said Gardiner Mater, looking at the picture window; “it is a handsome face. But, Lizzy, you can tell us whether or not he has changed.”
Reynolds Mater respect for Elizabeth seemed to increase on this intimation of her knowing her grandson.
“Does that young lass know Fitzwilliam?”
Elizabeth coloured, and said: “A little.”
“And do not you think him a very handsome gentleman?” 
“Yes, very handsome.”
“I am sure I know none so handsome. In the gallery up stairs you will see some finer, larger picture windows with scenes from the Earth and various colonies, as well as oil paintings of everyone in the family. This room was Darcy Pater’s favourite room, and these picture windows are just as they used to be then. He was very fond of them.”
Reynolds Mater then directed their attention to one of Miss Darcy, taken when she was only eight years old, playing with a pet pig.
“And is Miss Darcy as genetically well-endowed as her brother?” said Gardiner Mater.
“Oh! Yes—the most nubile young lass that ever was seen; and so accomplished!—She plays and sings all day long. In the next room is a new instrument just come down for her—a present from Fitzwilliam; she comes here to-morrow with him.”
Gardiner Pater, whose manners were very easy and pleasant, encouraged her communicativeness by his questions and remarks; Reynolds Mater, with pride and attachment, had evidently great pleasure in talking of her grandchildren.
“Is Squire Darcy much at Pemberley in the course of the year?” 
“Not so much as I could wish; but I dare say he may spend half his time here; and Georgiana is always down for the cold season.” 
“Except,” thought Elizabeth, “when she goes to Mendelsberg.” 
“If Squire Darcy would breed, you might see more of him.” 
“Yes, but I do not know when that will be. I do not know who is good enough for him to breed with.” 
Gardiner Pater and Gardiner Mater smiled. Elizabeth could not help saying, “It is very much to his credit, I am sure, that you should think so.” 
“I say no more than the truth, and everybody will say that knows him,” replied the other. Elizabeth put this down to a grandmother’s natural bias, but she listened with astonishment as Reynolds Mater added, “I have never known a cross word from him in my life. Even as a two-year-old he was more temperate than terrible.” 
This was praise, of all others most extraordinary, most opposite to her ideas. That he was not a good-tempered man had been her firmest opinion. Her keenest attention was awakened; she longed to hear more, and was grateful to her uncle for saying: “There are very few people of whom so much can be said. You are lucky in having such a grandson.” 
“Yes, I know I am. If I were to go through the world, I could not meet with a better. But I have always observed, that they who are good-natured when children, are good-natured when they grow up; and he was always the sweetest-tempered, most generous-hearted boy in the world.” 
Elizabeth almost stared at her. “Can this be Squire Darcy?” thought she.
“His father was an excellent man,” said Gardiner Pater.
“Yes, that he was indeed; and his son will be just like him—just as affable to the poor.” 
Elizabeth listened, wondered, doubted, and was impatient for more. Reynolds Mater could interest her on no other point. She related the subjects of other pictures windows, the dimensions of the rooms, and the price of the furniture, in vain. Gardiner Pater, highly amused by the kind of family prejudice to which he attributed her excessive commendation of her grandson, soon led again to the subject; and she dwelt with energy on his many merits as they proceeded together up the great staircase.
“He is the best of the Colony’s hereditary Governors,” said she, “that ever lived; not like other wild young men nowadays, who think of nothing but themselves. There is not one person in the government but will give him a good name. Some people call him proud; but I am sure I never saw anything of it. To my fancy, it is only because he does not rattle away like other young men.”
“In what an amiable light does this place him!” thought Elizabeth.
“This fine account of him,” whispered her aunt as they walked, “is not quite consistent with his behaviour to our poor friend.”
“Perhaps we might be deceived.”
“That is not very likely; our authority was too good.” 
On reaching the spacious lobby above they were shown into a very pretty sitting-room, lately fitted up with greater elegance and lightness than the apartments below; and were informed that it was but just done to give pleasure to Miss Darcy, who had taken a liking to the room when last at Pemberley.
“He is certainly a good brother,” said Elizabeth, as she walked towards one of the windows.
Reynolds Mater anticipated Miss Darcy’s delight, when she should enter the room. “And this is always the way with him,” she added. “Whatever can give his sister any pleasure is sure to be done in a moment. There is nothing he would not do for her.”
The picture-gallery, and two or three of the principal bedrooms, were all that remained be shown.
In the latter were impressive views in the picture windows of beautiful scenery from every continent of the Earth, as well as scenes from Ganymede, Callisto and Earth’s moon.  These, Reynolds Mater explained, were not on five minute loops like the small picture windows downstairs, but rather were stored with several years of footage that showed the natural changes of their locations in real time.
In the gallery there were many family portraits done in oil, but they could have little to fix the attention of a stranger. Elizabeth walked in quest of the only face whose features would be known to her. At last it arrested her—and she beheld a striking resemblance to Squire Darcy, with such a smile over the face as she remembered to have sometimes seen when he looked at her. She stood several minutes before the picture, in earnest contemplation, and returned to it again before they quitted the gallery. Reynolds Mater informed them that it had been painted in his father’s lifetime.
There was certainly at this moment, in Elizabeth’s mind, a more gentle sensation towards the original than she had ever felt at the height of their acquaintance. The commendation bestowed on him by Reynolds Mater was of no trifling nature. Who knew his character better than his own grandmother? Every idea that had been brought forward by the Reynolds Mater was favourable to his character, and as she stood before the canvas on which he was represented, and fixed his eyes upon herself, she thought of his regard with a deeper sentiment of gratitude than it had ever raised before; she remembered its warmth, and softened its impropriety of expression.
When all of the house that was open to general inspection had been seen, they returned downstairs, and, taking leave of Reynolds, were consigned over to the robot gardener, who met them at the hall-door.
As they walked across the hall towards the river, Elizabeth turned back to look again; her uncle and aunt stopped also, and while the former was conjecturing as to size of the estate’s robotic staff, the owner of it himself suddenly came forward from the road, which led behind it to the stables.
They were within twenty yards of each other, and so abrupt was his appearance, that it was impossible to avoid his sight. Their eyes instantly met, and the cheeks of both were overspread with the deepest blush. He absolutely started, and for a moment seemed immovable from surprise; but shortly recovering himself, advanced towards the party, and spoke to Elizabeth, if not in terms of perfect composure, at least of perfect civility.
She had instinctively turned away; but stopping on his approach, received his compliments with an embarrassment impossible to be overcome. Had his first appearance, or his resemblance to the picture they had just been examining, been insufficient to assure the other two that they now saw Squire Darcy, the robot gardener’s greeting confirmed it. They stood a little aloof while he was talking to their niece, who, astonished and confused, scarcely dared lift her eyes to his face, and knew not what answer she returned to his civil inquiries after her family. Amazed at the alteration of his manner since they last parted, every sentence that he uttered was increasing her embarrassment; and every idea of the impropriety of her being found there recurring to her mind, the few minutes in which they continued were some of the most uncomfortable in her life. Nor did he seem much more at ease; when he spoke, his accent had none of its usual sedateness; and he repeated his inquiries as to the time of her having left Liqua, and of her having stayed in New Arabia, so often, and in so hurried a way, as plainly spoke the distraction of his thoughts.
At length every idea seemed to fail him; and, after standing a few moments without saying a word, he suddenly recollected himself, and took leave.
The others then joined her, and expressed admiration of his figure; but Elizabeth heard not a word, and wholly engrossed by her own feelings, followed them in silence. She was overpowered by shame and vexation. Her coming there was the most unfortunate, the most ill-judged thing in the world! How strange it must appear to him! In what a disgraceful light might it not strike so vain a man! It might seem as if she had purposely thrown herself in his way again! Oh! Why did she come? Or, why did he thus come a day before he was expected? Had they been only ten minutes sooner, they should have been beyond the reach of his discrimination; for it was plain that he was that moment arrived—that moment alighted from his camel carriage. She blushed again and again over the perverseness of the meeting. And his behaviour, so strikingly altered—what could it mean? That he should even speak to her was amazing!—but to speak with such civility, to inquire after her family! Never in her life had she seen his manners so little dignified, never had he spoken with such gentleness as on this unexpected meeting. What a contrast did it offer to his last address at the stables of Reverend Collins, when he put his letter into her hand! She knew not what to think, or how to account for it.
They had now entered a beautiful walk by the side of the water, and every step was bringing forward a nobler fall of ground, or a finer reach of the woods to which they were approaching; but it was some time before Elizabeth was sensible of any of it; and, though she answered mechanically to the repeated appeals of her uncle and aunt, and seemed to direct her eyes to such objects as they pointed out, she distinguished no part of the scene. Her thoughts were all fixed on that one spot of Pemberley House, whichever it might be, where Squire Darcy then was. She longed to know what at the moment was passing in his mind—in what manner he thought of her, and whether, in defiance of everything, she was still dear to him. Perhaps he had been civil only because he felt himself at ease; yet there had been that in his voice which was not like ease. Whether he had felt more of pain or of pleasure in seeing her she could not tell, but he certainly had not seen her with composure. Of course, she did not wish him to be in love with her, she could not wish that his mind was still troubled by a deviant passion, but it was tempting to imagine that his inappropriate ardency could cool to something like respectable admiration and affection.
At length, however, the remarks of her companions on her absence of mind aroused her, and she felt the necessity of appearing more like herself.
They entered the woods, and bidding adieu to the river for a while, ascended some of the higher grounds; when, in spots where the opening of the trees gave the eye power to wander, were many charming views of the valley, the distant holographic hills, and occasionally part of the stream. Gardiner Pater expressed a wish of going round the whole park, but feared it might be beyond a walk. With a triumphant smile they were told that it was ten miles round. That settled the matter; and they pursued the accustomed circuit; which brought them again, after some time, in a descent among hanging woods, to the edge of the water, and one of its narrowest parts. They crossed it by a simple bridge, in character with the general air of the scene; it was a spot less adorned than any they had yet visited; and the rocky canyon allowed room only for the stream, and a narrow walk amidst the rough coppice-wood which bordered it. Elizabeth longed to explore its windings; but when they had crossed the bridge, and perceived their distance from the house, Gardiner Mater, who was not a great walker, could go no farther, and thought only of returning to the camel cart as quickly as possible. Her niece was, therefore, obliged to submit, and they took their way towards the house on the opposite side of the river, in the nearest direction; but their progress was slow, for Gardiner Pater, was so much engaged in watching the occasional appearance of some fish in the water that he advanced but little. Whilst wandering on in this slow manner, they were again surprised, and Elizabeth’s astonishment was quite equal to what it had been at first, by the sight of Squire Darcy approaching them, and at no great distance. The walk here being here less sheltered than on the other side, allowed them to see him before they met. Elizabeth, however astonished, was at least more prepared for an interview than before, and resolved to appear and to speak with calmness, if he really intended to meet them. For a few moments, indeed, she felt that he would probably strike into some other path. The idea lasted while a turning in the walk concealed him from their view; the turning past, he was immediately before them. With a glance, she saw that he had lost none of his recent civility; and, to imitate his politeness, she began, as they met, to admire the beauty of the place; but she had not got beyond the words “delightful,” and “charming,” when some unlucky recollections obtruded, and she fancied that praise of Pemberley from her might be mischievously construed. Her colour changed, and she said no more. She would not allow him to suppose that she was imagining Pemberley as a playground for her children.
Gardiner Mater was standing a little behind; and on her pausing, he asked her if she would do him the honour of introducing him to her friends. This was a stroke of civility for which she was quite unprepared; and she could hardly suppress a smile at his being now seeking the acquaintance of some of those very people against whom his pride had revolted in his offer to herself. “What will be his surprise,” thought she, “when he knows who they are? He takes them now for people of good pedigree.”
The introduction, however, was immediately made; and as she named their relationship to herself, she stole a sly look at him, to see how he bore it, and was not without the expectation of his decamping as fast as he could from such disgraceful companions. That he was surprised by the connection was evident; he sustained it, however, with fortitude, and so far from going away, turned back with them, and entered into conversation with Gardiner Pater. Elizabeth could not but be pleased, could not but triumph. It was consoling that he should know she had some relations for whom there was no need to blush, though were Gardiner Pater to succumb to an asthma attack, his inferior genetics could not be concealed. She listened most attentively to all that passed between them, and gloried in every expression, every sentence of her uncle, which marked his intelligence, his taste, or his good manners.
The conversation soon turned upon fish, since Gardiner Pater had never before seen fish swimming freely in a stream and was fascinated with their beauty and grace. He heard Squire Darcy invite him, with the greatest civility, to have a turn in his holodeck, which was attached to a large pool in the basement of Pemberley. Gardiner Pater was urged to visit at his leisure to experience snorkelling in the Maldives, the Great Barrier Reef or the Galapagos Islands. Gardiner Mater, who was walking arm-in-arm with Elizabeth, gave her a look expressive of wonder. Elizabeth said nothing, but it gratified her exceedingly; the compliment must be all for herself. Her astonishment, however, was extreme, and continually was she repeating, “Why is he so altered? From what can it proceed? It cannot be for me—it cannot be for my sake that his manners are thus softened. My reproofs at Hanoi could not work such a change as this. It is impossible that he should still love me.” And though she repeated to herself that such enduring love in the face of such rejection was impossible, and undesirable, she could not help but find the possibility extremely flattering.
After walking some time in this way, the two ladies in front, the two gentlemen behind, on resuming their places, after descending to the brink of the river for the better inspection of some curious water-plant, there chanced to be a little alteration. It originated in Gardiner Mater, who, fatigued by the exercise of the morning, found Elizabeth’s arm inadequate to her support, and consequently preferred her co-parent’s. Squire Darcy took her place by her niece, and they walked on together. After a short silence, the lass spoke first. She wished him to know that she had been assured of his absence before she came to the place, and accordingly began by observing, that his arrival had been very unexpected—”for your grandmother,” she added, “informed us that you would certainly not be here till tomorrow; and indeed, before we left Beihai, we understood that you were not immediately expected in the country.”
