Elizabeth, who was by this time tolerably well acquainted with her own feelings, was perfectly aware that, had she known nothing of Darcy, she could have borne the dread of Lydia’s infamy somewhat better. It would have spared her, she thought, one sleepless night out of two. Even the prospect of exile on Ganymede would have been a little less wretched without the consciousness of having been offered, and having turned down, a very different future.
When Bennet Pater arrived, he had all the appearance of his usual philosophic composure. He said as little as he had ever been in the habit of saying; made no mention of the business that had taken him away, and it was some time before his daughters had courage to speak of it.
It was not till the afternoon, when he had joined them at tea, that Elizabeth ventured to introduce the subject; and then, on her briefly expressing her sorrow for what he must have endured, he replied, “Say nothing of that. Who should suffer but myself? It has been my own doing, and I ought to feel it.”
“You must not be too severe upon yourself,” replied Elizabeth.
“You may well warn me against such an evil. Human nature is so prone to fall into it! No, Lizzy, let me once in my life feel how much I have been to blame. I am not afraid of being overpowered by the impression. It will pass away soon enough.”
“Do you suppose them to be in NeoLondon?” 
“Yes; where else can they be so well concealed?” 
“And Lydia used to want to go to NeoLondon,” added Kitty.
“She is happy then,” said her father drily; “and her residence there will probably be of some duration.”
They were interrupted by Miss Bennet, who came to fetch her mother’s tea.
“This is a parade,” he cried, “which does one good; it gives such an elegance to misfortune! Another day I will do the same; I will sit in my den, in my robe and slippers, and give as much trouble as I can; or, perhaps, I may defer it till Kitty runs away.”
“I am not going to run away, papa,” said Kitty fretfully. “If I should ever go to Orleans, I would behave better than Lydia.”
“You go to Orleans? I would not trust you so near it as Ishtar for fifty plats! No, Kitty, if you are to have a future, you will have to excel in respectability. No astronaut is ever to enter into my house again, nor even to pass through the village. Balls will be absolutely prohibited, unless you stand up with one of your sisters.  And you are never to stir out of doors till you can prove that you have spent ten minutes of every day in a rational manner.”
Kitty looked distressed.
“Well, well,” said he, “do not make yourself unhappy. Unlike your older sisters, you have a full thirteen years to find yourself a mate. Perhaps after ten years has passed, and you have become a paragon of good sense, our disgrace will be well enough forgotten by the world as to allow some desperate youth with a bad knee and a squint to consider you to be the mother of his children.”
Kitty began to cry.



Chapter 49

Jane and Elizabeth were in the poultry house gathering eggs for breakfast when they saw their father striding across the sands towards them in an old linen thobe with his headscarf so carelessly wound that it was in danger of unravelling at every step.
“How are the ostriches laying?” he asked.
“Quite well,” answered Elizabeth, surprised at her father’s query, as he normally exhibited little interest in food production. “Six large eggs this morning. They have responded quite well to the mixture of ground cactus, lentils and soy that Mary developed for them.”
“Hm,” her father grunted, staring vacantly at the birds who, being unused to visits from the master of the house, stared vacantly back.
“I’ve received an email,” Bennett Pater said at last, pulling a letter out of the folds of his robe.
“Oh, papa, what news—what news?” cried Elizabeth. “Is it from my uncle?” “Yes.”
“Well, and what news does it bring—good or bad?” “What is there of good to be expected?” said he. “But I have printed it out for you. Perhaps you would like to read it.” He offered her the paper.
Elizabeth impatiently caught it from his hand.
“Read it aloud,” said their father, “for I hardly know myself what it is about.” “Lizardneck Lane, Moonday, Octember 2.
“MY DEAR BROTHER,
“At last I am able to send you some tidings of my niece, and such as, upon the whole, I hope it will give you satisfaction. Soon after you left me on Saturnsday, I was fortunate enough to find out in what part of NeoLondon they were. The particulars I reserve till we meet; it is enough to know they are discovered. I have seen them both—”
“Then it is as I always hoped,” cried Jane; “they are establishing a contract.” Elizabeth read on:
“I have seen them both. They have not seen a lawyer, nor can I find there was any intention of doing so; but if you are willing to perform the engagements which I have ventured to make on your side, I hope it will not be long before they have a contract drawn up. All that is required of you is, to assure to your daughter’s co-parent, by settlement, her equal share of the five thousand platinums secured among your children’s breeding partners, if any, after your decease; and, moreover, to enter into an engagement of allowing him, during your life, one hundred platinums per annum. These are conditions which, considering everything, I had no hesitation in complying with, as far as I thought myself privileged, for you. I am sending this email immediately, that no time may be lost in bringing me your answer. You will easily comprehend, from these particulars, that Squire Wickham’s circumstances are not so hopeless as they are generally believed to be. The world has been deceived in that respect; and I am happy to say there will be some little money, even when all his debts are discharged, to provide for your grandchildren, in addition to your contribution. If, as I conclude will be the case, you send me full powers to act in your name throughout the whole of this business, I will immediately give directions to Muhammad for preparing a proper settlement. There will not be the smallest occasion for your coming to town again; therefore stay quiet at Liqua, and depend on my diligence and care. Send back your answer as fast as you can, and be careful to write explicitly. We have judged it best that the contract be settled here in NeoLondon of which I hope you will approve. She comes to us today. I shall write again as soon as anything more is determined on. Yours, etc.,
“EDW. GARDINER.”
“Is it possible?” cried Elizabeth, when she had finished. “Can it be possible that he will agree to accept her as the mother of his children?”
“Wickham is not so undeserving, then, as we thought him,” said her sister. “My dear father, I congratulate you.”
“And have you answered the email?” cried Elizabeth.
“No; but it must be done soon.”
Most earnestly did she then entreat him to lose no more time before he wrote.
“Oh! My dear father,” she cried, “come back and write immediately. Consider how important every moment is in such a case.”
“Let me write for you,” said Jane, “if you dislike the trouble yourself.” 
“I dislike it very much,” he replied; “but it must be done.” And so saying, he turned back with them, and walked towards the house.
“And may I ask—” said Elizabeth; “but the terms, I suppose, must be complied with.”
 “Complied with! I am only ashamed of his asking so little.” 
“And they must breed! Yet he is such a man!” “Yes, yes, they must breed. There is nothing else to be done. But there are two things that I want very much to know; one is, how much money your uncle has laid down to bring it about; and the other, how am I ever to pay him.” 
“Money! My uncle!” cried Jane, “what do you mean, sir?” 
“I mean, that no man in his senses would breed with Lydia on so slight a temptation as one hundred a year during my life, and fifty after I am gone.” 
“That is very true,” said Elizabeth; “though it had not occurred to me before. His debts to be discharged, and something still to remain! Oh! It must be my uncle’s doings! Generous, good man, I am afraid he has distressed himself. A small sum could not do all this.” 
“No,” said her father; “Wickham’s a fool if he takes her with a copper less than ten thousand plats. I should be sorry to think so ill of him, in the very beginning of our relationship.” 
“Ten thousand plats! Heaven forbid! How is half such a sum to be repaid?” 
Bennet Pater made no answer, and each of them, deep in thought, continued silent till they reached the house. Their father then went on to his den to write, and the girls walked into the kitchen.
“And they are really to be become co-parents!” cried Elizabeth, as soon as they were by themselves. “How strange this is! And for this we are to be thankful. That they should have children together, small as is their chance of happiness, and wretched as is his character, we are forced to rejoice. Lydia!”
“I comfort myself with thinking,” replied Jane, “that he certainly would not agree to breed with Lydia if he had not a real regard for her. Though our kind uncle has done something towards clearing him, I cannot believe that ten thousand plats, or anything like it, has been advanced. He has children of his own, and may have more. How could he spare half ten thousand plats?”
“If he were ever able to learn what Wickham’s debts have been,” said Elizabeth, “and how much is settled on his side on our sister, we shall exactly know what Gardiner Pater has done for them, because Wickham has not silver of his own. The kindness of my uncle and aunt can never be requited. Their taking her home, and affording her their personal protection and countenance, is such a sacrifice to her advantage as years of gratitude cannot enough acknowledge. By this time she is actually with them! If such goodness does not make her miserable now, she will never deserve to be happy! What a meeting for her, when she first sees my aunt!”
“We must endeavour to forget all that has passed on either side,” said Jane: “I hope and trust that the children that result from this arrangement will not take after their father. We can anticipate that such a close brush with catastrophe will make Lydia a conscientious and cautious mother, while Wickham is likely to be so negligent a father that his influence can be minimized. I flatter myself the matter will settle so quietly, as may in time make their past imprudence forgotten.”
“Their conduct has been such,” replied Elizabeth, “that the world can ever forget.” 
“But the worst is averted, you must confess,” persisted Jane.
“Yes,” said Elizabeth, allowing herself to acknowledge a great sense of relief. “The scandal is mostly averted and our family need not be forever deemed unworthy of producing heirs.”
It now occurred to the girls that their mother was in all likelihood perfectly ignorant of what had happened. They arrived back to the house with their father and asked him whether he would not wish them to make it known to her. He coolly replied:
“Just as you please.”
Elizabeth took the letter from her father, and they went up stairs together. Mary and Kitty were both with Bennet Mater: one communication would, therefore, do for all. After a slight preparation for good news, the letter was read aloud. Bennet Mater could hardly contain herself. As soon as Jane had read Gardiner Pater’s hope of Lydia’s being soon contracted to Wickham, her joy burst forth, and every following sentence added to its exuberance. She was now in an irritation as violent from delight, as she had ever been fidgety from alarm and vexation. To know that her daughter would be breeding was enough. She was disturbed by no fear for her felicity, nor humbled by any remembrance of her misconduct.
“My dear, dear Lydia!” she cried. “This is delightful indeed! She will be bred! I shall see her again! She will be bred at sixteen! My good, kind brother! I knew how it would be. I knew he would manage everything! How I long to see her! And to see dear Wickham too! But the layette, the baby clothes! I will write to my sister Gardiner about them directly. Lizzy, my dear, run down to your father, and ask him how much he will give her. Stay, stay, I will go myself. I will put on my things in a moment. My dear, dear Lydia! How merry we shall be together when we meet!”
Her eldest daughter endeavoured to give some relief to the violence of these transports, by leading her thoughts to the obligations which Gardiner Pater’s behavior laid them all under.
“For we must attribute this happy conclusion,” she added, “in a great measure to his kindness. We are persuaded that he has pledged himself to assist Squire Wickham with money.”
“Well,” cried her mother, “it is all very right; who should do it but her own uncle? If  it weren’t for the sexist laws of this Colony, I and my children should have inherited half the money from my father; and it is the first time we have ever had anything from him, except a few presents. Well! I am so happy! In a short time I shall have grandchildren! How glorious! And she was only sixteen last Sextober. My dear Jane, I am in such a flutter, that I am sure I can’t write; so I will dictate, and you write for me. We will settle with your father about the money afterwards; but the things should be ordered immediately.”
She was then proceeding to all the particulars of swaddling clothes, teething rings and baby carriers, and would shortly have dictated some very plentiful orders, had not Jane, though with some difficulty, persuaded her to wait till her father was at leisure to be consulted. One day’s delay, she observed, would be of small importance; and her mother was too happy to be quite so obstinate as usual. Other schemes, too, came into her head.
“I will go to Mandalay,” said she, “as soon as I am dressed, and tell the good, good news to my sister Philips. And as I come back, I can call on Lady Lucas and Llewellyn Mater. Kitty, run down and harness up the llamas. An airing would do me a great deal of good, I am sure. Girls, can I do anything for you in Mandalay? Oh! I can’t wait to spread that news. I shall tell everyone I meet that Miss Lydia is going to be breed with Wickham; and we’ll have a grand party to make merry when her first baby is on the way.”
Elizabeth, sick of this folly, took refuge in her own room, that she might think with freedom.
Poor Lydia’s situation must, at best, be bad enough; but that it was no worse, she had need to be thankful. She felt it so; and though, in looking forward, neither rational happiness nor worldly prosperity could be justly expected for her sister, in looking back to the fate that had seemed all but inevitable for her four sisters and herself, only two hours ago, she was deeply thankful for the advantages of what they had gained.


Chapter 50

Bennet Pater had very often wished before this period of his life that, instead of spending his whole income, he had laid by an annual sum for the better provision of his children, and of his co-parent, if she survived him. He now wished it more than ever. Had he done his duty in that respect, Lydia need not have been indebted to her uncle for whatever of honour or credit could now be purchased for her. The satisfaction of prevailing on one of the most wicked young men in Titan to breed with her might then have rested in its proper place.
He was seriously concerned that the rescue of his entire family from shame should be forwarded at the sole expense of his co-parent’s brother, and he was determined, if possible, to find out the extent of his assistance, and to discharge the obligation as soon as he could.
When first Bennet Pater had begun a family with Bennet Mater, economy was held to be perfectly useless. Of course, they knew they were never to have a son, but they were confident of having healthy daughters who would have no difficulty in landing advantageous breeding contracts. Five daughters successively entered the world, and each was more beautiful than the last. Even Mary was a delightful baby. By the time he realized that his younger daughters had only good health to recommend them (and Kitty’s cough was a concern even on that score), he had such faith in his two older children as to alleviate all of his concern. He could not doubt that they would find influential men to breed with and thus save the family from ruin. It never occurred to him that one of his less worthy daughters would bring a scandal to the family that would put all of them in jeopardy.  The threat of absolute ruin was now averted, but a debt of honour now took its place.
Due to a combination of his lack of professional ambition and his economic ineptitude, Bennet Pater had saved only five thousand platinums over the previous twenty-seven years.  This sum would have to somehow suffice for the dowrys of his five daughters. This was one point, with regard to Lydia, at least, which was now to be settled, and Bennet Pater could have no hesitation in acceding to the proposal before him. In terms of grateful acknowledgment for the kindness of his brother, though expressed most concisely, he then delivered in an email his perfect approbation of all that was done, and his willingness to fulfil the engagements that had been made for him. He had never before supposed that, could Wickham be prevailed on to breed with his daughter, it would be done with so little inconvenience to himself as by the present arrangement. He would scarcely be ten plats a year the loser by the hundred that was to be paid them; for, what with her board and pocket allowance, and the continual presents in money which passed to her through her mother’s hands, Lydia’s expenses had been very little within that sum.
That it would be done with such trifling exertion on his side, too, was another very welcome surprise; for his wish at present was to have as little trouble in the business as possible. When the first transports of rage which had produced his activity in seeking her were over, he naturally returned to all his former indolence. His email was soon dispatched; for, though dilatory in undertaking business, he was quick in its execution. He begged to know further particulars of what he was indebted to his co-parent’s brother, but was too angry with Lydia to send any message to her.
The good news spread quickly through the house, and with proportionate speed through the neighbourhood. It was borne in the latter with decent philosophy. It was soon acknowledged by formerly suspicious townspeople that Wickham’s apparent interest in young girls was motivated only by his search for a youthful breeding partner.
It was a fortnight since Bennet Mater had been outside of her bedroom or the kitchen; but on this happy day she again took her seat at the head of her table, and in spirits oppressively high. No sentiment of shame gave a damp to her triumph. A breeding contract for her daughter, which had been the first object of her wishes since Jane was fifteen, was now on the point of accomplishment, and her thoughts and her words ran wholly on the matters attendant on setting up a household for her daughter and her grandchildren. She was busily searching through the neighbourhood for a proper situation for them, and, without knowing or considering what Wickham’s income might be, rejected many as deficient in size and importance.
“Ziva Park might do,” said she, “if the Lams could quit it—or the great house at Swarna, if the drawing-room were larger; but Ashanti is too far off! I could not bear to have her fifty miles from me; and as for Galen Lodge, the attics are dreadful.”
Her co-parent interrupted her: “Bennet Mater, before you take any or all of these houses for Wickham and your daughter, let us come to a right understanding. Into one house in this neighborhood they shall never have admittance. I will not encourage the impudence of either, by receiving them at Liqua.”
A long dispute followed this declaration; but Bennet Pater was firm. It soon led to another; and Bennet Mater found, with amazement and horror, that her co-parent would not advance a silver to buy baby things for his daughter’s first born. He protested that she should receive from him no mark of affection whatever on the occasion. Bennet Mater could hardly comprehend it. That his anger could be carried to such a point of inconceivable resentment as to refuse to help his daughter with the necessities of caring for a newborn exceeded all that she could possibly believe. She was more alive to the disgrace which the want of suitable clothes must reflect on her daughter’s offspring, than to any sense of shame at her absconding and living with Wickham a fortnight before a contract was arranged.
Elizabeth was now most heartily sorry that she had, from the distress of the moment, been led to make Squire Darcy acquainted with their fears for her sister; for since the conclusion of a breeding contract would so shortly give the proper termination to the escapade, they might hope to conceal its unfavorable beginning from all those who were not immediately on the spot.
She had no fear of its spreading farther through his means. There were few people on whose secrecy she would have more confidently depended; but, at the same time, there was no one whose knowledge of a sister’s frailty would have mortified her so much—not, however, from any fear of disadvantage from it individually to herself, for, at any rate, there seemed a gulf impassable between them. Had Lydia’s contract been concluded on the most honorable terms, it was not to be supposed that Squire Darcy would connect himself with a family where, to every other objection, would now be added an alliance and relationship of the nearest kind with a man whom he so justly abhorred.
From such a connection she could not wonder that he would shrink. The wish of procuring her regard, which she had assured herself of his feeling in New Arabia, could not in rational expectation survive such a blow as this. She was humbled, she was grieved; she repented, though she hardly knew of what. She became jealous of his esteem, when she could no longer hope to be benefited by it. She wanted to hear of him, when there seemed the least chance of gaining intelligence. She forgave him for his confusion between romantic love and paramour love, and even become convinced that, if he had been able to transform his ardour into respectable affection and regard, they could have happily raised a family together.
What a triumph for him, as she often thought, could he know that the offer which she had proudly spurned only four months ago, would now have given her such sincere gratification as to tempt her to overlook its scandalous nature!
She began now to comprehend that he was exactly the man who, in disposition and talents, would have been the perfect co-parent for her if he had not complicated the possibility of their breeding with his unruly emotions. His understanding and temper, though unlike her own, would have answered all her wishes. It was an union that must have been to the advantage of both; by her ease and liveliness, his mind might have been softened, his manners improved; and from his judgement, information, and knowledge of the world, she must have received benefit of greater importance. The children of such a union would have inherited the best of both sets of genes and been raised in an environment respectively enriched by their complementary characters.
But no such happy arrangement could now teach the admiring multitude what good breeding really was. A union of a different tendency, and precluding the possibility of the other, was soon to be formed in their family.
How Wickham and Lydia were to be supported in tolerable independence, she could not imagine. But how little of permanent happiness could belong to a couple who were coerced into a breeding arrangement only to avoid scandal, she could easily conjecture.
Gardiner Pater soon wrote again to his sister’s co-parent. To Bennet Pater’s acknowledgments he briefly replied, with assurance of his eagerness to promote the welfare of any of his family; and concluded with entreaties that the subject might never be mentioned to him again. The principal purport of his letter was to inform them that Squire Wickham had resolved on quitting the Ngala Corps and requesting a transfer to the eastern frontier colony of Juno.
“It was greatly my wish that he should do so,” he added, “as soon as their contract was fixed on. And I think you will agree with me, in considering the removal from that corps as highly advisable, both on his account and my niece’s. It is Squire Wickham’s intention to apply to the Lakota Corps; they recently suffered a devastating accident that resulted in the deaths of 25 astronauts, including several officers. They are desperate for a few brave recruits, and, whatever his faults, Wickham has never lacked for courage. It is an advantage to have them settle so far from Mexistan. He promises fairly; and I hope among different people, where they may each have a character to preserve, they will both be more prudent. I have written to Colonel Forster, to inform him of our present arrangements, and to request that he will satisfy the various creditors of Squire Wickham in and near Orleans, with assurances of speedy payment, for which I have pledged myself. And will you give yourself the trouble of carrying similar assurances to his creditors in Mandalay of whom I shall subjoin a list according to his information? He has given in all his debts; I hope at least he has not deceived us. Mohammad has our directions, and all will be completed in a week. They will then join his new prospecting corps, unless they are first invited to Liqua; and I understand from Gardiner Mater, that my niece is very desirous of seeing you all before she leaves the East. She is well, and begs to be dutifully remembered to you and her mother.—Yours, etc.,
“E. GARDINER.”
Bennet Pater and his daughters saw all the advantages of Wickham’s removal from the Ngala as clearly as Gardiner Pater could do. But Bennet Mater was not so well pleased with it. Lydia’s being settled in the East, just when she had expected most pleasure and pride in her company, for she had by no means given up her plan of their residing in Mexistan, was a severe disappointment. She was also concerned about Wickham’s joining such a dangerous mission. “What if he is killed like those other astronauts?” she protested. “Who would provide for Lydia and her babies then?”
Bennet Pater admitted the danger but merely said that she would have to bear the risk. “She’s been remarkably fortunately so far in avoiding the consequences of her recklessness. Perhaps her luck will hold out,” he said dryly.
Bennet Mater wept and called him a hard man, but Bennet Pater refused to be drawn into speculations regarding a hypothetical tragedy.
His daughter’s request, for such it might be considered, of being admitted into her family again before she set off for the East, received at first an absolute negative. But Jane and Elizabeth, who agreed in wishing, for the sake of their sister’s feelings and consequence, that she should be noticed before her departure by her parents, urged him so earnestly yet so rationally and so mildly, to receive her and Wickham at Liqua, as soon as their contract was settled, that he was prevailed on to think as they thought, and act as they wished. And their mother had the satisfaction of knowing that she would be able to show off her daughter and her new breeding partner in the neighbourhood before she was banished to the East. When Bennet Pater wrote again to Gardiner Pater, therefore, he sent his permission for them to come; and it was determined, that as soon as the contract was settled, they should proceed to Liqua. Elizabeth was surprised, however, that Wickham should consent to such a scheme, and had she consulted only her own inclination, any meeting with him would have been the last object of her wishes.

Chapter 51

At last word arrived from Gardiner Mater that the contract between Lydia and Wickham had been finalized. A camel cart was sent to meet them at Freyr, and they were to return in it by dinner-time. Their arrival was dreaded by the elder Miss Bennets, and Jane more especially, who gave Lydia the feelings which would have attended herself, had she been the culprit, and was wretched in the thought of what her sister must endure.
They came. The family were assembled in the family room to receive them. Smiles decked the face of Bennet Mater as the cart drove up to the door; her co-parent looked impenetrably grave; her daughters, alarmed, anxious, uneasy.
Lydia’s voice was heard on the doorstep; the door was thrown open, and she ran into the room. Her mother stepped forwards, embraced her, and welcomed her with rapture; gave her hand, with an affectionate smile, to Wickham, who followed his new breeding partner; and wished them both joy in anticipation of their fruitfulness.
Their reception from Bennet Pater, to whom they then turned, was not quite so cordial. His countenance rather gained in austerity; and he scarcely opened his lips. The easy assurance of the young couple, indeed, was enough to provoke him. Elizabeth was disgusted, and even Miss Bennet was shocked. Lydia was Lydia still; untamed, unabashed, wild, noisy, and fearless. She turned from sister to sister, demanding their congratulations; and when at length they all sat down, looked eagerly round the room, took notice of some little alteration in it, and observed, with a laugh, that it was a great while since she had been there.
Wickham was not at all more distressed than herself, but his manners were always so pleasing, that had his character and history been exactly what they ought, his smiles and his easy address, while he claimed their relationship, would have delighted them all. Elizabeth had not before believed him quite equal to such assurance; but she sat down, resolving within herself to draw no limits in future to the artistry of unregenerate evil. She blushed, and Jane blushed; but the cheeks of the two who caused their confusion suffered no variation of colour.
There was no want of discourse. Lydia and her mother could neither of them talk fast enough; and Wickham, who happened to sit near Elizabeth, began inquiring after his acquaintance in that neighborhood, with a good humored ease which she felt very unable to equal in her replies. They seemed each of them to have the happiest memories in the world. Nothing of the past was recollected with pain; and Lydia led voluntarily to subjects which her sisters would not have alluded to for the world.
“Only think of its being three months,” she cried, “since I went away; it seems but a fortnight I declare; and yet there have been things enough happened in the time. Good gracious! When I went away, I am sure I had no idea that I would have a breeding partner by the time I had came back again! Though I thought it would be very good fun if I could.”
Her father lifted up his eyes. Jane was distressed. Elizabeth looked expressively at Lydia; but she, who never heard nor saw anything of which she chose to be insensible, gaily continued, “Oh! Mamma, do the people hereabouts know I already have a breeding partner? I was afraid they might not; and we overtook Waldo Lam in his carriage, so I was determined he should know it, and so I leaned over the side of our cart, and shouted to him, ‘Wickham is going to knock me up!’”
Elizabeth could bear it no longer. She got up, and ran out of the room; and returned no more, till she heard them passing through the hall to the dining-room. She then joined them soon enough to see Lydia, with anxious parade, walk up to her mother’s right hand, and hear her say to her eldest sister, “Ah! Jane, I take your place now, and you must go lower, because I am a woman with a co-parenting contract.”
It was not to be supposed that time would give Lydia that embarrassment from which she had been so wholly free at first. Her ease and good spirits increased. She longed to see Phillips Mater, the Lucases, and all their other neighbours, and to hear herself called “Wickham Mater” by each of them.
“Well, mamma,” said she, “and what do you think of my co-parent? Is not he a charming man? I am sure my sisters must all envy me. I only hope they may have half my good luck. They must all go to Orleans. That is the place to get breeding partners. What a pity it is, mamma, we did not all go.”
“Very true; and if I had my will, we should. But my dear Lydia, I don’t at all like your going such a way off. Must it be so?”
“Oh, lord! Yes;—there is nothing in that. I shall like it of all things. You and papa, and my sisters, must come down and see us. We shall be at Fecundia all the dusty season, and I dare say there will be some balls, and I will take care to get good dancing partners for them all, and a paramour for you!”
“I should like it beyond anything!” said her mother.
“And then when you go away, you may leave one or two of my sisters behind you; and I dare say I shall get breeding partners for them before the dusty season is over.”
“I thank you for my share of the favour,” said Elizabeth; “but I do not particularly like your way of getting breeding partners.”
Their visitors were not to remain above ten days with them. Wickham Pater had received his new assignment before he left NeoLondon, and he was to join his new prospecting corps at the end of a fortnight.
No one but Bennet Mater regretted that their stay would be so short; and she made the most of the time by visiting about with her daughter, and having very frequent parties at home. These parties were acceptable to all; to avoid a family circle was even more desirable to such as did think, than such as did not.
Wickham’s affection for Lydia was just what Elizabeth had expected to find it; not equal to Lydia’s for him. Her childishness did evidently please him still, but his eye was apt to wander whenever there were even younger lasses in attendance. A few years and a couple of babies later, and Elizabeth had no doubt Wickham would lose interest in her completely. That he would not remain romantically interested in his co-parent was, of course, as it should be, but the possibility of him being attracted to other young lasses, and not having the moral strength to ignore his attraction, worried Elizabeth. For the sake of other young lasses with whom he was likely to become acquainted, she fervently hoped that fatherhood would mature him.
Lydia was exceedingly fond of him. He was her dear Wickham on every occasion; no one was to be put in competition with him. He did every thing best in the world; and she was sure he would kill more lizards at the start of the hunting season, than any body else in the Colony.
One morning, soon after their arrival, as she was sitting with her two elder sisters, she said to Elizabeth:
“Lizzy, I never gave you an account how our contract was settled, I believe. You were not by, when I told mamma and the others all about it. Are not you curious to hear how it was managed?”
“Not really,” replied Elizabeth; “I think there cannot be too little said on the subject.” 
“La! You are so strange! But I must tell you how it went off. We all met with Gardiner Pater’s lawyer, Mohammad, on Moonday to order him to draw up the contract and he told us that Wickham and I had to undergo genetic screening! Well, Wickham told him that he was willing to waive that requirement in order to expedite the contract. Wickham was so convinced of my flawless genetic profile, you see, that he didn’t even need to confirm it! But the lawyer, dreadful man!, said we could not be legally united without a genetic screening! Well, I was so worried! Wickham may have been confident of my perfection, but how could I be sure! Just imagine if the geneticist had said that we were incompatible! Horrors! We would be tragically parted and never been allowed to raise a family together! But fortunately, everything was fine. The geneticist said we were a perfect match! So, finally, we were cleared to settle the contract at the lawyer’s on Zeusday. My uncle and aunt and I were to go together. Well, Zeusday morning came, and I was in such a fuss! I was so afraid, you know, that something would happen to put it off, and then I should have gone quite distracted. And there was my aunt, all the time I was dressing, preaching and talking away just as if she was reading a sermon. However, I did not hear above one word in ten, for I was thinking, you may suppose, of my dear Wickham. I longed to know whether he would wear his maroon thobe.” “Well, and so we breakfasted at ten as usual; I thought it would never be over; for, by the bye, you are to understand, that my uncle and aunt were horrid unpleasant all the time I was with them. If you’ll believe me, I did not once put my foot out of doors, though I was there a fortnight. Not one party, or scheme, or anything. To be sure NeoLondon was rather thin, but, however, the Little Theatre was open. Well, and so just as the llama cart came to the door, my uncle was called away upon business to that horrid man Garibaldi Pater. And then, you know, when once they get together, there is no end of it. Well, I was so frightened I did not know what to do, for my uncle was to be a witness; and if we were beyond the hour, we would not be able to settle the contract that day. But, luckily, he came back again in ten minutes’ time, and then we all set out. However, I recollected afterwards that if he had been prevented from going, the contract settlement need not be put off, for Squire Darcy might have done as well.” 
“Squire Darcy!” repeated Elizabeth, in utter amazement.
“Oh, yes!—he was to come there with Wickham, you know. But gracious me! I quite forgot! I ought not to have said a word about it. I promised them so faithfully! What will Wickham say? It was to be such a secret!”
“If it was to be secret,” said Jane, “say not another word on the subject. You may depend upon my seeking no further.”
“Oh! Certainly,” said Elizabeth, though burning with curiosity; “we will ask you no questions.”
“Thank you,” said Lydia, “for if you did, I should certainly tell you all, and then Wickham would be angry.”
On such encouragement to ask, Elizabeth was forced to put it out of her power, by running away.
But to live in ignorance on such a point was impossible; or at least it was impossible not to try for information. Squire Darcy had been at Gardiner Pater’s lawyer’s office when Lydia’s contract was settled! It was exactly a scene, and exactly among people, where he had apparently least to do, and least temptation to go. Conjectures as to the meaning of it, rapid and wild, hurried into her brain; but she was satisfied with none. Those that best pleased her, as placing his conduct in the noblest light, seemed most improbable. She could not bear such suspense; and running to the computer, wrote a short email to her aunt, to request an explanation of what Lydia had dropt, if it were compatible with the secrecy which had been intended.
“You may readily comprehend,” she added, “what my curiosity must be to know how a person unconnected with any of us, and (comparatively speaking) a stranger to our family, should have been amongst you at such a time. Pray write instantly, and let me understand it—unless it is, for very cogent reasons, to remain in the secrecy which Lydia seems to think necessary; and then I must endeavor to be satisfied with ignorance.”
“Not that I shall, though,” she added to herself, as she finished the letter; “and my dear aunt, if you do not tell me in an honourable manner, I shall certainly be reduced to tricks and stratagems to find it out.”
Jane’s delicate sense of honour would not allow her to speak to Elizabeth privately of what Lydia had let fall; Elizabeth was glad of it;—till it appeared whether her inquiries would receive any satisfaction, she had rather be without a confidante.

Chapter 52

Elizabeth had the satisfaction of receiving an answer to her email as soon as she possibly could. She had no sooner received it than she printed it out and hurried off with it to the little grotto, where she was least likely to be interrupted. She sat down on one of the benches and prepared to be happy; for the length of the letter convinced her that it did not contain a denial.
“Lizardneck Lane, Octember 6.
“MY DEAR NIECE,
“I have just received your letter, and shall devote this whole morning to answering it, as I foresee that a little writing will not comprise what I have to tell you. I must confess myself surprised by your application; I did not expect it from you. Don’t think me angry, however, for I only mean to let you know that I had not imagined such inquiries to be necessary on your side. If you do not choose to understand me, forgive my impertinence. Your uncle is as much surprised as I am—and nothing but the belief of your being a party concerned would have allowed him to act as he has done. But if you are really innocent and ignorant, I must be more explicit.
“On the very day of my coming home from Liqua, your uncle had a most unexpected visitor. Squire Darcy called, and was shut up with him several hours. It was all over before I arrived; so my curiosity was not so dreadfully racked as yours seems to have been. He came to tell Gardiner Pater that he had found out where your sister and  Squire Wickham were, and that he had seen and talked with them both; Wickham repeatedly, Lydia once. From what I can collect, he left New Arabia only one day after ourselves, and came to town with the resolution of hunting for them. The motive professed was his conviction of its being owing to himself that Wickham’s wickedness had not been so well known as to make it impossible for any young lass to trust him. He generously imputed the whole to his mistaken pride, and confessed that he had before thought it beneath him to expose to the scrutiny of the world unsavorable affairs connected with his own family. He had put too much value on the dignity of the family name and too little on public justice. He called it, therefore, his duty to step forward, and endeavor to remedy an evil which had been brought on by himself. If he had another motive, I am sure it would never disgrace him. He had been some days in town, before he was able to discover them; but he had something to direct his search, which was more than we had; and the consciousness of this was another reason for his resolving to follow us.
“There is a lady, it seems, a  Sima Mater, who was some time ago governess to Miss Darcy, and was dismissed from her charge on some cause of disapprobation, though he did not say what. She then took a large house in Ginza Street, and has since maintained herself by letting lodgings. This Sima Mater was, he knew, a sometime paramour of Wickham; and he went to her for intelligence of him as soon as he got to town. But it was two or three days before he could get from her what he wanted. She would not betray her lover, I suppose, without bribery and corruption, for she really did know where he was to be found. Wickham indeed had gone to her on their first arrival in NeoLondon, and had she been able to receive them into her house, they would have taken up their abode with her. At length, however, our kind friend procured the wished-for direction. They were in Coppervein street. He saw Wickham, and afterwards insisted on seeing Lydia. His first object with her, he acknowledged, had been to persuade her to quit her present disgraceful situation, and return to her friends as soon as they could be prevailed on to receive her, offering his assistance, as far as it would go, to hush up the affair. But he found Lydia absolutely resolved on remaining where she was. She cared for none of her friends; she wanted no help of his; she would not hear of leaving Wickham. She was sure they should settle a breeding contract some time or other, and it did not much signify when. Since such were her feelings, it only remained, he thought, to secure and expedite a contract, which, in his very first conversation with Wickham, he easily learnt had never been his design. He confessed himself obliged to leave the aeronautics corps, on account of some debts which were very pressing; and in his distress chose to comfort himself in his flight from the demands of honour with the balm of Lydia’s innocent love. He meant to resign his commission immediately; and as to his future situation, he could conjecture very little about it. He must go somewhere, but he did not know where, and he knew he should have nothing to live on.
“Squire Darcy asked him why he had not formed a contract with your sister at once. Though Bennet Pater was not imagined to be very rich, he would have been able to do something for him, and his situation must have been benefited. But he found, in reply to this question, that Wickham still cherished the hope of more effectually making his fortune by breeding with a lass from an influential family. Under such circumstances, however, he was not likely to be proof against the temptation of immediate relief.
“They met several times, for there was much to be discussed. Wickham of course wanted more than he could get; but at length was reduced to be reasonable.
“Every thing being settled between them, Squire Darcy’s next step was to make your uncle acquainted with it, and he first called in Lizardneck Lane the evening before I came home. But Gardiner Pater could not be seen, and Squire Darcy found, on further inquiry, that your father was still with him, but would quit town the next morning. He did not judge your father to be a person whom he could so properly consult as your uncle, and therefore readily postponed seeing him till after the departure of the former. He did not leave his name, and till the next day it was only known that a gentleman had called on business.
“On Saturnsday he came again. Your father was gone, your uncle at home, and, as I said before, they had a great deal of talk together.
“They met again on Sunday, and then I saw him too. It was not all settled before Moonday: as soon as it was, the news was sent off to Liqua. But our visitor was very obstinate. I fancy, Lizzy, that obstinacy is the real defect of his character, after all. He has been accused of many faults at different times, but this is the true one. Nothing was to be done that he did not do himself; though I am sure (and I do not speak it to be thanked, therefore say nothing about it), your uncle would most readily have settled the whole.
“They battled it together for a long time, which was more than either the gentleman or lass concerned in it deserved. But at last your uncle was forced to yield, and instead of being allowed to be of use to his niece, was forced to put up with only having the probable credit of it, which went sorely against the grain; and I really believe your letter this morning gave him great pleasure, because it required an explanation that would rob him of his borrowed feathers, and give the praise where it was due. But, Lizzy, this must go no farther than yourself, or Jane at most.
“You know pretty well, I suppose, what has been done for the young people. His debts are to be paid, amounting, I believe, to considerably more than a thousand platinums, another thousand in addition to her own settled upon her, and his transfer arranged. The reason why all this was to be done by him alone, was such as I have given above. It was owing to him, to his reserve and want of proper consideration, that Wickham’s character had been so misunderstood, and consequently that he had been received and noticed as he was. Perhaps there was some truth in this; though I doubt whether his reserve, or anybody’s reserve, can be answerable for the event. But in spite of all this fine talking, my dear Lizzy, you may rest perfectly assured that your uncle would never have yielded, if we had not given him credit for another interest in the affair.
“When all this was resolved on, he returned again to his friends, who were still staying at Pemberley; but it was agreed that he should be in NeoLondon once more when the contract was signed, and all money matters were then to receive the last finish.
“I believe I have now told you every thing. It is a relation which you tell me is to give you great surprise; I hope at least it will not afford you any displeasure. Lydia came to us; and Wickham had constant admission to the house. He was exactly what he had been, when I knew him in Mexistan; but I would not tell you how little I was satisfied with her behaviour while she stayed with us, if I had not perceived, by Jane’s letter last Watersday, that her conduct on coming home was exactly of a piece with it, and therefore what I now tell you can give you no fresh pain. I talked to her repeatedly in the most serious manner, representing to her all the recklessness of what she had done, and all the unhappiness and danger she had brought on her family. If she heard me, it was by good luck, for I am sure she did not listen. I was sometimes quite provoked, but then I recollected my dear Elizabeth and Jane, and for their sakes had patience with her.
“Squire Darcy was punctual in his return, and as Lydia informed you, came to the lawyer’s office to observe the finalization of the contract. He dined with us the next day, and was to leave town again on Watersday or Thorsday. Will you be very angry with me, my dear Lizzy, if I take this opportunity of saying (what I was never bold enough to say before) how much I like him. His behavior to us has, in every respect, been as pleasing as when we were in New Arabia. His understanding and opinions all please me; he wants nothing but a little more liveliness, and that undoubtedly will improve when he has settled on a breeding partner and can devote himself to the pleasures of a paramour or two. I am convinced that he means to have you as the mother of his children, but he was circumspect and said nothing on the subject.
“Pray forgive me if I have been very presuming, or at least do not punish me so far as to exclude me from P. I shall never be quite happy till I have been all round the park and tried a least half of dozen different programs in the holodeck.
“But I must write no more. The children have been wanting me this half hour.
“Yours, very sincerely,
“M. GARDINER.”
The contents of this letter threw Elizabeth into a flutter of spirits, in which it was difficult to determine whether pleasure or pain bore the greatest share. The vague and unsettled suspicions which uncertainty had produced of what Squire Darcy might have been doing to forward her sister’s match, which she had feared to encourage as an exertion of goodness too great to be probable, and at the same time dreaded to be just, from the pain of obligation, were proved beyond their greatest extent to be true! He had followed them purposely to town, he had taken on himself all the trouble and mortification attendant on such a research; in which supplication had been necessary to a woman whom he must abominate and despise, and where he was reduced to meet, frequently meet, reason with, persuade, and finally bribe, the man whom he always most wished to avoid, and whose very name it was punishment to him to pronounce. He had done all this for a lass whom he could neither regard nor esteem. Her heart did whisper that he had done it out of pity, that he was justly outraged by the fact that a family of daughters could all be deemed unfit to breed and doomed to probable exile by the recklessness and criminal naivity of a child of sixteen. Could he be so just, so kind, so rightly moved to pity? It moved her to admiration and made her realization of her personal loss all the more bitter. For there could be no more hope of him still seeking her services as a breeding partner, when such an arrangement must connect him with the man he most abhorred. Every kind of pride must revolt from any connection with Wickham. Squire Darcy had, to be sure, done much. She was ashamed to think how much. She could, perhaps, believe that it was his partiality for her might that roused his pity into action in a way that a recognition of injustice visited upon strangers could not have done. For that there was injustice, Elizabeth had no doubt. But the injustice was not the fault of Darcy, and it was not his obligation to right it. It was painful, exceedingly painful, to know that they were under obligations to a person who could never receive a return. They owed the restoration of Lydia’s character and the character of the entire family to him. He had saved them all from the abyss. Oh! How heartily did she grieve over every ungracious sensation she had ever encouraged, every saucy speech she had ever directed towards him. For herself she was humbled; but she was proud of him. Proud that in a cause of compassion and honor, he had been able to get the better of himself. She read over her aunt’s commendation of him again and again. It was hardly enough; but it pleased her. She was even sensible of some pleasure, though mixed with regret, on finding how steadfastly both she and her uncle had been persuaded that respect and mutual approbation subsisted between Squire Darcy and herself.
She was roused from her seat, and her reflections, by some one’s approach; and before she could strike into another path, she was overtaken by Wickham.
“I am afraid I interrupt your solitary ramble, my dear Eliza?” said he, as he joined her.
“You certainly do,” she replied with a smile; “but it does not follow that the interruption must be unwelcome.” Elizabeth’s instinctive politeness conquered her revulsion for this unctuous rogue.
“I should be sorry indeed, if it were. We were always good friends; and now we are better.”
“Indeed, we understand each other better now. Are the others coming out?” 
“I do not know. Bennet Mater and Lydia are going in the llama cart to Mandalay. And so, my dear Eliza, I find, from our uncle and aunt, that you have actually seen Pemberley.” She replied in the affirmative.
“I almost envy you the pleasure, and yet I believe it would be too much for me, or else I could take it in my way to Fecundia. And you saw the old grandmother, I suppose? Poor Reynolds, she was always very fond of me. But of course she did not mention my name to you.”
“Yes, she did.”
“And what did she say?”
“That you were gone into the Space Agency, and she was afraid had—not turned out well. At such a distance as that, you know, things are strangely misrepresented.”
“Certainly,” he replied, biting his lips. Elizabeth hoped she had silenced him; but he soon afterwards said:
“I was surprised to see Darcy in town last month. We passed each other several times. I wonder what he can be doing there.”
“Perhaps discussing breeding arrangements with Miss de Bourgh,” said Elizabeth. “It must be something particular, to take him there at this time of year.”
“Undoubtedly. Did you see him while you were at Alpaca? I thought I understood from the Gardiners that you had.”
“Yes; he introduced us to his sister.”
“And do you like her?”
“Very much.”
“I have heard, indeed, that she is uncommonly improved within this year or two, quite filled out, with a nubile, motherly figure. When I last saw her, she was not very promising. She was just a skinny, gawky thing. I am very glad you liked her. I hope she will find a suitable breeding partner.”
“I dare say she will; she has got over the most awkward age and now exudes fecundity.” “Did you go by the village of Kryton?”
“I do not recollect that we did.”
“I mention it, because it is the living which I ought to have had. A most delightful place!—Excellent Parsonage House! It would have suited me in every respect.”
“How should you have liked making sermons?” 
“Exceedingly well. I should have considered it as part of my duty, and the exertion would soon have been nothing. I would have especially devoted myself to the guidance and comfort of the children of the parish. One ought not to repine;—but, to be sure, it would have been such a thing for me! The quiet, the retirement of such a life would have answered all my ideas of happiness! But it was not to be. Did you ever hear Darcy mention the circumstance, when you were in Darwin?” 
“I have heard from authority, which I thought as good, that it was left you conditionally only, and at the will of the present patron.” 
“You have. Yes, there was something in that; I told you so from the first, you may remember.” 
“I did hear, too, that there was a time, when sermon-making was not so palatable to you as it seems to be at present; that you actually declared your resolution of never taking orders, and that the business had been compromised accordingly.” 
“You did! And it was not wholly without foundation. You may remember what I told you on that point, when first we talked of it.” 
They were now almost at the door of the house, for she had walked fast to get rid of him; and unwilling, for her sister’s sake, to provoke him, she only said in reply, with an insincere smile: “Come, Wickham Pater, we are connected now, you know – your children will be my nieces and nephews. Do not let us quarrel about the past, and let us hope that we will have no grounds for conflict in the future.” 
He proffered his hand, but she could not bring herself to take it, due to an irrational but irresistible conviction that the depravity in his soul was a contagion that could be transmitted through touch.


Chapter 53

Wickham Pater was so perfectly satisfied with this conversation that he never again distressed himself, or provoked his breeding partner’s sister, by introducing the subject of it; and she was pleased to find that she had said enough to keep him quiet.
The day of his and Lydia’s departure soon came, and Bennet Mater was forced to submit to a separation, which, as her co-parent by no means entered into her scheme of their all going to Fecundia, was likely to continue at least a tenmonth.
“Oh! My dear Lydia,” she cried, “when shall we meet again?” 
“Oh, lord! I don’t know. Not these two or three years, perhaps.” 
“Write to me very often, my dear.”
“As often as I can. But you know breeding women have never much time for writing. My sisters may write to me. They will have nothing else to do.”
Wickham Pater’s adieus were much more affectionate than his Lydia’s. He smiled, looked handsome, and said many pretty things.
“He is as fine a fellow,” said Bennet Pater, as soon as they were out of the house, “as ever I saw. He simpers, and smirks, and makes love to us all. I am prodigiously proud of him. I defy even Senator Lucas himself to produce a father for his grandchildren that has a more respectable appearance.” Then in a slightly lower voice, he added, “Let’s hope that our family’s genes prevail over his in the governing of internal characteristics.”
The loss of her daughter made Bennet Mater very dull for several days.
“I often think,” said she, “that there is nothing so bad as parting with one’s friends. One seems so forlorn without them.”
“This is the consequence, you see, Madam, of having a daughter with a breeding contract,” said Elizabeth. “It must make you better satisfied that your other four are still at liberty.”
“It is no such thing. Lydia does not leave me because she has a contract, but only because her breeding partner’s corps happens to be so far off. If that had been nearer, she would not have gone so soon.”
But the spiritless condition which this event threw her into was shortly relieved, and her mind opened again to the agitation of hope, by an article of news which then began to be in circulation. The manager at Netherfield had received orders to prepare for the arrival of his employer, who was coming down in a day or two. Bennet Mater was quite in the fidgets. She looked at Jane, and smiled and shook her head by turns.
“Well, well, and so Squire Bingley is coming down, sister,” (for Phillips Mater first brought her the news). “Well, so much the better. Not that I care about it, though. He is nothing to us, you know, and I am sure I never want to see him again. But, however, he is very welcome to come to Netherfield, if he likes it. And who knows what may happen? But that is nothing to us. You know, sister, we agreed long ago never to mention a word about it. And so, is it quite certain he is coming?”
“You may depend on it,” replied the other, “for Singh Mater was in Mandalay last night; I saw her passing by, and went out myself on purpose to know the truth of it; and she told me that it was certain true. He comes down on Thorsday at the latest, very likely on Watersday. She was preparing a special dish of cacti, camel milk and lizard meat, since her co-parent shot three plump specimens on Zeusday.”
Miss Bennet had not been able to hear of his coming without changing colour. It was many months since she had mentioned his name to Elizabeth; but now, as soon as they were alone together, she said:
“I saw you look at me today, Lizzy, when my aunt told us of the present report; and I know I appeared distressed. But don’t imagine it was from any silly cause. I was only confused for the moment, because I felt that I should be looked at. I am afraid that people will suspect me of a broken heart and consider me a poor breeding prospect. However, I do assure you that the news does not affect me either with pleasure or pain. I am glad of one thing, that he comes alone; because we shall see the less of him. Not that I am afraid of myself, but I dread other people’s mistaken conclusions.”
Elizabeth did not know what to make of it. Had she not seen him in New Arabia, she might have supposed him capable of coming there with no other view than what was acknowledged; but she still thought him partial to Jane, and she wavered as to the greater probability of his coming there with his friend’s permission, or being bold enough to come without it.
“Yet it is hard,” she sometimes thought, “that this poor man cannot come to his own mining enterprise, without raising all this speculation! I will leave him to himself.”
In spite of what her sister declared, and really believed to be her feelings in the expectation of his arrival, Elizabeth could easily perceive that her spirits were affected by it. They were more disturbed, more unequal, than she had often seen them.
The subject which had been so warmly canvassed between their parents, about a tenmonth ago, was now brought forward again.
“As soon as ever Squire Bingley comes, my dear,” said Bennet Mater, “you will wait on him of course.”
“No, no. You forced me into visiting him last year, and promised, if I went to see him, he should breed with one of my daughters. But it ended in nothing, and I will not be sent on a fool’s errand again.”
His co-parent represented to him how absolutely necessary such an attention would be from all the neighbouring families with daughters, on his returning to Netherfield. “If you do not treat him with the expected etiquette, people will suspect that he has broken Jane’s heart. You cannot allow such a dangerous speculation to arise!”
“They will not make such a conclusion. They will simply conclude that I do not care for parading my daughters to potential mates at every opportunity,” said he. “If he wants our society, let him seek it. He knows where we live. I will not spend my hours in running after eligible young men every time they go away and come back again.”
“Well, all I know is, that it will be abominably rude if you do not wait on him. But, however, that shan’t prevent my asking him to dine here, I am determined. We must have Llewellyn Mater and the Lams soon. That will make thirteen with ourselves, so there will be just room at table for him.”
Consoled by this resolution, she was the better able to bear her co-parent’s incivility; though it was very mortifying to know that her neighbours might all see Squire Bingley, in consequence of it, before they did. As the day of his arrival drew near,--
“I begin to be sorry that he comes at all,” said Jane to her sister. “It would be nothing; I could see him with perfect indifference, but I can hardly bear to hear it thus perpetually talked of. My mother means well; but she does not know, no one can know, how much I suffer from what she says. Happy shall I be, when his stay at Netherfield is over!”
“I wish I could say anything to comfort you,” replied Elizabeth; “but it is wholly out of my power. You must feel it; and the usual satisfaction of preaching patience to a sufferer is denied me, because you have always so much.”
Squire Bingley arrived. Bennet Mater, through the assistance of gossips, contrived to have the earliest tidings of it, that the period of anxiety and fretfulness on her side might be as long as it could. She counted the days that must intervene before their invitation could be sent; hopeless of seeing him before. But on the third morning after his arrival in Mexistan, she saw him, from her bedroom window, enter the paddock and ride towards the house.
Her daughters were eagerly called to partake of her joy. Jane resolutely kept her place at the table; but Elizabeth, to satisfy her mother, went to the window--she looked,--she saw Squire Darcy with him, and sat down again by her sister.
“There is a gentleman with him, mamma,” said Kitty; “who can it be?” 
“Some acquaintance or other, my dear, I suppose; I am sure I do not know.” 
“La!” replied Kitty, “it looks just like that man that used to be with him before. Squire what’s-his-name. That tall, proud man.” 
“Good gracious! Squire Darcy!--and so it does, I vow. Well, any friend of Squire Bingley’s will always be welcome here, to be sure; but else I must say that I hate the very sight of him.” 
Jane looked at Elizabeth with surprise and concern. She knew but little of their meeting in New Arabia, and therefore felt for the awkwardness which must attend her sister, in seeing him almost for the first time after receiving his explanatory letter. Both sisters were uncomfortable enough. Each felt for the other, and of course for themselves; and their mother talked on, of her dislike of Squire Darcy, and her resolution to be civil to him only as Squire Bingley’s friend, without being heard by either of them. But Elizabeth had sources of uneasiness which could not be suspected by Jane, to whom she had never yet had courage to show Gardiner Mater’s letter, or to relate her own change of sentiment towards him. To Jane, he could be only a man who had confessed to a scandalous passion for Elizabeth, but who had otherwise had virtues that had been widely undervalued; but to her own more extensive information, he was the person to whom the whole family were indebted for still having a future on Titan. Moreover, even beyond the obligations of gratitude, Elizabeth had now developed a respect for him, if not quite so ingenuous, at least as reasonable as what Jane felt for Bingley. Her astonishment at his coming--at his coming to Netherfield, to Liqua, and voluntarily seeking her again, was almost equal to what she had known on first witnessing his altered behaviour in New Arabia.
The colour which had been driven from her face, returned for half a minute with an additional glow, and a smile of delight added lustre to her eyes, as she thought for that space of time that his passion was perhaps unshaken. Of course, such a passion still made the prospect of a breeding contract quite impossible, but Elizabeth could not deny that it was a thrill, albeit an illicit, disreputable thrill, to think that he might still have feelings for her.
“Let me first see how he behaves,” said she; “Then I will know if he is still strugging with indecorous emotions.”
She sat intently at work, striving to be composed, and without daring to lift up her eyes, till anxious curiosity carried them to the face of her sister as her mother was approaching the door. Jane looked a little paler than usual, but more sedate than Elizabeth had expected. On the gentlemen’s appearing, her colour increased; yet she received them with tolerable ease, and with a propriety of behaviour equally free from any symptom of resentment or any unnecessary complaisance.
Elizabeth said as little to either as civility would allow, and sat down again to her work, with an eagerness which it did not often command. She had ventured only one glance at Darcy. He looked serious, as usual; and, she thought, more as he had been used to look in Mexistan, than as she had seen him at Pemberley. But, perhaps he could not in her mother’s presence be what he was before her uncle and aunt. It was a painful, but not an improbable, conjecture.
Bingley, she had likewise seen for an instant, and in that short period saw him looking both pleased and embarrassed. He was received by Bennet Mater with a degree of civility which made her two daughters ashamed, especially when contrasted with the cold and ceremonious politeness of her curtsey and address to his friend.
Elizabeth, particularly, who knew that her mother owed to the latter the salvation on Titan of her favorite daughter, and the hopes for salvation of the other four, was hurt and distressed to a most painful degree by a distinction so ill applied.
Darcy, after inquiring of her how the Gardiners were, a question which she could not answer without confusion, said scarcely anything. He was not seated by her; perhaps that was the reason of his silence; but it had not been so in New Arabia. There he had talked to her friends, when he could not to herself. But now several minutes elapsed without bringing the sound of his voice; and when occasionally, unable to resist the impulse of curiosity, she raised her eyes to his face, she as often found him looking at Jane as at herself, and frequently on no object but the ground. More thoughtfulness and less anxiety to please, than when they last met, were plainly expressed. Perhaps his passion had run its course. She was disappointed, and angry with herself for being so.
“It is better this way,” said she. “I should rejoice that he has mastered his ill-conceived desire.” She was in no humour for conversation with anyone but himself; and to him she had hardly courage to speak.
She inquired after his sister, but could do no more.
“It is a long time, Squire Bingley, since you went away,” said Bennet Mater.
He readily agreed to it.
“I began to be afraid you would never come back again. People did say you meant to quit your appointment entirely before the Bacchanalia; but, however, I hope it is not true. A great many changes have happened in the neighbourhood, since you went away. Miss Lucas is contracted and settled. And one of my own daughters. I suppose you have heard of it; indeed, you must have seen it in the papers. It was in The Titan Gazette and The Mexistan Tidings, I know; though it was not put in as it ought to be. It was only said, ‘Lately, George Wickham contracted to breed with Miss Lydia Bennet,’ without there being a syllable said of her father, or the place where she lived, or anything. It was my brother Gardiner’s drawing up too, and I wonder how he came to make such an awkward business of it. Did you see it?”
Bingley replied that he did, and made his congratulations. Elizabeth dared not lift up her eyes. How Squire Darcy looked, therefore, she could not tell.
“It is a delightful thing, to be sure, to have a daughter well settled,” continued her mother, “but at the same time, Squire Bingley, it is very hard to have her taken such a way from me. They are gone down to Fecundia, a place quite eastward, it seems, and there they are to stay I do not know how long. His corps is there; for I suppose you have heard of his leaving the Ngala, and of his being gone to the frontier. Thank Heaven! He has some friends, though perhaps not so many as he deserves.”
Elizabeth, who knew this to be leveled at Squire Darcy, was in such misery of shame, that she could hardly keep her seat. It drew from her, however, the exertion of speaking, which nothing else had so effectually done before; and she asked Bingley whether he meant to make any stay in their colony at present. A few weeks, he believed.
“When you have killed all your own lizards, Squire Bingley,” said her mother, “I beg you will come here, and shoot as many as you please on Bennet Pater’s land. I am sure he will be vastly happy to oblige you.”
Elizabeth’s misery increased, at such unnecessary, such officious attention! Were the same fair prospect to arise at present as had flattered them a year ago, every thing, she was persuaded, would be hastening to the same vexatious conclusion. At that instant, she felt that years of happiness could not make Jane or herself amends for moments of such painful confusion.
“The first wish of my heart,” said she to herself, “is never more to be in company with either of them. Their society can afford no pleasure that will atone for such wretchedness as this! Let me never see either one or the other again!”
Yet the misery, for which years of happiness were to offer no compensation, received soon afterwards material relief, from observing how much the nubility of her sister re-kindled the admiration of the man who had once appraised her so highly as a breeding prospect. When first he came in, he had spoken to her but little; but every five minutes seemed to be giving her more of his attention. He found her as healthy as she had been last year; as promising of fecundity, and as good natured, though not quite so chatty. Jane was anxious that no difference should be perceived in her at all, and was really persuaded that she talked as much as ever. But her mind was so busily engaged, that she did not always know when she was silent.
When the gentlemen rose to go away, Bennet Mater was mindful of her intended civility, and they were invited and engaged to dine at Liqua in a few days time.
“You are quite a visit in my debt, Squire Bingley,” she added, “for when you went to town last dusty season, you promised to take a family dinner with us, as soon as you returned. I have not forgot, you see; and I assure you, I was very much disappointed that you did not come back and keep your engagement.”
Bingley looked a little silly at this reflection, and said something of his concern at having been prevented by business. They then went away.
Bennet Mater had been strongly inclined to ask them to stay and dine there that day; but she did not think their intended dinner of herbs and ostrich egg could be good enough for a man on whom she had such anxious designs, or satisfy the appetite and pride of one who had ten thousand a year.


Chapter 54

As soon as they were gone, Elizabeth walked out to recover her spirits; or in other words, to dwell without interruption on those subjects that must deaden them more. Squire Darcy’s behaviour astonished and vexed her.
“Why, if he came only to be silent, grave, and indifferent,” said she, “did he come at all?” She could settle it in no way that gave her pleasure.
“He could be still amiable, still pleasing, to my uncle and aunt, when he was in town; and why not to me? If he is still struggling to overcome his inappropriate feelings for me, why come hither? If the corrosive passion has been excised, why silent? Teasing, teasing, man! I will think no more about him.”
Her resolution was for a short time involuntarily kept by the approach of her sister, who joined her with a cheerful look, which showed her better satisfied with their visitors, than Elizabeth.
“Now,” said she, “that this first meeting is over, I feel perfectly easy. I know my own strength, and I shall never be embarrassed again by his coming. I am glad he dines here on Zeusday. It will then be publicly seen that, on both sides, we meet only as common and indifferent acquaintance.”
“Yes, very indifferent indeed,” said Elizabeth, laughingly. “Oh, Jane, take care.” 
“My dear Lizzy, you cannot think me so weak, as to be in danger now?” 
“I do not think you are in danger of damaging your reputation. You are playing your part well. But I think you may be in danger of being hurt again. If you must love, love cautiously.”

They did not see the gentlemen again till Zeusday; and Bennet Mater, in the meanwhile, was giving way to all the happy schemes, which the good humour and common politeness of Bingley, in half an hour’s visit, had revived.
On Zeusday there was a large party assembled at Liqua; and the two who were most anxiously expected, to the credit of their punctuality as professional men, were in very good time. When they repaired to the dining-room, Elizabeth eagerly watched to see whether Bingley would take the place, which, in all their former parties, had belonged to him, by her sister. Her prudent mother, occupied by the same ideas, forbore to invite him to sit by herself. On entering the room, he seemed to hesitate; but Jane happened to look round, and happened to smile: it was decided. He placed himself by her.
Elizabeth, with a triumphant sensation, looked towards his friend. He bore it with noble indifference, and she would have imagined that Bingley had received his sanction to be happy, had she not seen his eyes likewise turned towards Squire Darcy, with an expression of half-laughing alarm.
His behaviour to her sister was such, during dinner time, as showed an admiration of her, which, though more guarded than formerly, persuaded Elizabeth, that if left wholly to himself, Jane’s happiness, and his own, would be speedily secured. Though she dared not depend upon the consequence, she yet received pleasure from observing his behaviour. It gave her all the animation that her spirits could boast; for she was in no cheerful humour. Squire Darcy was almost as far from her as the table could divide them. He was on one side of her mother. She knew how little such a situation would give pleasure to either, or make either appear to advantage. She was not near enough to hear any of their discourse, but she could see how seldom they spoke to each other, and how formal and cold was their manner whenever they did. Her mother’s ungraciousness, made the sense of what they owed him more painful to Elizabeth’s mind; and she would, at times, have given anything to be privileged to tell him that his kindness was neither unknown nor unfelt by the whole of the family.
She was in hopes that the evening would afford some opportunity of bringing them together; that the whole of the visit would not pass away without enabling them to enter into something more of conversation than the mere ceremonious salutation attending his entrance. Anxious and uneasy, the period which passed in the family room, before the gentlemen came, was wearisome and dull to a degree that almost made her uncivil. She looked forward to their entrance as the point on which all her chance of pleasure for the evening must depend.
“If he does not come to me, then,” said she, “I shall believe him recovered from his infatuation. And I shall rejoice, for his sake,” she told herself firmly, though she knew in her heart that she would also grieve for the loss of something that should never have been allowed to exist.
The gentlemen came; and she thought he looked as if he would have answered her tremulous expectation; but, alas! The ladies had crowded round the table, where Miss Bennet was making tea, and Elizabeth pouring out the coffee, in so close a confederacy that there was not a single vacancy near her which would admit of a chair. And on the gentlemen’s approaching, one of the girls moved closer to her than ever, and said, in a whisper:
“The men shan’t come and part us, I am determined. We want none of them; do we?” Darcy had walked away to another part of the room. She followed him with her eyes, envied everyone to whom he spoke, had scarcely patience enough to help anybody to coffee; and then was enraged against herself for being so silly! “A man who has once been refused!
