How could I ever be foolish enough to be concerrned about a renewal of his offer? If he is no longer suffering from the wholly inappropriate feelings that had compelled him to propose a breeding contract with me, there is no reason for him to consider me as a co-parent. Even if he has learned that there is no reason that I would not be able to give him a son, all the other objections to my family’s imperfect genes and poverty of connections remain. And if, on the other hand, he is still in the grip of a romantic obsession, I cannot but refuse him again. There is no more perilous basis for a breeding arrangement than love!” And yet, though she knew nothing worthwhile could come of it, Elizabeth could not prevent herself from hoping that Darcy would speak to her.
Her spirits lifted when she saw him bringing back his coffee cup himself; and she seized the opportunity of saying:
“Is your sister at Pemberley still?”
“Yes, she will remain there till the Bacchanalia.” 
“And quite alone? Have all her friends left her?” 
“Mizuno Mater is with her. The others have been gone on to Dharma, these three weeks.” 
She could think of nothing more to say; but if he wished to converse with her, he might have better success. He stood by her, however, for some minutes, in silence; and, at last, on the young lady’s whispering to Elizabeth again, he walked away.
When the tea-things were removed, and the mahjong-tables placed, the ladies all rose, and Elizabeth was then hoping to be soon joined by him, when all her views were overthrown by seeing him fall a victim to her mother’s rapacity for a fourth for her table. She now lost every expectation of pleasure. They were confined for the evening at different tables, and she had nothing to hope, but that his eyes were so often turned towards her side of the room, as to make him play as unsuccessfully as herself. No, she told herself sternly, if he can’t take his eyes off of me, then his emotional state is not conducive for his own happiness and cannot contribute to any future between us. But she nevertheless longed for an indication that she was in his thoughts.
Bennet Mater had designed to keep the two Netherfield gentlemen to supper; but they had business to attend to and could not be detained.
“Well girls,” said she, as soon as they were left to themselves, “What say you to the day? I think every thing has passed off uncommonly well, I assure you. The dinner was as well dressed as any I ever saw. The llama calf was roasted to a turn—and everybody said they never saw so fat a haunch. The ostrich egg curry was fifty times better than what we had at the Lucases’ last week; and even Squire Darcy acknowledged, that the lizards were remarkably well done; and I suppose he has the most sophisticated robotic cook. And, my dear Jane, I never saw you look more fertile. Llewellyn Mater said so too, for I asked her whether you did not. And what do you think she said besides? ‘Ah! Bennet Mater, we shall have her at Netherfield at last.’ She did indeed. I do think Llewellyn Mater is as good a creature as ever lived—and her nieces are very pretty behaved girls, and rather lacking in secondary sexual characteristics: I like them prodigiously.”
Bennet Mater, in short, was in very great spirits; she had seen enough of Bingley’s behaviour to Jane, to be convinced that she would get a contract with him at last; and her expectations of advantage to her family, when in a happy humour, were so far beyond reason, that she was quite disappointed at not seeing him there again the next day, to make an offer.
“It has been a very agreeable day,” said Miss Bennet to Elizabeth. “The party seemed so well selected, so suitable one with the other. I hope we may often meet again.”
Elizabeth smiled.
“Lizzy, you must not do so. You must not suspect me. It mortifies me. I assure you that I have now learnt to enjoy his conversation as an agreeable and sensible young man, without having a wish beyond it. I am perfectly satisfied, from what his manners now are, that he never had any design of breeding with me. It is only that he is blessed with greater sweetness of address, and a stronger desire of generally pleasing, than any other man.”
“You are very cruel,” said her sister, “you will not let me smile, and are provoking me to it every moment.”
“How hard it is in some cases to be believed!” 
“And how impossible in others!”
“But why should you wish to persuade me that I feel more than I acknowledge?”
 Elizabeth’s smile faded and she said with gentle seriousness, “I only hope that you will recognize the danger your heart is in. Enjoy Bingley’s agreeable manners, by all means, but temper your hopes. If he does not offer you a breeding contract, I hope you will not let it afflict you. You need to retain that natural good health and liveliness of spirits, so that another man may come to see you as a prospective mother to his children.” 
“But another man will not be Bingley,” objected her sister.
“But another man will keep you here on Titan,” said Elizabeth, “And when the time for paramours comes … Bingley will be waiting!”


Chapter 55

The next day, Elizabeth received an email informing her that the Gardiners were visiting the Philipses the following morning. She was delighted to hear this, as her mind had been heavily burdened by misgivings regarding the possible future activities of her sister’s new breeding partner and she felt that only a conference with the Gardiners could hope to relieve her feelings.
The next morning, she rose early, saddled her favorite llama, a light-footed pinto called Jacqui, and rode out to have a private discussion with her aunt and uncle.
In order to ensure discretion, she invited the Gardiners to take a stroll with her on the Philips’s grounds.

“Well, Lizzy,” Gardiner Mater said with a smile. “What is it that has roused you out of bed with such urgency this morning?”

Elizabeth was unsure how to start, but having come so far, decided that it would be foolish to waste time on pleasantries.

“First of all, I want to express my deepest gratitude for all you have done. You and, of course, Squire Darcy, have not only saved Lydia, but saved all of us from an unthinkable fate,” said Elizabeth.

“You know that we were more than happy to do so, Lizzy,” said Gardiner Mater. “You need not thank us for doing what was in our interests as much as yours.” She smiled. “We could not bear to lose you!”

“Thank you. I deeply appreciate the futures that have been preserved for us,” she continued. She took a deep breath. “But I worry that we have purchased our chances of a future on Titan at too great a cost.” She sighed. “Not to Lydia – she has come out of this much better than she deserves. But I am worried, haunted, by the possible consequences of continuing to cover up Wickham’s predilictions.”

Elizabeth paused, struggling to admit to a foreboding that she was reluctant to express and yet knew that she could not leave unexpressed.

“I am afraid that he could lead other young girls to ruin,” she said, at last. “Girls for whom no mechanism could be fabricated to save their reputations.”

Gardiner Pater looked at her with admiration and approval. “This concern of yours is evidence of a very fine moral sense,” he said. “And it is a very real concern. Fortunately I have it within my powers to relieve your mind entirely on this subject.”

“You have?” said Elizabeth with surprise.

“Squire Darcy and I had similar fears,” Gardiner Pater explained. “We both agreed that we could not be responsible for allowing Wickham to remain at liberty if that would give him the opportunity to repeat his offence with another innocent child.  After some consideration, we decided to use Darcy’s influence to arranged a private hearing with a judge, and in this way had Wickham convicted of deviancy with a minor. He was only able to preserve his freedom by agreeing to submit to a treatment that would eliminate all of his carnal desires.”

Elizabeth was shocked. “Are you referring,” she said in a low voice, “to chemical castration?”

“Chemical castration is not reliable enough,” said Gardiner Pater. “I am referring to castration.”

“Oh,” Elizabeth said in a small voice.

“The procedure will be carried out in Fecundia after his removal there. But first, a quantity of his sperm will be preserved so that he and Lydia can fulfill their duty to have a full cohort of children.”

She took a few minutes to process this new information and finally said, “Poor Lydia.” She reflected that, whereas Charlotte would no doubt have been grateful to be relieved of the duty of conceiving children the natural way, Lydia was unlikely to feel the same way.

Gardiner Pater smiled gently. “Lydia is scarcely more than a child,” he said. “She is too young for the kind of delights that Wickham has introduced her to. But she will soon be too fully occupied in raising a family to miss the pleasures of the flesh. And it is perhaps fortunate for Lydia that she is so young. She will only be in her thirties when her children are old enough to free her for other pursuits. At that time she will find ample opportunity for sampling the pleasures on offer in a frontier populated primarily by men.”

Elizabeth said nothing. She thought that perhaps Gardiner Pater had forgotten that he was talking to an innocent lass and not a mature woman, and she tried to disguise her embarrassment.

Finally she said, “Thank you for putting my mind at ease. Indeed, I no longer need to fear for the daughters of Fecundia.” She smiled, and recovered her countenance sufficiently to look Gardiner Pater in the eye.  “I truly cannot thank you enough. You and Squire Darcy have arranged everything beautifully.”

“But castration!” Gardiner Mater suddenly interjected, having mulled the thought over in her mind these last few minutes. “That is such a medieval solution! Do  you not feel some pity for a man whose indecent proclivities were not of his own choosing?”

Elizabeth felt her anger toward Wickham surge again in her breast. “It is not his proclivities that make him wicked,” she said warmly. “but the fact that he chose to indulge those proclivities at the cost of a young girl’s future. That is what makes him worthy of contempt! and punishment!”

Elizabeth’s indignation was just and Gardiner Mater felt her momentary pity for Wickham wane.

“Moreover,” Elizabeth continued, “There is every reason to believe that Lydia was not his first victim. I am greatly relieved to be assured that she will be his last.”

They walked around a small cluster of palm trees that marked the edge of Philips Pater’s grounds and turned back to the house, whereupon Gardiner Pater reflected, “How perplexing it is that Squire Darcy should want to keep you in the dark on his involvement in this matter.” The three friends continued in silence for a moment, contemplating this peculiarity.

“Though it would be remarkable for Squire Darcy to undergo all this trouble just to impress a potential breeding partner,” continued Gardiner Pater thoughtfully, “it is not inconceivable. I can understand that a man as proud as Darcy feels that he must have the very best for his children. Having once convinced himself that you were the very best possible mother, it would not be out of character for him to put a considerable amount of effort into securing his prize.

“But,” he  continued, with an air of perplexity, “To do all that he has done and then to try to prevent you from learning about it… I cannot make any sense of it.”
 
“You flatter me, uncle,” said Elizabeth, “Is it not simpler to conjecture that he really was motivated only by his own exacting conscience. Distressed with the guilt of having obscured Wickham’s true nature from the world, he felt an obligation to put things right. And  this he has done, handsomely.” She paused. “There is no reason to think that his actions have anything to do with me.”

Elizabeth felt the force of her own words; it was surely more plausible that Darcy was uncommonly scrupulous than that he would take such extraordinary pains on behalf of a lass of middling genetic endowment and indifferent accomplishments.

“You may be right,” said Gardiner Mater, looking at her niece thoughtfully. “But then again, Lizzy, you may be wrong.”



A few days after this visit, Squire Bingley called again to Liqua, and alone. His friend had left him that morning for NeoLondon, but was to return home in ten days’ times. He sat with them above an hour, and was in remarkably good spirits. Bennet Mater invited him to dine with them; but, with many expressions of concerns, he confessed that he had business at the mine to attend to.
“Next time you call,” said she, “I hope we shall be more lucky.” He should be particularly happy at any time, &c. &c.; and if she would give him leave, would take an early opportunity of waiting on them.
“Can you come tomorrow?”
Yes, he had no engagement at all for tomorrow; and her invitation was accepted with alacrity.
He came, and in such very good time that the ladies were none of them dressed. In ran Bennet Mater to her daughters’ room, in her camelhair robe and tasselled slippers, crying out,
“My dear Jane, make haste and hurry down. He is come – Squire Bingley is come. – He is, indeed. Make haste, make haste.”
“We will be down as soon as we can,” said Jane; “but I dare say Kitty is forwarder than either of us, for she went up stairs half an hour ago.”
“Oh! Hang Kitty! What has she to do with it? Come, be quick, be quick! Where is your sash, my dear?”
But when her mother was gone, Jane would not be prevailed on to go down without one of her sisters.
The same anxiety to get them by themselves was visible again in the evening. After tea, Bennet Pater retired to his den, as was his custom, and Mary went upstairs to her lute. Two obstacles of the five being thus removed, Bennet Mater sat looking and winking at Elizabeth and Catherine for a considerable time, without making any impression on them. Elizabeth would not observe her; and when at last Kitty did, she very innocently said, “What is the matter mamma? Why do you keep winking? Is the dust getting in your eye?”
“It’s nothing, child, nothing. The dust never bothers me.” She then sat still five minutes longer; but unable to waste such a precious occasion, she suddenly got up, and saying to Kitty, “Come here, my love, I want to speak to you,” took her out of the room. Jane instantly gave a look at Elizabeth which spoke her distress at such premeditation, and her entreaty that she would not give in to it. In a few minutes, Bennet Mater half-opened the door and called out,
“Lizzy, my dear, I want to speak with you.”
Elizabeth was forced to go.
“We may as well leave them by themselves, you know,” said her mother, as soon as she was in the hall. “Kitty and I are going upstairs to sit in my bedroom.”
Elizabeth made no attempt to reason with her mother, but remained quietly in the hall till she and Kitty were out of sight, then returned into the family room.
Bennet Mater’s schemes for this day were ineffectual. Bingley was every thing that was charming, except the purveyor of a breeding contract. His ease and cheerfulness rendered him a most agreeable addition to their evening party; and he bore with the ill-judged officiousness of the mother, and heard all her silly remarks with a forbearance and command of countenance particularly grateful to the daughter.
He scarcely needed an invitation to stay to supper; and before he went away, an engagement was formed,  chiefly through his own and Bennet Mater’s means, for his coming next morning to shoot lizards with Bennet Pater.
After this day, Jane said no more of her indifference. Not a word passed between the sisters concerning Bingley; but Elizabeth went to bed in the happy belief that all must speedily be concluded. Surely, Bingley’s renewed attentions meant that he had learned that the most serious objection proposed to his breeding with Jane, that Jane might not be able to give him a son, was baseless, and that he had been given permission by Darcy, to use his own judgement in deeming Jane a worthy mother to his children.
Bingley was punctual in his appointment; and he and Bennet Pater spent the morning together productively, bagging enough lizards for a feast. Bennet Pater was much more agreeable than his companion expected. With the repercussions of Lydia’s folly so nearly bringing disaster to his entire family, Bennet Pater had repented of his carefree and satirical attitude towards breeding arrangements. He now took seriously his duty to facilitate opportunities for his daughters to breed, and he was more communicative, and less eccentric, than Bingley had ever seen him. Bingley of course returned with him to dinner; and in the evening Bennet Mater’s invention was again at work to get everybody away from him and her daughter. Elizabeth, who had a letter to write, went into the kitchen for that purpose soon after tea; for as the others were all going to sit down to play monopoly, she could not be wanted to counteract her mother’s schemes.
But on returning to the family room, when her letter was finished, she saw, to her infinite surprise, there was reason to fear that her mother had been too ingenious for her. On opening the door, she perceived her sister and Bingley standing together over the hearth, as if engaged in earnest conversation; and had this led to no suspicion, the faces of both, as they hastily turned round and moved away from each other, would have told it all. Their situation was awkward enough, but hers she thought was still worse. Not a syllable was uttered by either; and Elizabeth was on the point of going away again, when Bingley, who as well as the other, had sat down, suddenly rose, and whispering a few words to her sister, ran out of the room.
Jane could have no reserves from Elizabeth, where confidence would give pleasure; and instantly embracing her, acknowledged, with the liveliest emotion, that she was the happiest creature in the world.
“Tis too much!” she added, “by far too much. I do not deserve it. Oh! Why is not everybody as happy?’
Elizabeth’s congratulations were given with sincerity, a warmth, a delight, which words could but poorly express. Every sentence of kindness was a fresh source of happiness to Jane. But she would not allow herself to stay with her sister, or say half that remained to be said for the present.
“I must go instantly to my mother,” she cried. “I would not on any account trifle with her affectionate solicitude; or allow her to hear it from anyone but myself. He is gone to my father already. Oh! Lizzy, to know that what I have to relate will give such pleasure to all my dear family! To secure my own future on Titan, to be able to help my sisters find breeding contracts … these are but the smallest part of my joy! For the greatest part is knowing that I will be able to breed with such a man, and to have such children! How shall I bear so much happiness!”
She then hastened away to her mother, who had purposely broken up the monopoly game, and was sitting upstairs with Kitty.
Elizabeth, who was left by herself, now smiled at the rapidity and ease with which the agreement was finally settled, that had given them so many previous months of suspense and vexation.
“And this,” said she, “is the end of all his friend’s anxious circumspection! Of all his sister’s falsehood and contrivance! The happiest, wisest, most reasonable end!”
In a few minutes she was joined by Bingley, whose conference with her father had been short and to the purpose.
He then came up to her and said, “Now, dearest Elizabeth, you must wish us well in our design to furnish you with a passel of nieces and nephews!”
“Indeed I do!” replied Elizabeth. “The two of you will make excellent parents, and your offspring are guaranteed to be intelligent, healthy and good-natured. I can think of no two people with a better chance at genetic compatibility!”
“She is the most motherly of creatures!” Bingley said effusively. “Nubile, graceful, kind, intelligent and empathetic. No child could hope for a better caregiver. Whatever nature begins with promise, her nurture will bring to perfection!”
Elizabeth really believed all his expectations of felicity to be rationally founded, because they had for basis the excellent understanding and the super-excellent disposition of Jane, and the intelligence, health and good looks of both that spoke of immaculate genetics.
It was an evening of no common delight to them all; the satisfaction of Miss Bennett’s mind gave a glow of such sweet animation to her face, as made her look handsomer than ever. Kitty simpered and smiled, and hoped her turn was coming soon. Bennet Mater could not give stop talking about what beautiful babies the two of them would make and how affectionate a grandmother she herself would be; and when Bennet Pater joined them at supper, his voice and manner plainly showed how really happy he was.
Not a word, however, passed his lips in allusion to it, till their visitor took his leave for the night; but as soon as he was gone, he turned to his daughter, and said,
“Jane, I congratulate you. You will be a very happy woman, and your children will have a father who is both kind and influential.”
Jane went to him instantly, kissed him, and thanked him for his goodness.
“You are a good girl,” he replied, “and I have great pleasure in thinking you will be so happily settled. I have not a doubt of the two of you breeding successfully and co-parenting cooperatively. The only danger I can foresee in raising children with a man of such easy temperament is that he will spoil the children and exceed his income in  his indulgence of them.”
“Exceed his income! My dear Bennet Pater,” cried his co-parent, “what are you talking of? Why, he has four or five thousand a year, and very likely more.” Then addressing her daughter, “Oh! My dear, dear Jane. I am so happy! I am sure I shan’t get a wink of sleep all night. I knew how it would be. I always said it must  be so, at last. I was sure you could not be so nubile for nothing! I remember, as soon as ever I saw him, when he first came to Mexistan last year, I thought how likely it was that you two should breed. Oh! He is the most genetically well-endowed young man that ever was seen!”
“And financially well-endowed too,” added her father, approvingly.
Wickham, Lydia were all forgotten. Jane was beyond competition her mother’s favorite child. And her happiness was not limited to her gratification that Jane’s future was secured; it was augmented by the anticipation that Jane’s future connections would enable her three remaining daughters to find their own preservatives from the horror of exile.
Bingley, from this time, was of course a daily visitor at Liqua; coming frequently before breakfast, and always remaining until after supper; unless when some barbarous neighbour, who could not be enough detested, had given him an invitation to dinner which he thought himself obliged to accept. Of course, the neighbours were informed that Bingley had offered a contract to Jane, but they would not entirely abandon hope for their own daughters until a genetic counselor had cleared Bingley and Jane for breeding.
Elizabeth had now but little time for conversation with her sister, but plenty of time to observe the two prospective parents together. She knew that Jane was blissfully in love with Bingley, which was right and proper, but she could also see that Bingley admired Jane immensely. Was this admiration similar to love? She thought of Darcy’s claim that no man would enter into a breeding contract with a lass that he was not attracted to. Was it true, she wondered? Bingley certainly thought very highly of Jane, enjoyed quietly gazing at her, and treated her with eager solicitation. How, Elizabeth wondered, was this different than the “love” Darcy claimed to feel for her?
Certainly, there is a difference, she told herself. Affectation, solicitude, and even attraction, were positive emotions that would bode well for the intricate cooperation that raising children together entailed. But love, especially the passionate kind of love expressed by Darcy, was a destabilizing factor in a relationship. Love invited jealousy, suspicion and even hate into the family, and passion produced turbulence.
Elizabeth wondered once again if passion could be cooled and tempered into regard, respect and rational affection. But no, she thought, passion probably just burns itself out, leaving a black hole of indifference.
Her thoughts were interrupted one evening by Jane when Bingley was occupied with business. “He has made me so happy,” she said, “by telling me that he was totally ignorant of my being in town during the last clear season. I had not believed it possible.”
“I suspected as much,” replied Elizabeth. “But how did he account for it?” 
“It must have been his sisters’ doing. They were certainly no friends to his acquaintance with me, which I cannot wonder at, since he might have chosen so much more advantageously in many respects. But when they see, as I trust they will, what robust babies I give to their brother, they will learn to be contented, and we shall be on good terms again; though we can never be what we once were to each other.” 
“That is the most unforgiving speech,” said Elizabeth, “that I ever heard you utter. Good girl! It would vex me, indeed, to see you again the dupe of Miss Bingley’s pretended regard.” 
“Would you believe it, Lizzy, that when he went to town last Nonember, he had already decided that I would be the  mother of his children. That is, until he became concerned that I would not be able to bear sons. When it occurred to him that a family with five daughters must have a genetic deficiency, he quite naturally changed his plan.” Jane sighed. “How fortunate that my father was able to let the truth be known before Bingley had made an offer to another lass!” 
“Indeed!” said Elizabeth.
“I am certainly the most fortunate creature that ever existed!” cried Jane. “Oh! Lizzy, why am I thus singled out from my family, and blessed above them all! If I could but see you as happy! If there were but such another man for you!”
“If you were to give me forty such men, I never could be so happy as you. Till I have your disposition, your goodness, I never can have your happiness. No, no, let me shift for myself; and, perhaps, if I have very good luck, I may meet with another Reverend Collins in time.”
When genetic counseling proclaimed Jane and Bingley to be compatible, the other families of Mandalay relinquished all hope of their daughters breeding with Bingley, and the Bennets were speedily pronounced to be the luckiest family in the world, though only a few weeks before, when Lydia had first run away, they had generally proved to be marked out for misfortune.


Chapter 56

One morning, about a week after Bingley’s arrangement with Jane had been formed, Bennet Mater, Elizabeth and Kitty were sitting together in the drawing-room when their attention was suddenly drawn to the window by the hum of a ferflivver; and they perceived an unusual ferflivver descending into the landing pad a short walk from the house. The ferflivver was a sporty and elegant model that they had never seen before and was a deep ruby red in color. It answered to none of their relative’s vehicles, nor to that of any paramours of Bennet Mater or Bennet Pater. As they engaged in unfruitful conjectures, they observed a lady step out, accompanied by two robot servants holding parasols over her head, doubly protecting her from the blistering sun and the curious eyes of the inhabitants of Liqua. When finally a knock came at the door and was answered, Lady Catherine de Bourgh stepped in.
They were of course all intending to be surprised; but their astonishment was beyond their expectation; and on the part of Bennet Mater and Kitty, though she was perfectly unknown to them, even inferior to what Elizabeth felt.
She entered the room with an air more unusually ungracious, made no other reply to Elizabeth’s salutation than a slight inclination of the head, and sat down without saying a word. Elizabeth had mentioned her name to her mother on her ladyship’s entrance, though no request of introduction had been made.
Bennet Mater, all amazement, though flattered by having a guest of such high importance, received her with the utmost politeness. After sitting for a moment in silence, she said very stiffly to Elizabeth,
“I hope you are well, Miss Bennet. That lady, I suppose, is your mother.” Elizabeth replied very concisely that she was.
“And that I suppose is one of your sisters.”
“Yes, madam,” said Bennet Mater, delighted to speak to a Lady Catherine. “She is my youngest girl but one. My youngest of all is already breeding, and my eldest is somewhere about the grounds, walking with a young man, with whom she will shortly be contracting.”
“You have a rather featureless patch of desert here.” “It is nothing in comparison of Rosings, my lady, I dare say; but I assure you it has more scrub for the llamas than Senator Lucas’s grounds.” 
“This must be a more inconvenient sitting room in the dusty season, with no trees in front to block the wind.” 
“In the dusty season, we mostly sit upstairs,” Bennet Mater replied, and added, “May I take the liberty of asking your ladyship whether you left Reverend Collins and Collins Mater well.” 
“Yes, very well. I saw them last night.”
Elizabeth now expected that she would produce some small gift from Catherine, perhaps her renowned camel cheese, as it seemed the only probably motive for her calling. But no gift appeared, and she was completely puzzled.
Bennet Mater, with great civility, begged her ladyship to have some cactus tea; but Lady Catherine declined resolutely, and not very politely, declined eating anything; and then, rising up, said to Elizabeth,
“Miss Bennet, there seemed to be a prettyish kind of a little grotto on one side of your grounds. I should be glad to take a turn in it, if you will favour me with your company.”
“Go, my dear,” cried her mother, “and show her ladyship the grotto. I think she will enjoy the walk.”
Elizabeth obeyed, and running into her own room for her parasol, attended her esteemed guest downstairs. Lady Catherine’s two robot servants, who had waited outside the door, joined them and continued their office of providing shade to their owner as she and Elizabeth strode over the baking sands.
They proceeded in silence, as Elizabeth was determined to make no effort for conversation with a woman who was now more than usually insolent and disagreeable.
“How could I ever think her like her nephew?” she said, as she looked in her face.
As they neared the grotto, Lady Catherine began in the following manner: - “You can be at  no loss, Miss Bennet, to understand the reason of my journey hither. Your own heart, your own conscience, must tell you why I came.” Elizabeth looked with unaffected astonishment.
“Indeed, you are mistaken, madam. I have not been at all able to account for the honour of seeing you here.”
“Miss Bennet,” replied her ladyship, in an angry tone. “you ought to know, that I am not to be trifled with. But however insincere you may choose to be, you shall not find me so. My character has ever been celebrated for its sincerity and frankness, and in a cause of such moment as this, I shall certainly not depart from it. A report of a most alarming nature reached me yesterday. I was told that not only your sister was on the point of concluding a most advantageous breeding contract, but that you, that Miss Elizabeth Bennet, would in all likelihood, be soon contracted to my nephew, my own nephew, Squire Darcy. Though I know it must be a scandalous falsehood, though I would not injure him too so much as to suppose the truth of it possible, I instantly resolved on setting off for this place, that I might make my sentiments known to you.”
“If you believed it impossible to be true,” said Elizabeth, coloring with astonishment and disdain, “I wonder you took the trouble of coming so far. What could your ladyship propose by it?”
“At once to insist upon having such a report universally contradicted.” 
“Your coming to Liqua, to see me and my family,” said Elizabeth coolly, “will rather be a confirmation of it; if, indeed, such a report is in existence.” 
“If! Do you then pretend to be ignorant of it? Has it not been industriously circulated by yourselves? Do you not know that such a report is spread abroad?” “I never heard that it was.”
“And can you likewise declare, that there is no foundation for it?” 
“l frankness with your ladyship. You may ask questions which I shall not choose to answer.” 
“This is not to be borne. Miss Bennet, I insist on being satisfied. Has he, has my nephew, offered you a breeding contract?” 
“Your ladyship has declared it to be impossible.” 
“It ought to be so; it must be so, while he retains the use of his reason. But, I have observed, that he has a certain … weakness … for young lasses. Nothing he has ever acted upon, of course, as he is a noble and honourable scion of this family, but a predilection, perhaps, that could make him vulnerable to the arts and inducements of an unscrupulous schemer. You may have preyed upon his weakness and drawn him in.” 
“If I have, I shall be the last person to confess it.” Elizabeth was hot with indignation for being suspected of using arts to encourage an unnatural attraction, but she was determined exude an icy cool.
“Miss Bennet, do you know who I am? I have not been accustomed to such language as this. I am almost the nearest relation he has in the world, and am entitled to know all his dearest concerns.”
“But you are not entitled to know mine; nor will such behavior as this ever induce me to be explicit.”
“Let me be rightly understood. This contract, to which you have the presumption to aspire, can never be yours. No, never.  It has already been arranged that Squire Darcy will breed with my daughter. Now what have you to say?”
“Only this; that if that is so, you can have no reason to suppose he will make an offer to me.”
Lady Catherine hesitated for a moment, and then replied: “The arrangement between them is of a peculiar kind. From their infancy, they have been intended for each other. It was the favourite wish of his mother, as well as of hers. While in their cradles, we planned the union: and now, at the moment when the wishes of both would be accomplished by their breeding together, to be prevented by a young woman of inferior genetics, of no importance in the world, and wholly unallied to any influential family! Do you pay no regard to the wishes of his friends? To his tacit agreement with Miss de Bourgh? Are you lost to every feeling of propriety and delicacy? Have you not heard me say that from his earliest hours he was destined for his cousin?” 
“Yes, and I had heard it before. But what is that to me? If there is no other objection to my breeding with your nephew, I shall certainly not be kept from it by knowing that his mother and aunt wished him to breed with Miss de Bourgh. You both did as much as you could in planning the contract. Its completion depended on others. If Squire Darcy is neither by honour nor inclination confined to his cousin, why is not he to make another choice? And if I am that choice, why may not I accept him?” 
“Because honour, decorum, prudence, nay, interest, forbid it. Yes, Miss Bennet, interest; for do not expect to be noticed by his family or friends, if you willfully act against the inclinations of all. You will be censured, slighted, and despised, by everyone connected with him. Your alliance will be a disgrace; your name will never even be mentioned by any of us.” 
“These are heavy misfortunes,” replied Elizabeth. “But, as influential as I know you are are, I believe that Darcy has his own independent influence, and the co-parent of Squire Darcy must have such access to the favors and benefits of the ruling class that she could, upon the whole, have no cause to worry, either for her own future or the future of those she loves.” 
“Obstinate, headstrong girl! I am ashamed of you! Is this your gratitude for my attentions to you during the last clear season? Is nothing due to me on that score? Let us sit down. You are to understand, Miss Bennet, that I came here with the determined resolution of carrying my purpose; nor will I be dissuaded from it. I have not been used to submit to any person’s whims. I have not been in the habit of brooking disappointment.” 
“That will make your ladyship’s situation at present more pitiable; but it will have no effect on me.” 
“I will not be interrupted. Hear me in silence. My daughter and my nephew are formed for each other. They are descended from forebears with the highest genetic quality – generations without the most trivial genetic deficiency. Their fortune on both sides is splendid. They are destined for each other by the voice of every member of their respective houses; and what is to divide them? The upstart pretensions of a young lass without connections or fortune whose family is riddled with genetic flaws. Is this to be endured! But it must not, shall not be. If you were sensible of your own good, you would not wish to breed above your natural class.” 
“In breeding with your nephew, I should not consider myself as quitting that class. He is a flawless specimen of masculinity; but my DNA is as free from defects as his own; so far we are equal.” 
“To look at your phenotype, one might think so. However, the evidence of your family suggests otherwise. Look at your mother! Look at your uncles and aunts! Do not imagine me ignorant of their conditions.” 
“At least I am able to display all the signs of robust good health,” said Elizabeth, “which is more than you can claim for your daughter.” 
“My daughter is descended from the best bloodlines on Titan,” replied Lady Catherine, bristling. “Her relative fraility is due to the fact that she shared her  mother’s womb with a twin brother, and does not reflect on her DNA.” 
“Perhaps, but perhaps Darcy has reason to believe that I would be more able to produce healthy Darcy descendents than she is.” 
“Rubbish!” Lady Catherine announced imperiously. “Tell me once for all, have you and Darcy agreed to enter into a breeding contract?” 
Though Elizabeth would not, for the mere purpose of obliging Lady Catherine, have answered this question, she could not but say, after a moment’s deliberation: “We have not.”
Lady Catherine seemed pleased.
“And will you promise me, never to enter into such an agreement?” 
“I will make no promise of the kind.”
“Miss Bennet I am shocked and astonished. I expected to find a more reasonable young lass. But do not deceive yourself into a belief that I will ever recede. I shall not go away till you have given me the assurance I require.”
“And I certainly never shall give it. I am not to be intimidated into anything so wholly unreasonable. Your ladyship wants Squire Darcy to breed with your daughter; but would my giving you the wished-for promise make a contract between them at all more probable? Supposing him to believe that I would make a better mother to his children than your daughter, would my refusing to accept his contract make him wish to offer it to his cousin? If he believes me to be superior in this regard to your daughter, then you can be sure that there are other prospects that he would find equally preferable. And for her part, if your daughter is not able to keep the interest of a man like Darcy, who has the security of status to be able choose on the basis of genetic quality alone, I am sure there are other prospects who could be persuaded to accept a little less vigor in a breeding partner in return for the social advantages that would accrue from entering into a contract with her.”
“Impertinent girl!” Lady Catherine scolded. “My desire for Darcy to contract with my daughter is based on an impartial evaluation of the interests of both and the dignity of the family.”
“Allow me to say, Lady Catherine, that the arguments with which you have supported this extraordinary application have been as frivolous as the application was ill-judged. You have widely mistaken my character, if you think I can be worked on by such persuasions as these. How far your nephew might approve of your interference in his affairs, I cannot tell; but you have certainly no right to concern yourself in mine. I must beg, therefore, to be importuned no farther on the subject.”
“Not so hasty, if you please. I have by no means done. To all the objections I have already urged, I have still another to add. I am no stranger to the particulars of your youngest sister’s infamous escapade. I know it all; that the young man’s contract with her was a patched-up business, at the expense of your father and uncles. The artfulness of your family of vixens knows no limits. And she only 16 years old! Is such a girl to be the aunt of my nephew’s children? Is her breeding partner, the son of his late father’s property manager, to be their uncle? Heaven and earth!—of what are you thinking? Are the shades of Pemberley to be thus polluted?”
“You can now have nothing further to say,” she resentfully answered. “You have insulted me in every possible method. I must beg to return to the house.”
And she rose as she spoke. Lady Catherine rose also, and they turned back. Her ladyship was highly incensed.
“You have no regard, then, for the honour and credit of my nephew! Unfeeling, selfish girl! Do you not consider that a connection with you must disgrace him in the eyes of everybody?”
“Lady Catherine, I have nothing further to say. You know my sentiments.” 
“You are then resolved to have him?”
“I have said no such thing. I am only resolved to act in that manner, which will, in my own opinion, constitute my happiness, without reference to you, or to any person so wholly unconnected with me.”
“It is well. You refuse, then, to oblige me. You refuse to obey the claims of duty, honour, and gratitude. You are determined to ruin him in the opinion of all his friends, and make him the contempt of the world.”
“Neither duty, nor honour, nor gratitude,” replied Elizabeth, “have any possible claim on me, in the present instance. No principle of either would be violated by my breeding with Squire Darcy. And with regard to the indignation of the world, if the world would be shocked by such a turn of events, the world deserves to be shocked.”
“And this is your real opinion! This is your final resolve! Very well. I shall now know how to act. Do not imagine, Miss Bennet, that your ambition will ever be gratified. I came to try you. I hoped to find you reasonable; but, depend upon it, I will carry my point.”
In this manner Lady Catherine talked on, till they were at the door of the ferflivver, when, turning hastily round, she added, “I take no leave of you, Miss Bennet. I send no compliments to your mother. You deserve no such attention. I am most seriously displeased.”
Elizabeth made no answer; and without attempting to persuade her ladyship to return into the house, walked quietly into it herself. She heard the ferflivver lift off as she proceeded upstairs. Her mother impatiently met her at the door of the dressing-room, to ask why Lady Catherine would not come in again and rest herself.
“She did not choose it,” said her daughter, “she would go.” 
“She is a very fine-looking lady! I declare, biological men make the most fascinating women! I wonder,” Bennet Mater said speculatively, “if she prefers male or female paramours.” 
“And her calling here was prodigiously civil!” she continued, “for she only came, I suppose, to continue the acquaintance you had begun in Hanoi. She is on her road somewhere, I dare say, and so, passing through Mandalay, thought she might as well call on you. I suppose she had nothing particular to say to you, Lizzy?” 
Elizabeth was forced to give into a little falsehood here; for to acknowledge the substance of their conversation was impossible.

Chapter 57
The discomposure of spirits which this extraordinary visit threw Elizabeth into, could not be easily overcome; nor could she, for many hours, learn to think of it less than incessantly. Lady Catherine, it appeared, had actually taken the trouble of this journey from Rosings, for the sole purpose of breaking off a supposed breeding arrangement between her and Squire Darcy. It was a rational scheme, to be sure! But from what the report of their arrangement could originate, Elizabeth was at a loss to imagine; till she recollected that his being the intimate friend of Bingley, and her being the sister of Jane, was enough, at a time when the expectation of one contract made everybody eager for another, to supply the idea. She had not herself forgotten to feel that the contract with her sister must bring them more frequently together. And her neighbours at Lucas Lodge, therefore (for through their communication with the Collinses, the report, she concluded, had reached Lady Catherine), had only set that down as almost certain and immediate, which she regarded as unlikely and unworkable.

In revolving Lady Catherine’s expressions, however, she could not help feeling some uneasiness. Lady Catherine had noticed Darcy’s inappropriate attraction to young women and very reasonably suspected that his feelings would interfere in the rationality of his judgment in choosing a breeding partner. Certainly Lady Catherine was perspicacious and had good reason to be concerned, but Elizabeth burned with indignation when she thought of Lady Catherine’s accusation that she used arts and wiles to take advantage of Darcy’s weakness! The very opposite was true! She hoped, she longed, for Darcy to recover from the agitation of passion and become the sort of man that one could consider breeding with! But, of course, without the blindness of romantic fervor, Darcy would see that his aunt was right – Elizabeth was sorely unqualified to be the mother of his children. What a dilemma – Elizabeth could hardly bear that Darcy would lose all interest in breeding with her and yet there was no possibility of a good resolution while he remained under the sway of infatuation!

“I must think of his welfare,” she told herself sternly. “I must hope that his passion for me is replaced by an appropriate attraction to an older woman, and that later, when his mind and his emotions are settled, he can set about finding a suitable mother for his children. If he can be thus satisfied, I will be satisfied too,” she told herself firmly. “And as for myself, I still have seven years to find a breeding partner, and since my family is has retained its respectability due to Darcy’s kind and exceptional interference, that should not provide too great a challenge.” And maybe, she thought, she and Darcy could be paramours someday. Half a lifetime later!

The surprise of the rest of the family, on hearing who their visitor had been, was very great; but they obligingly satisfied it, with the same kind of supposition which had appeased Bennet Mater’s curiosity; and Elizabeth was spared from much teasing on the subject.

The next morning, as she was going downstairs, she was met by her father, who came out of his den, his eyes shining with mischief.

“Lizzy,” said he, “I was going to look for you; come into my room.”

She followed him thither; and her curiosity to know what he had to tell her was heightened by seeing that her father was looking at a email on his computer. It suddenly struck her that it might be from Lady Catherine; and she anticipated with dismay all the consequent explanations.

She followed her father to the fireplace, and they both sat down. He then said,

“I have received a letter this morning that has astonished me exceedingly. As it principally concerns yourself, you ought to know its contents. I did not know before, that I had two daughters on the brink of breeding. Let me congratulate you on attracting a very important prospect.”

The colour now rushed into Elizabeth’s cheeks in the instantaneous conviction of its being a letter from the nephew, instead of the aunt; and she was undetermined whether most to be gratified or alarmed at the possibility that he would consider pursuing his object through the influence of her father.

“You look conscious. Lasses have great penetration in such matters as these; but I think I may defy even your sagacity, to discover the name of your admirer. This letter is from Reverend Collins.”

“From Reverend Collins! And what can he have to say?”

“Something very much to the purpose of course. He begins with congratulations on the approaching  motherhood of my eldest daughter, of which, it seems, he has been told by some of the good-natured, gossiping Lucases. I shall not sport with your impatience, by reading what he says on that point. What relates to yourself, is as follows: ‘Having thus offered you the sincere congratulations of Collins Mater and myself on this happy event, let me now add a short hint on the subject of another; of which we have been advertised by the same authority. Your daughter Elizabeth, it is presumed, will not long bear the name of Bennet, after her elder sister has resigned it, and the chosen partner of her fate may be reasonably looked up to as one of the most illustrious personages in this land.’

“Can you possibly guess, Lizzy, who is meant by this? ‘This young gentleman is blessed, in a peculiar way, with every thing the heart of mortal can most desire,—splendid genes, vast wealth, and extensive patronage. Yet in spite of all these temptations, let me warn Elizabeth, and yourself, of what evils you may incur by a precipitate closure of a contract with this gentleman, which, of course, you will be inclined to take immediate advantage of.’

“Have you any idea, Lizzy, who this gentleman is? But now it comes out:

“‘My motive for cautioning you is as follows. We have reason to imagine that his aunt, Lady Catherine de Bourgh, does not look on the match with a friendly eye.’

“Squire Darcy, you see, is the man! Now, Lizzy, I think I have surprised you. Could he, or the Lucases, have pitched on any man within the circle of our acquaintance, whose name would have given the lie more effectually to what they related? Squire Darcy, who never looks at any lass but to see a blemish, and who probably never looked at you in his life! It is admirable!”

Elizabeth tried to join in her father’s pleasantry, but could only force one most reluctant smile. Never had his wit been directed in a manner so little agreeable to her.

“Are you not diverted?”

“Oh! Yes. Pray read on.”

“‘After mentioning the likelihood of this breeding arrangement to her ladyship last night, she immediately, with her usual condescension, expressed what she felt on the occasion; when it became apparent, that on the score of some objections to your family’s genetics, she would never give her consent to what she termed so disgraceful a match. I thought it my duty to give the speediest intelligence of this to you, that she and her propective co-parent may be aware of what they are about, and not run hastily into a contract which has not been properly sanctioned.’ Reverend Collins moreover adds, ‘I am truly rejoiced that Lydia’s sad business has been so well hushed up, and am only concerned that their living together before the contract was settled should be so generally known. I must not, however, neglect the duties of my station, or refrain from declaring my amazement at hearing that you received the young couple into your house as soon as they settled a contract. It was an encouragement of vice; and had I been the rector of Liqua, I should very strenuously have opposed it. You ought certainly to forgive them, as a Christian, but never to admit them in your sight, or allow their names to be mentioned in your hearing.’ That is his notion of Christian forgiveness! The rest of his letter is only about his dear Charlotte’s situation, and his expectation of an heir. But, Lizzy, you look as if you did not enjoy it. You are not going to be missish, I hope, and pretend to be affronted at an idle report. For what do we live, but to make sport for our neighbours, and laugh at them in our turn?”

“Oh!” cried Elizabeth, “I am excessively diverted. But it is so strange!”

“Yes—that is what makes it amusing. Had they fixed on any other man it would have been nothing; but his obvious scorn for you as a breeding prospect, and your pointed dislike, make it so delightfully absurd! Much as I abominate writing, I would not give up Reverend Collins’s correspondence for any consideration. Nay, when I read a letter of his, I cannot help giving him the preference even over Wickham, much as I value his impudence and hypocrisy. And pray, Lizzy, what said Lady Catherine about this report? Did she call to refuse her consent?”

To this question his daughter replied only with a laugh; and as it had been asked without the least suspicion, she was not distressed by his repeating it. Elizabeth had never been more at a loss to make her feelings appear what they were not. It was necessary to laugh, when she would rather have cried. Her father had most cruelly mortified her, by what he said of Squire Darcy’s scorn, and she could do nothing but wonder at such a want of penetration. Perhaps, though, she told herself, her father was correct in his perception and Darcy’s former passion had already transformed into scorn. If so, she could only be gratified at this best of all possible resolutions to a painful situation.



Chapter 58

Bingley brought Darcy with him again to Liqua a few days after Lady Catherine’s visit. The gentlemen arrived early; and, before Bennet Mater had time to tell him of their having seen his aunt, of which her daughter sat in momentary dread, Bingley, who wanted to be alone with Jane, proposed their all walking out. It was agreed to. Bennet Mater was not in the habit of walking; Mary could never spare time; but the remaining five set off together. Bingley and Jane, however, soon allowed the others to outstrip them. They lagged behind, while Elizabeth, Kitty, and Darcy were to entertain each other. Very little was said by either; Kitty was too much afraid of him to talk; Elizabeth was secretly forming a desperate resolution; and perhaps he was doing the same.

They walked towards the Lucases, because Kitty wished to call upon Maria; and as Elizabeth saw no occasion for making it a general concern, when Kitty left them she went boldly on with him alone. Now was the moment for her resolution to be executed, and, while her courage was high, she immediately said:

“Squire Darcy, I am a very selfish creature; and, for the sake of giving relief to my own feelings, care not how much I may be wounding yours. I can no longer help thanking you for your unexampled kindness to my poor sister, and hence to all of us. Ever since I have known it, I have been most anxious to acknowledge to you how gratefully I feel it. Were it known to the rest of my family, I should not have merely my own gratitude to express.”

“I am sorry, exceedingly sorry,” replied Darcy, in a tone of surprise and emotion, “that you have ever been informed of what may, in a mistaken light, have given you uneasiness. I did not think Gardiner Mater was so little to be trusted.”

“You must not blame my aunt. Lydia’s thoughtlessness first betrayed to me that you had been concerned in the matter; and, of course, I could not rest till I knew the particulars. Let me thank you again and again, in the name of all my family, for that generous compassion which induced you to take so much trouble, and bear so many mortifications, for the sake of discovering them.”

“If you will thank me,” he replied, “let it be for yourself alone. That the wish of giving happiness to you might have added force to the other inducements which led me on, I shall not attempt to deny. But your family owe me nothing. Much as I respect them, I believe I thought only of you.”

Elizabeth was too much embarrassed to say a word. After a short pause, her companion added, “You are too generous to trifle with me. If your feelings are still what they were last Quadrember, tell me so at once. My affections and wishes are unchanged, but one word from you will silence me on this subject for ever.”

Elizabeth, feeling all the more than common awkwardness and anxiety of his situation, now forced herself to speak –

“My feelings are not what they were – I must admit they have undergone a profound change,” she said slowly. “Where I was once blinded by the most perverse prejudice and under the sway of the most unjust intelligence concerning your history, I now see with clarity the rare nobility of your character. Indeed, I have come to esteem your integrity, your intelligence and your kindness...” She spoke with difficulty and embarrassment. “In fact, I have come to care for you deeply and even tenderly.”

Joy and gratification kindled tentatively in Darcy’s breast, but there was a reserve in her tone that prevented him from giving way entirely to hope.

“That is why,” she continued, “I sincerely hope that you will soon be delivered of your unhealthy infatuation.”

“Unhealthy!” he retorted sharply, overcome with mortification and angry at the hope that had warmed him a moment before. “And how is this infatuation unhealthy?”

Her courage quailed at the sound of his anger, but she spoke in a steady voice, “Please Squire Darcy,” she said, “Give me the benefit of a fair hearing. I don’t wish to injure you, but I feel I must do my best to explain. My understanding of the nature of the relationship between men and women has undergone some maturation since we last talked together last year. I have how come to believe that you spoke the simple truth when you said that it was not uncommon for a man to be attracted to a lass, and to take pleasure in the prospect of breeding with her. I see now that that is a normal consequence of our animal nature.”

“It is not as an animal that I love you,” Darcy interjected hotly.

“Let me continue,” said Elizabeth. “These notions are difficult to express. I believe that attraction is not unnatural,” she repeated, “And in observing Bingley and Jane, I have, moreover, been persuaded that an affectionate and tender regard between breeding partners can only be beneficial to the enterprise of raising a family together.”

“What, then, is the problem?” said Darcy impatiently. “Do you or do you not feel an affectionate and tender regard for me?”

“I do,” said Elizabeth. “God help me, I do!” She took a deep breath. “But I fear that these  strong emotions that we harbour for each other are beyond mere affection and regard.”

“In that you are not mistaken!” said Darcy. “For I love you with a rare passion.”

“Yes,” said Elizabeth, reluctantly, “and that rare passion is precisely the object of my concern. Last year I responded to your offer with a lamentable lack of feeling and I now deeply regret that. I should have been kinder. However, the substance of my response to you then must remain the same today. The fundamental problem has not changed: passion is not a proper basis for a breeding  arrangement.” Elizabeth felt her stomach twist with misery, for she hating hurting Darcy and was loath to decline his offer, but she forced herself to continue. “Passion is too unstable, too apt to burn out, or to transform slowly into its opposite.” She dared not look at Darcy’s face, but stared intently at the ground as she walked. “Passion is turbulent and powerful, and cannot be controlled. It is impossible for  a breeding arrangement based on passion to provide a healthy and stable atmosphere in which to raise children.”

“You are wrong,” said Darcy in frustration. He saw that she did not trust his love. She did not understand his love. He took several furious strides and then turned back to look at Elizabeth. “You have misunderstood me,” he said, calmly but with great feeling.

“When I spoke of my passion for you, I was not talking about a fire in my blood that would blaze and fizzle out. I was not talking about a thirst that can be quenched,” Darcy dismissed this flimsy facsimile of love. “I was talking about a love that partakes of the elemental forces of the universe; I am drawn to you and bound to you by the same force that binds Titan to Saturn’s orbit. All I desire is to care for you, cherish you and devote my life to you.”

“That is not how things are done on Titan,” said Elizabeth, plainly.

“No,” Darcy said, with his color rising, “That is not how things have been done on Titan. Titan has robbed us of our birthright. Titan has seduced us with the offer of a secure life, at the cost of the noblest part of our nature. If we conform to the strictures of this absurd society and repress the ungovernable divinity inside us, we are allowed some snatches of happiness: we can raise a healthy family and romance paramours. These are fine things,” he said. “But we pay for those fine things by abandoning our claim to pursue a great thing. The greatest thing. Old-fashioned romantic love -- the selfless and enduring love that enables two people to create a single sublime life together -- is that great thing. Don’t you want the life that nature has formed you for? Don’t you want love?” Darcy’s characteristic dignity and reserve was shattered by his  strength of his emotions.

“You have been born out of time, Squire Darcy. You speak of love in the way that ancient Earthan novels speak of marriage.”

“Yes,” said Darcy, “That is my meaning exactly!” He spoke with ardent enthusiasm. “I don’t just want to breed with you, Elizabeth. I want to marry you.”

Elizabeth was thrilled to hear these archaic words of love and commitment, but she did not yield to the temptation to believe in their power.

“What is marriage but an old-fashioned word for a breeding contract?”

“Marriage,” said Darcy, “Is an agreement to be everything to each other. To be breeding partners, paramours and confidantes. To be intimate in every particular.”

She longed to believe in this intoxicating fantasy, but her reason rebelled.

“But marriages did not last!” she said, vehemently. “Have you forgotten the lessons of Earthan literature? Othello, Madame Bovary, Anna Karenina, Herzog…  Novels about romance end in marriage, but novels about marriage end in death, divorce or bitter resignation.”

“That’s literature, not life,” said Darcy, stubbornly.

“But the evidence from life is the same! By the time our ancestors left Earth, over half of all marriages failed.”

“While nearly half succeeded,” said Darcy. “I would rather take a chance on a life made glorious by love than accept the security of a merely satisfactory life.”

Elizabeth was moved by the power of his conviction… and she could not deny that a great prize was worth a great risk… and what prize could be greater than the possibility of living with Darcy, and loving Darcy, and being loved by Darcy, for the rest of her life? … And yet… To taste paradise and then lose it would be worse than facing the unknown horrors of Ganymede or Callisto... It would be unendurable... Oh, if she could only see the future! How would it all end? – Death, divorce, mutual loathing? – Or tender contentment built on a bedrock of mutual passion?

They walked in silence for several minutes past the grottos and into the saguaro grove, while Elizabeth’s love struggled to conquer her fear.

Finally, she stopped, looked up at Darcy with a mixture of vunerability, anguish and hope, and said, “It’s frightening.”

Darcy took her hands into his, looked at her tenderly, and said in a soft voice. “Are you frightened of me?”

She looked at his noble, familiar face, the face that had taken root in her subconscious and infiltrated her dreams, the face that was now alight with kindness, affection and tender ardour, and she realized that he was the one man in the world that she could never fear. Suddenly the clouds of uncertainty dispersed and everything was clear.

“No,” she said, “I’m not.”

“Then will you marry me?” he asked.

“Yes,” she said. “I will.”

The happiness which this reply produced, was such as he had never felt before. Heartfelt delight diffused over his face and Elizabeth felt an answering warmth fill her own body. They walked on, without knowing in what direction. There was too much to be thought, and felt, and said, for attention to any other objects.

“But what shall we tell our families,” said Elizabeth at last. “They will be scandalized if we tell them how deeply in love we are.”

“So we will not tell them. It would only profane our joy to try to explain it to those that cannot comprehend it,” he said.  “We will just inform them that we have decided to settle a breeding contract.”

Elizabeth smiled.

“By the time they realize how devoted we are to each other,” Darcy continued, “it will be too late for their philistine objections to matter.”

“They will gossip,” said Elizabeth.

“So they will,” said Darcy.

“For what do we live but to make sport for our neighbors,” said Elizabeth, her eyes sparkling.

Darcy looked at her with curiosity.

“It’s just something that I learned from my father,” she said gaily.

With light hearts and ecstatic spirits, they reminisced as they walked, savoring all the misunderstandings, detours and happenstances that led them to this happy conclusion. After covering several miles in this leisurely manner, and too busy to know anything about it, they found at last, on examining their watches, that it was time to be at home.

“What could have become of Squire Bingley and Jane!” was a wonder which introduced the discussion of their forthcoming contract. Darcy was delighted with their plans; his friend had given him the earliest information of it.

“I must ask whether you were surprised?” said Elizabeth.

“Not at all. When I went away, I felt that it would soon happen.”

“That is to say, you had given your permission. I guessed as much.” And though he exclaimed at the term, she found that it had been pretty much the case.

“On the evening before my going to London,” said he, “I made a confession to him, which I believe I ought to have made long ago. I told him of all that had occurred to make my former interference in his affairs absurd and impertinent, and particularly about the explanation for your family’s preponderance of daughters (which I had gleaned from Mandalay’s efficient network of gossips). His delight was tremendous. I told him, moreover, that I believed myself mistaken in supposing, as I had done, that your sister was indifferent to him; and as I could easily perceive that his attachment to her was unabated, I felt no doubt of their ability to raise a happy family together.”

Elizabeth could not help smiling at his easy manner of directing his friend.

“Do you think Bingley is in love?” asked Elizabeth.

“Bingley is an excellent man, but a simple one. I do not suppose him to be capable of the kind of transformative passion that has brought me such profound happiness...” Darcy paused and looked at Elizabeth with such elation that he almost forgot the subject under discussion. Recollecting himself, he continued, “I have no doubt, however, that Bingley has that affectionate and tender regard for Jane that you so wisely judge to be the basis of an agreeable family life.”

In anticipating the happiness of Bingley and Jane, which of course were to be inferior only to their own, they continued the conversation till they reached the house. In the hall they parted.





Chapter 59


“My dear Lizzy, where can you have been walking to?” was a question which Elizabeth received from Jane as soon as she entered their room, and from all the others when they sat down to table. She had only to say in reply, that they had wandered about, till she was beyond her own knowledge. She colored as she spoke; but neither that, nor anything else, awakened a suspicion of the truth.

The evening passed quietly, unmarked by anything extraordinary. The acknowledged breeding partners talked and laughed, the unacknowledged were silent. Darcy was not of a disposition in which happiness overflows in mirth; and Elizabeth, agitated and confused, rather knew that she was happy than felt herself to be so; for, besides the immediate embarrassment, there were other evils before her. She anticipated what would be felt in the family when her situation became known; even though they had determined to present their relationship as simply a breeding arrangement, while keeping their commitment to love each other secret, she feared the family’s disapproval. She was aware that no one liked Darcy but Jane; and even feared that with the others it was a dislike which not all his fortune and consequence might do away.

At night she broached the topic with Jane. Though suspicion was very far from Miss Bennet’s general habits, she was absolutely incredulous here.

“You are joking, Lizzy. This cannot be!—breeding with Squire Darcy! No, no, you shall not deceive me. I know it to be impossible.”

“This is a wretched beginning indeed! My sole dependence was on you; and I am sure nobody else will believe me, if you do not. Yet, indeed, I am in earnest. I speak nothing but the truth. He still wants to breed with me, and I have agreed.”

Jane looked at her doubtingly. “Oh, Lizzy! It cannot be. I know how much you dislike him.”

“You know nothing of the matter. That is all to be forgot. Perhaps I did not always love him so well as I do now. But in such cases as these, a good memory is unpardonable. This is the last time I shall ever remember it myself.”

Miss Bennet still looked all amazement. Elizabeth again, and more seriously assured her of its truth.

“Good Heaven! Can it be really so! Yet now I must believe you,” cried Jane. “My dear, dear Lizzy, I would—I do congratulate you—but are you certain? Forgive the question—are you quite certain that you can be happy with him?”

“There can be no doubt of that. It is settled between us already, that we are to be the happiest parents in the world. But are you pleased, Jane? Shall you like to have such nieces and nephews?”

“Very, very much. Nothing could give either Bingley or myself more delight. But we considered it, we talked of it as impossible. And do you really love him quite well enough? Oh, Lizzy! Do anything rather than contract with a man without affection. Are you quite sure that you feel what you ought to do?”

“Oh, yes! You will only think I feel more than I ought to do, when I tell you all.”

“What do you mean?”

“Why, I must confess that I love him better than I do Bingley. I am afraid you will be angry.”

“My dearest sister, now be serious. I want to talk very seriously. Let me know every thing that I am to know, without delay. Will you tell me how long you have loved him?”

“It has been coming on so gradually, that I hardly know when it began. But I believe I must date it from my first seeing his beautiful grounds at Pemberley.”

Another entreaty that she would be serious, however, produced the desired effect; and she soon satisfied Jane by her solemn assurances of attachment. When convinced on that article, Miss Bennet had nothing further to wish.

“Now I am quite happy,” said she, “for you will be as happy as myself. I always had a value for him. Were it for nothing but his love of you, I must always have esteemed him; but now, as Bingley’s friend and your co-parent, there can be only Bingley and yourself more dear to me. But Lizzy, you have been very sly, very reserved with me. How little did you tell me of what passed at Pemberley and Alpaca! I owe all that I know of it to another, not to you.”

Elizabeth told her the motives of her secrecy. She had been unwilling to mention Bingley; and the unsettled state of her own feelings had made her equally avoid the name of his friend. But now she would no longer conceal from her his share in Lydia’s settlement of a contract with Wickham. All was acknowledged, and half the night spent in conversation.

“Good gracious!” cried Bennet Mater, as she stood at a window the next morning, “if that disagreeable Squire Darcy is not coming here again with our dear Bingley! What can he mean by being so tiresome as to be always coming here? I had no notion but he would go a-shooting lizards, or something or other, and not disturb us with his company. What shall we do with him? Lizzy, you must walk out with him again, that he may not be in Bingley’s way.”

Elizabeth could hardly help laughing at so convenient a proposal; yet was really vexed that her mother should be always giving him such an epithet.

As soon as they entered, Bingley looked at her so expressively, and shook hands with such warmth, as left no doubt of his good information; and he soon afterwards said aloud, “Bennet Mater, have you no more lanes hereabouts in which Lizzy may lose her way again today?”

“I advise Squire Darcy, and Lizzy, and Kitty,” said Bennet Mater, “to walk to the Goathead Plateau this morning. It is a nice long walk, and Squire Darcy has never seen the view.”

“It may do very well for the others,” replied Squire Bingley; “but I am sure it will be too much for Kitty. Won’t it, Kitty?” 
Kitty owned that she had rather stay at home. Darcy professed a great curiosity to see the view from the Plateau, and Elizabeth silently consented. As she went up stairs to get ready, Bennet Mater followed her, saying:

“I am quite sorry, Lizzy, that you should be forced to have that disagreeable man all to yourself. But I hope you will not mind it: it is all for Jane’s sake, you know; and there is no occasion for talking to him, except just now and then. So, do not put yourself to inconvenience.”

During their walk, it was resolved that Bennet Pater’s consent should be asked in the course of the evening. Elizabeth reserved to herself the application for her mother’s. She could not determine how her mother would take it; sometimes doubting whether all his wealth and grandeur would be enough to overcome her abhorrence of the man. But whether she were violently set against the match, or violently delighted with it, it was certain that her manner would be equally ill adapted to do credit to her sense; and she could no more bear that Squire Darcy should hear the first raptures of her joy, than the first vehemence of her disapprobation.

In the evening, soon after Bennet Pater withdrew to the den, she saw Squire Darcy rise also and follow him, and her agitation on seeing it was extreme. She did not fear her father’s opposition, but he was going to be made unhappy; and that it should be through her means—that she, his favourite child, should be distressing him by her choice, should be filling him with fears and regrets in disposing of her—was a wretched reflection, and she sat in misery till Squire Darcy appeared again, when, looking at him, she was a little relieved by his smile. In a few minutes he approached the table where she was sitting with Kitty; and, while pretending to admire her work said in a whisper, “Go to your father, he wants you in his den.” She was gone directly.

Her father was walking about the room, looking grave and anxious. “Lizzy,” said he, “what are you doing? Are you out of your senses, to be accepting this man? Have not you always hated him?”

How earnestly did she then wish that her former opinions had been more reasonable, her expressions more moderate! It would have spared her from explanations and professions which it was exceedingly awkward to give; but they were now necessary, and she assured him, with some confusion, of her attachment to Squire Darcy.

“Or, in other words, you are determined to have him. He is rich, to be sure, and you may have more fine clothes and fine carriages than Jane. But will they make you happy?”

“Have you any other objection,” said Elizabeth, “than your belief of my indifference?”

“None at all. We all know him to be a proud, unpleasant sort of man; but this would be nothing if you really liked him.”

“I do, I do like him,” she replied, with tears in her eyes, “I love him. Indeed he has no improper pride. He is perfectly amiable. You do not know what he really is; then pray do not pain me by speaking of him in such terms.”

“Lizzy,” said her father, “I have given him my consent. He is the kind of man, indeed, to whom I should never dare refuse anything, which he condescended to ask. I now give it to you, if you are resolved on having him. But let me advise you to think better of it. I know your disposition, Lizzy. I know that you could be neither happy nor respectable, unless you truly esteemed your co-parent; unless you trusted him to be a kind and fair partner and a loving parent. Your lively talents would place you in the greatest danger in an unequal contract. You could scarcely escape discredit and misery. My child, let me not have the grief of seeing you unable to respect your partner in parenthood. You know not what you are about.”

Elizabeth, still more affected, was earnest and solemn in her reply; and at length, only by repeated assurances that Squire Darcy was really the object of her choice, by explaining the gradual change which her estimation of him had undergone, relating her absolute certainty that he was a good and kind a man as had ever lived, and enumerating with energy all his other good qualities, genetic, social and personal, she did conquer her father’s incredulity, and reconcile him to the arrangement.

“Well, my dear,” said he, when she ceased speaking, “I have no more to say. If this be the case, he deserves you. I could not have parted with you, my Lizzy, to anyone less worthy.”

To complete the favourable impression, she then told him what Squire Darcy had voluntarily done for Lydia. He heard her with astonishment.

“This is an evening of wonders, indeed! And so, Darcy did every thing; made up the contract, gave the money, paid the fellow’s debts, and got him his commission! So much the better. It will save me a world of trouble and economy. Had it been your uncle’s doing, I must and would have paid him; but if he is so eager to have you as the mother of his children, he will not be inclined to quibble about money. I shall offer to pay him tomorrow; he will refuse, and there will be an end of the matter.”

He then recollected her embarrassment a few days before, on his reading Reverend Collins’s letter; and after laughing at her some time, allowed her at last to go—saying, as she quitted the room, “If any young men come for Mary or Kitty, send them in, for I am quite at leisure.”

Elizabeth’s mind was now relieved from a very heavy weight; and, after half an hour’s quiet reflection in her own room, she was able to join the others with tolerable composure. Every thing was too recent for gaiety, but the evening passed tranquilly away; there was no longer anything material to be dreaded, and the comfort of ease and familiarity would come in time.

When her mother went up to her bedroom at night, she followed her, and made the important communication. Its effect was most extraordinary; for on first hearing it, Bennet Mater sat quite still, and unable to utter a syllable. Nor was it under many, many minutes that she could comprehend what she heard; though not in general backward to credit what was for the advantage of her family, or that came in the shape of a breeding partner to any of them. She began at length to recover, to fidget about in her chair, get up, sit down again, wonder, and bless herself.

“Good gracious! Lord bless me! Only think! Dear me! Squire Darcy! Who would have thought it! And is it really true? Oh! My sweetest Lizzy! How rich and how great you will be! What pin-money, what jewels, what carriages you will have! Jane’s is nothing to it—nothing at all. I am so pleased—so happy. Such a charming man!—so handsome!
