So tall!such impeccable genes!—Oh, my dear Lizzy! Pray apologise for my having disliked him so much before. I hope he will overlook it. Dear, dear Lizzy. A house in town! Every thing that is charming! Three daughters bred! With his influence, Mary and Kitty will be assured of staying on Titan as well! And ten thousand a year! Oh, Lord! What will become of me. I shall go distracted.”

This was enough to prove that her approbation need not be doubted: and Elizabeth, rejoicing that such an effusion was heard only by herself, soon went away. But before she had been three minutes in her own room, her mother followed her.

“My dearest child,” she cried, “I can think of nothing else! Ten thousand a year, and very likely more! A hereditary governor and perhaps a president someday? And such an inheritance for your sons! But my dearest love, tell me what dish Squire Darcy is particularly fond of, that I may have it tomorrow.”

This was a sad omen of what her mother’s behavior to the gentleman himself might be; and Elizabeth found that, though in the certain possession of his ardent love, and unafraid at last to love him in return, there was still something to be wished for. But the morrow passed off much better than she expected; for Bennet Mater luckily stood in such awe of the future father of her grandchildren that she ventured not to speak to him, unless it was in her power to offer him any attention, or mark her deference for his opinion.

Elizabeth had the satisfaction of seeing her father taking pains to get acquainted with him; and Bennet Pater soon assured her that he was rising every hour in his esteem. “If your offspring resemble their parents, they will be intelligent, sensible and good-looking. And if they do not, at least they will be rich!”



Chapter 60


Elizabeth’s spirits soon rising to playfulness again, she wanted Squire Darcy to account for his having ever fallen in love with her. “How could you begin?” said she. “I can comprehend your going on obstinately, when you had once made a beginning; but what could set you off in the first place?”
“I cannot fix on the hour, or the spot, or the look, or the words, which laid the foundation. It is too long ago. I was in the middle before I knew that I had begun.”
“My beauty you had early withstood, and as for my manners—my behavior to you was at least always bordering on the uncivil, and I never spoke to you without rather wishing to give you pain than not. Now be sincere; did you admire me for my impertinence?”
“For the liveliness of your mind, I did.”
“You may as well call it impertinence at once. It was very little less. The fact is, that you were sick of civility, of deference, of officious attention. You were disgusted with the lasses who were always speaking, and looking, and thinking for your approbation alone. I roused, and interested you, because I was so unlike them. Had you not been really amiable, you would have hated me for it; but in spite of the pains you took to disguise yourself, your feelings were always noble and just; and in your heart, you thoroughly despised the lasses who so assiduously courted you. There—I have saved you the trouble of accounting for it; and really, all things considered, I begin to think it perfectly reasonable. To be sure, you knew no actual good of me—but nobody thinks of that when they fall in love.”
“You underestimate my discernment when you think that I could not detect the good in you. I judged quite early on that in both nubility of body and quality of mind you were exceptionally well-designed for motherhood. I saw, moreover, that you exhibited a fine array of traits beneficial to a parent– kindness, honesty, patience, courage, and, above all, a natural resiliance of spirit that springs from a high valuation of your own worth. I was impressed that my outspoken scorn for you seemed to amuse rather than distress you.  I knew that such a mother would be able to handle with grace the frustrations and occasional heartaches inevitably attached to raising children.”
“So you judged me to be well-qualified for breeding, in defiance of popular opinion?” 
“Let us just say that I saw your potential to be a good mother for another man’s children. However, as you, lamentably, know all too well, I was not persuaded that you were altogether the wisest choice of breeding partner for me.” 
“Indeed, I do remember that detail.”
“It was only after I had abandoned my rational assessment of your qualifications and listened to my ungovernable heart that I realized that you were the only one I wanted to breed with,” continued Darcy. “For that, I can give no explanation. The logic of the heart is impenetrable.”
“And so,” said Elizabeth, smiling, “Love conquers prudence, and we are to be paramours as well as breeding partners.”
“Yes.”
“But, Squire Darcy,” said Elizabeth doubtfully, her expression changing from playful to serious, “have you considered that mothers are rather too fully occupied by the demands of offspring to be available for romance?”
“Wasn’t it ever thus in the history of our species?” responded Darcy. “Love finds its way through the tumult of daily life.”
“I think that you have read too many romances and not enough history,” said Elizabeth teasingly. “It is my impression that on Earth, babies inevitably brought a great deal of strain to the marital relationship, often leaving no time or energy for romance.” She frowned. “And that was frequently true even when circumstances were far more favorable for mothers than here on Titan. In the past few centuries on Earth, childrearing duties were shared equally between the mother and father, household upkeep was considerably easier than on this primitive Colony, and parents were able to avail themselves of outside childcare and schools to mind the children during the day.”
“Titan does indeed present special difficulties,” said Darcy, gravely. “When considered in this light, it is unsurprising that romance is considered the domain of older women here. But,” he continued, “our abiding love for each other will help us to overcome whatever difficulties we meet.”
Elizabeth smiled wistfully and said, “Marriages usually began with love. But love often failed as practical problems became overwhelming.”
“That would not happen with us,” said Darcy stubbornly. “I would not allow it!” He spoke with the imperious command of Canute ordering the waves to retreat. He paused, however, to consider the logistical problem. “You have perhaps forgotten, Miss Bennet, that I am very well off,” he continued. “Pemberley is overflowing with servants, who will tend to your every need.”
“That’s true,” said Elizabeth, with half a smile. “But robot servants cannot raise children.” 
“No,” said Darcy, judiciously, “but they can handle the animal management, tend the crops, cook, clean the house, and relieve you myriads of other tiresome duties that many mothers are burdened with,” Darcy continued. “And I will lend my own energy and talents to the nurture and education of our offspring, when unencumbered by professional duties.” 
“Which will be seldom enough, I suppose,” said Elizabeth skeptically.
Darcy laughed, and considered further. “My grandmother will also help,” he proposed. “She is a very affectionate woman and doted over me as a child. It is perhaps our good fortune that she does not have a very active love-life, and will be sure to have time to look after the children when we have planned a romantic evening or an excursion.”
“Perhaps it can work,” said Elizabeth. The thought of romantic excursions with Darcy was sufficient to fill her heart with an airy optimism and her eyes shone in anticipation of such delights.
“And don’t forget that we will share a bed every night,” added Darcy, with a long glance at Elizabeth that comprehended both solemnity and joy.
Elizabeth’s heart leapt and her face flushed.
“We will make it work,” she said, her native confidence returning in full force. “We will be pioneers in love.” She laughed gleefully. “Where the illustrious Fitzwilliam Darcy goes, all of Titan society will want to follow. Soon it will be all the rage to be in love with your breeding partner!”
“What a scandal!” said Darcy.
“Will Lady Catherine ever forgive us, do you think?” said Elizabeth.
“I hope not!” said Darcy.


Chapter 61

Happy for all her maternal feelings was the day on which Bennet Mater saw her two most deserving daughters formalize contracts that ensured their futures on Titan. With what delighted pride she afterwards visited Bingley Mater, and talked of Darcy Mater, may be guessed. I wish I could say, for the sake of her family, that the accomplishment of her earnest desire in the establishment of so many of her children produced so happy an effect as to make her a sensible, amiable, well-informed woman for the rest of her life; but, although she still was occasionally nervous and invariably silly, she was unquestionably a most devoted grandmother.

Bennet Pater missed his second daughter exceedingly; his affection for her drew him oftener from home than anything else could do. He delighted in going to Pemberley, especially when he was least expected.

Bingley and Jane remained at Netherfield only a tenmonth. So near a vicinity to her mother and Mandalay relations was not desirable even to his easy temper, or her affectionate heart. The darling wish of his sisters was then gratified; he purchased a mineshare in a nearby town in New Arabia, and Jane and Elizabeth, in addition to every other source of happiness, were within thirty miles of each other. Bingley and Jane were happy and affectionate co-parents who remained warmly attached to each other for the rest of their lives, even though, as irredeemably conventional people, they both enjoyed paramours from time to time.

Mary’s character blossomed with the security of knowing that Darcy’s influence would save her from exile even if she were not able to find a breeding partner. She began to read for the pleasure of developing her ideas rather than for the sake of displaying her native intelligence to prospective breeding partners, and she nurtured her latent capacity for free thinking. Within a few years, she became a leading activist for change on Titan. Although her proposal to raise women to full equality in the governance of the Colony was too far ahead of its time to have an immediate effect, her granddaughters benefited from her pioneering agitation. More successful in the short term was her campaign to allow men to have more than one breeding partner, which resulted in the rescue of thousands of lasses from exile. Later in life, she established a school which allowed young mothers to have some respite from their offspring, freeing up some hours of every day for them to devote to social activism, self improvement or the enjoyment of paramours. Mary’s particular duty at the school was to teach the younger generation of Titan to value liberty, democracy and the inherent dignity of every human being.

Kitty, to her very material advantage, spent the chief of her time with Mary. Although not as earnest as Mary by nature, she imbibed the values of her older sister as she gradually discarded those of Lydia, and learned to appreciate the joys of an intellectual life. She eventually became the second breeding partner to one of the teachers at Mary’s school, and raised five progressive children, one of whom became the first woman on Titan’s board of governors.

As for Wickham and Lydia, their characters suffered no revolution. He bore with philosophy the operation which deprived him of his special interest in little girls and became a tolerably affectionate father, though no operation could deprive him of his innate selfishness, unreliability and profligacy. Lydia found motherhood wearisome, but bore five children in ten years, with the happy result that she was free to pursue paramours before she had reached the age of forty. Beautiful, vivacious and living in the frontier Colony of Juno, where men vastly outnumbered women, Lydia found herself much sought after as a paramour, to her profound delight.

Miss Bingley was very deeply mortified by Darcy’s choice of breeding partner; but as she thought it advisable to retain the right of visiting at Pemberley, she dropped all her resentment; was fonder than ever of Georgiana, almost as attentive to Darcy as heretofore, and paid off every arrear of civility to Elizabeth.
 
Lady Catherine was extremely indignant on the conclusion of the co-parental contract of her nephew; and as she gave way to all the genuine frankness of her character in her reply to the letter which announced its arrangement, she sent him language so very abusive, especially of Elizabeth, that for some time all intercourse was at an end. But at length, by Elizabeth’s persuasion, he was prevailed on to overlook the offence, and seek a reconciliation; and, after a little further resistance on the part of his aunt, her resentment gave way, either to her affection for him, or to her curiosity to see her nephews and nieces; and she condescended to wait on them at Pemberley, in spite of that pollution which its woods had received, not merely from the presence of such a co-parent, but also from the visits of her genetically and socially deficient uncle and aunt.

With the Gardiners, they were always on the most intimate terms. Darcy, as well as Elizabeth, really loved them; and they were both ever sensible of the warmest gratitude towards the persons who, by bringing her into New Arabia, had been the means of uniting them.

Pemberley was now Georgiana’s home; and the attachment of the sisters was exactly what Darcy had hoped to see. They were able to love each other even as well as they intended. Georgiana had the highest opinion in the world of Elizabeth; though at first she often listened with an astonishment bordering on alarm at her lively, sportive manner of talking to her brother. He, who had always inspired in herself a respect which almost overcame her affection, she now saw the object of open pleasantry. Her mind received knowledge which had never before fallen in her way. Through Elizabeth and Darcy’s example, she came to learn that a man may love his breeding partner as ardently as one loves a paramour, and that even on Titan, co-parents can sometimes achieve an old-fashioned lifelong devotion to each other.
